Buyu — Sales and Trade

Explicit Permission: General vs Specific
Distinguishing general vs specific permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general and specific permission (idhn) and state the Hanafi ruling on whether a slave becomes ma'dhun.

Core Idea
General permission in trade makes the slave a full trader; a single command does not.

In Hanafi fiqh, the nature of permission granted to a slave has significant legal consequences. This lesson examines the distinction between general permission (idhn 'amm) which authorizes the slave to engage in all trade, and specific permission (idhn khass) which is merely a command for a specific act and does not confer the status of ma'dhun. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of slave agency in classical Hanafi commercial law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of permission
Look at the wording: if it is broad like 'atijir' or 'idhn fi al-tijara', it is general; if it is a command for a single item, it is specific.
General permission often uses terms like 'trade' or 'commerce' without limitation.
2
Determine the legal effect
General permission makes the slave ma'dhun with full trading capacity; specific permission is istikhdam (use as servant).
The status of ma'dhun allows the slave to buy, sell, and enter contracts on behalf of the master.
3
Check for implicit permission
Even without explicit words, actions like giving a donkey for hire indicate permission if they imply repeated transactions.
Commands for multiple contracts (e.g., 'earn and pay') indicate trade; single commands do not.
4
Note the knowledge requirement
The slave must be aware of the permission for transactions to be valid until he learns of it.
If the slave transacts before knowing, the transactions are invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أذن له عاماً لا بشراء شيء بعينه يبيع ويشتري قال صاحب العناية : ونوقض بالإذن في النكاح رعاية الحجر وإسقاط الحق، وإذا أذن له أن يتزوج فلانة ليس له أن يتزوج غيرها وأجيب أن النكاح تصرف مملوك للمولى لأنه لا يجوز إلا بولي والرق أخرج العبد من أهل الولاية فلأن يجيزه المولى على النكاح مخصص بخلاف البيع والإذن على نوعين : عام وخاص. فالعام أن يقول لعبده أذنت لك في التجارة أو قال اتجر، ولو قال أدي ألفاً وأنت حر يصير مأذوناً في التجارة، وكذا لو قال اكتسب وأد ذلك . وقوله أد ألفاً وأنت بمنزلة ما إذا قال إن أديت ألفاً فأنت حر لأن جواب الأمر بالواو كالفاء بخلاف ما إذا قال أد ألفاً أنت حر ولو أذن لعبده ولم يعلم العبد بالإذن ولا من الناس فتصرف ثم علم لم يجز لعدم علمه ولو قال لقوم بايعوه فبايعوه ولم يعلم العبد بذلك فهو مأذون وذكر في الزيادات : ولو قال لرجل عبدك هذا بع من ابني الصغير فباعه منه وقبل الابن إن علم بأمر الأب جاز، وإن لم يعلم لم يجز قبل الإذن على الروايتين . والفرق بين الروايتين إن إذن الصبي توكيل وليس بإذن في التجارة لأنه فوض إليه عقد واحد وبتفويض عقد واحد لا يثبت الأذن، وفي مسألة المأذون إذن لا توكيل لأنه فوض إليه عقوداً متكررة فيجوز أن يثبت الإذن ضمناً للأمر بالمبايعة في عقود متكررة بدون علمه وإن لم يثبت مقصوداً بخلاف العقد الواحد، ولو لم يبايعه أحد منهم وبايعه من لم يأمره المولى لم يصر مأذوناً لأن الإذن إنما يثبت في ضمن أمره بالمبايعة. ولو دفع له حماراً ليكريه ويبيع عليه صار مأذوناً والإذن يصح تعليقه بلا شرط وإضافته إلى الزمان كالطلاق والحجر والعزل لا يصح تعليقهما ولا إضافتهما كالنكاح ، وأما الإذن الخاص فلا يكون به مأذوناً كما لو أمره بشراء ثوب للكسوة أو لحم للأكل لأن هذا استخدام فلا بد من فاصل بين الاستخدام والتجارة أن الأمر بعقد مرة بعد مرة استخدام والأمر بعقود متعدده يعد تجارة لأنه يدل على أنه للربح. ولما بين المؤلف الإذن الصريح شرع في الإذن دلالة ا.هـ.
If he permits him generally, not for the purchase of a specific thing, he sells and buys. The author of al-Inayah said: It is objected by the permission in marriage: the regard for restriction and dropping of right; if he permits him to marry so-and-so, he may not marry another. And it was answered that marriage is a transaction owned by the master because it is not valid except with a guardian, and slavery removed the slave from being qualified for guardianship; thus, the master's permission for marriage is specific, unlike sale. And permission is of two types: general and specific. The general is that he says to his slave: 'I have permitted you in trade' or he says 'Trade'. And if he says: 'Pay a thousand and you are free,' he becomes authorized in trade. Similarly if he says: 'Earn and pay that.' And his statement: 'Pay a thousand and you are free' is like if he said: 'If you pay a thousand, you are free' because the response to the command with waw is like fa', unlike if he said: 'Pay a thousand, you are free.' And if he permits his slave and the slave does not know of the permission, nor do the people, then he transacts and then he knows, it is not valid due to his lack of knowledge. And if he says to a group: 'Sell to him,' and they sell to him, and the slave does not know of that, he is authorized. And it is mentioned in al-Ziyadat: If he says to a man: 'This slave of yours, sell from my young son,' and he sells from him and the son accepts, if he knows of the father's order, it is valid; if he does not know, it is not valid before permission, according to two narrations. The difference between the two narrations is that the permission of the minor is a delegation and is not permission in trade because he delegated a single contract; with delegation of a single contract, permission is not established. And in the matter of the authorized slave, it is permission, not delegation, because he delegated repeated contracts, so it is possible that permission is established implicitly for the command of selling in repeated contracts without his knowledge, even if it is not established explicitly, unlike a single contract. And if none of them sold to him and someone whom the master did not order sold to him, he does not become authorized because permission is only established within the command of selling. And if he gave him a donkey to hire out and sell on, he becomes authorized. And permission is valid suspended without condition and added to time, like divorce; restriction and exclusion are not valid suspended nor added, like marriage. As for specific permission, one is not authorized by it, as if he orders him to buy a garment for clothing or meat for eating, because this is using him as a servant. There must be a distinction between using as a servant and trade: the command for a contract once or repeatedly is using as a servant, and the command for multiple contracts counts as trade because it indicates profit. When the author finished explicit permission, he began implicit permission.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the types of explicit permission given to a slave. The author distinguishes between general permission ('idhn 'amm) which makes the slave a fully authorized trader (ma'dhun), and specific permission ('idhn khass) which does not. General permission is phrased in broad terms like 'I permit you in trade' or simply 'Trade,' and it covers all commercial activities. Specific permission is a command to perform a single transaction, such as buying a specific item for personal use; this treats the slave as a servant (istikdam), not a merchant. The passage also addresses edge cases: if the master says 'Pay a thousand and you are free,' this implies permission in trade because earning requires trade. If the slave is unaware of the permission, his transactions are invalid until he learns. A notable discussion concerns the difference between a minor's permission (which is considered tawkil) and a slave's permission (which is idhn). The relied-upon Hanafi position is that general permission with multiple transactions confers the status of ma'dhun, and the permission can be suspended temporally (like 'for a month'), as it is an isqat, unlike marriage. Specific commands for single acts do not confer this status.

Key Terms
إذن عام (idhn 'amm): General permission expressed in broad terms like 'I permit you in trade' or 'trade', which makes the slave ma'dhun in all commercial transactions.
إذن خاص (idhn khass): Specific permission or command for a single transaction, which does not confer the status of ma'dhun and is considered istikhdam (using as a servant).
استخدام (istikdam): Using the slave for personal service, as opposed to authorizing him in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following makes a slave ma'dhun?
A) 'Buy this loaf'
B) 'Trade in the market'
C) 'Pay a thousand and you are free'
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
General permission authorizes the slave fully for trade; specific commands do not.

Implied Permission Through Master's Silence
Explains the effect of the master's silence on slave's trade

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's silence when seeing his slave trade and distinguish between its effect on the observed transaction and future transactions.

Core Idea
Silence implies permission for future trades, not necessarily for the observed one.

In Hanafi fiqh, permission to trade can be established by implication (dilalah) rather than explicit words. The master's silence upon seeing his slave engage in buying and selling is a key example. This lesson explores the ruling in al-Hidayah, a distinction by Qadi Khan, and the resolution of an apparent contradiction, clarifying how silence operates as a source of authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Start by stating the rule from al-Hidayah: if a master sees his slave selling and buying and remains silent, that silence establishes permission for the slave to trade. This applies to any property sold, with or without prior permission.
The silence must be without any prior indication to negate permission.
2
Understand Qadi Khan's distinction
Qadi Khan states that if the master sees the slave selling a specific item owned by the master and remains silent, that does not become permission for that specific transaction. However, it does become permission for future trades.
This distinction prevents retroactive validation of a transaction made before authorization.
3
Reconcile the views
The commentator shows that al-Hidayah and Qadi Khan are not contradictory. Al-Hidayah's general statement about permission refers to future transactions, while Qadi Khan's exception refers only to the observed transaction.
The analogy of the mortgagee (seeing mortgagor sell and silent) supports this: the mortgage is not void for that sale.
4
Note the alternative view
Al-Tahawi narrated from the Hanafi school that silence is consent and voids the mortgage (i.e., validates the transaction). But the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the one in al-Hidayah, as explained.
This shows there is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, but the majority view is adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويثبت بالسكوت بأن رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري يثبت الإذن للعبد بسكوت المولى عند ما يراه يبيع ويشتري ولم يتقدم قرينة بنفيه . ولا فرق بين ذلك أن يبيع عيناً مملوكاً للمولى أو لغيره بإذنه أو بغير إذنه بيعاً صحيحاً أو فاسداً ؛ كذا في الهداية وغيرها وقال قاضيخان في فتاواه : إن رآه عيناً من أعيان المالك فسكت لم يكن إذناً، وكذا المرتهن إذا رأى الراهن يبيع فسكت لا يبطل الرهن. وروى الطحاوي عن أصحابنا أنه رضا ويبطل الرهن ؛ كذا نقله الإمام الزيلعي. وظاهر كلامه أنه فهم المخالفة بين كلام الهداية وقاضيخان وليس كذلك، فقول قاضيخان لا يصير إذنا أي في حق ذلك التصرف الذي صادفه السكوت ويصير إذناً فيما بعده ويدل عليه ذكر المرتهن . قال في البدائع والإذن بطريق الدلالة كما إذا رأى عبده يبيع وهو يبيع
And it is established by silence: if he sees his slave selling and buying, permission is established for the slave by the silence of the master when he sees him selling and buying, and there is no prior indication to negate it. There is no difference in that whether he sells a specific owned by the master or by another with his permission or without his permission, a valid sale or a void one. Thus in al-Hidayah and other works. And Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: If he sees a specific owned by the owner and remains silent, it is not permission; similarly, if the mortgagee sees the mortgagor selling and remains silent, the mortgage is not void. And al-Tahawi narrated from our companions that it is consent and the mortgage is void; as reported by Imam al-Zayla'i. The apparent meaning of his speech is that he understood a discrepancy between the statement of al-Hidayah and Qadi Khan, but it is not so. Qadi Khan's statement that it does not become permission means with respect to that specific transaction which the silence encountered, and it becomes permission for later transactions; and this is indicated by the mention of the mortgagee. He said in al-Bada'i: And permission by way of indication, like when he sees his slave selling and he is selling.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage deals with the establishment of permission through implication (dilalah), specifically by the master's silence. The general Hanafi position, as stated in al-Hidayah, is that if the master sees his slave engaging in buying and selling and remains silent without any prior indication to the contrary, this silence constitutes permission for the slave to trade. This applies whether the slave sells the master's property or another's, with or without prior permission, and whether the sale is valid or void. However, Qadi Khan in his Fatawa makes a distinction: if the master sees the slave selling a specific item that belongs to the master and remains silent, that silence does not create permission for that specific transaction, but does create permission for future transactions. He likens this to a mortgagee who sees the mortgagor selling the mortgaged property and says nothing; the mortgage is not void (meaning the sale is not consented to) but the silence may imply consent for future transactions. Al-Tahawi narrates a different view from the Hanafi school that such silence is consent and voids the mortgage. The text resolves the apparent conflict: Qadi Khan's 'not becoming permission' refers only to the past transaction, while the silence is permission for the future. The relied-upon position is that silence implies permission for future trades but does not retroactively validate the specific transaction observed.

Key Terms
السكوت (al-sukut): The master's silence upon seeing his slave trade; this silence can be interpreted as implied permission.
الإذن دلالة (al-idhn dilalah): Implied permission, established by actions or circumstances (like silence) rather than explicit words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciled view in this passage, what is the effect of the master's silence when he sees his slave selling a specific item?
A) The sale is valid and the slave becomes authorized for future trades.
B) The sale is not valid, but the slave becomes authorized for future trades.
C) The sale is valid only if the slave returns the item.
D) The sale is void and the slave is not authorized for any trades.

Key Takeaway
The master's silence upon seeing his slave trade establishes implied permission for future trades only, not for the specific transaction observed.

Permission by Silence: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on implicit permission through master's silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on permission by silence, explain the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal based on custom.

Core Idea
Silence permits future transactions, not the witnessed one—custom speaks louder than ambiguity.

When a master sees his slave trading and says nothing, does that grant permission? The Hanafi school says yes—but only for future trades. The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing silence is ambiguous. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proof from custom and the scholarly debate that follows.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify that the master's silence when seeing his slave trade establishes permission (idhn) for subsequent transactions, but not for the specific transaction witnessed.
Synthesize from the opening and the reconciliation of al-Hidayah and Qadi Khan.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
Find the custom-based argument: masters who disapprove would forbid; silence therefore indicates consent, analogous to the Prophet's silence.
The passage says: 'هذه التصرفات مبنية على عادات الناس... فإذا سكت دل على رضاه به'.
3
Identify the opposing position
State that Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar reject permission via silence because silence is ambiguous and cannot prove consent.
Their dalil: 'السكوت يحتمل الرضا والرد فلا يثبت بالشك'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Note how the Hanafi rebuttal appeals to custom to resolve ambiguity, and that the burden of proof shifts: the master would have objected if displeased.
The response begins with 'قلنا' and invokes custom as a decisive contextual factor.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis deem the slave ma'dhun for future trades; Shafi'is keep him interdicted unless explicit permission is given.
This affects the validity of countless transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويثبت بالسكوت بأن رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري يثبت الإذن للعبد بسكوت المولى عند ما يراه يبيع ويشتري ولم يتقدم قرينة بنفيه . ولا فرق بين ذلك أن يبيع عيناً مملوكاً للمولى أو لغيره بإذنه أو بغير إذنه بيعاً صحيحاً أو فاسداً ؛ كذا في الهداية وغيرها وقال قاضيحان في فتاواه : إن رآه عيناً من أعيان المالك فسكت لم يكن إذناً، وكذا المرتهن إذا رأى الراهن يبيع فسكت لا يبطل الرهن. وروى الطحاوي عن أصحابنا أنه رضا ويبطل الرهن ؛ كذا نقله الإمام الزيلعي. وظاهر كلامه أنه فهم المخالفة بين كلام الهداية وقاضيخان وليس كذلك، فقول قاضيخان لا يصير إذنا أي في حق ذلك التصرف الذي صادفه السكوت ويصير إذناً فيما بعده ويدل عليه ذكر المرتهن . قال في البدائع والإذن بطريق الدلالة كما إذا رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري يصير مأذوناً في التجارة عندنا إلا في البيع الذي صادفه السكوت . وقال محمد رحمه الله : وهذا بمنزلة ما لو رأى المولى عبده المسلم يشتري الخمر أو الخنزير فسكت يصير مأذوناً في التجارة وإن كان لا يجوز هذا الشراء فكذا هنا، فكيف يجوز حمل كلام قاضيخان على خلاف ما ذكر محمد في الأصل؟ وفي المحيط البرهاني قال محمد في الأصل: إذا نظر الرجل إلى عبده وهو يبيع ويشتري ولم ينهه عن ذلك يصير العبد مأذوناً في التجارة عند علمائنا الثلاثة، وإذا رأى عبده يبيع عيناً من أعيان ماله يصير مأذوناً في التجارة ولكن لا يجوز بيعه مال المولى. وفي قاضيخان : إذن الصغير في التجارة وأبوه يأبي صح إذن القاضي إذا رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري فسكت لم يكن إذناً ا هـ . فهم بعض أهل العصر أن سكوت القاضي إذا رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري لا يكون اذناً بخلاف سكوت المولى كما فهم الإمام الزيلعي كما تقدم وليس كذلك، بل المراد لا يكون إذناً في الذي سكت عنده ويكون إذناً في الذي بعده كما تقدم، ولو أمره المولى أن يبيع متاع غيره يصير مأذوناً، ولو رأى عبده يشتري شيئاً ويبيع في حانوته فسكت حتى باع متاعاً كثيراً من ذلك كان إذناً ولا ينفذ على المولى بيع العبد ذلك المتاع. ولو رأى المولى عبده يشتري شيئاً بدراهم المولى أو دنانيره فلم ينهه يصير إذناً، فإن كان هذا الثمن من مال المولى كان للمولى أن يرده ولا يبطل البيع بالاسترداد، ولو أن رجلاً أجنبياً دفع إلى عبده مالاً ليبيعه فباعه والمولى يراه ولم ينهه كان إذناً ويجوز ذلك البيع على صاحب المتاع. واختلفوا في عهدة البيع ؛ قيل يرجع إلى الآمر، وقيل إلى العبد.
وفي المحيط : وإن لم يره المولى جاز البيع والعهدة على صاحب المتاع لأن عهدة العبد المحجور متى توكل عن غيره يكون على الموكل ولو اشترى عبداً على أنه بالخيار فرآه يتصرف فلم ينهه فإن لحقه دين فهو نقض للبيع وإلا فلا، وإن تم البيع فهو محجور عليه. والفرق أن الإذن لا ينافي في خيار البائع لأن الإذن مع خيار البائع يجتمعان ويفترقان، فمن باع عبداً مأذوناً على أنه بالخيار بقي العبد مأذوناً له في مدة الخيار فلم يكن إذن البائع منافياً خياره فبقي خياره، وأما الأذن مع خيار المشتري لا يجتمعان فإن من اشترى مأذوناً على أنه بالخيار بطل الإذن، وإن أذن المشتري بالخياره سقط خياره، وإن كان العبد اكتسب شيئاً فهو للمشتري، فإن اكتسب بعد القبض طاب وقبل القبض يتصدق به ؛ قيل هذا قولهما، وعند الإمام الكسب للبائع ا هـ . وقال الإمام الشافعي وزفر : لا يثبت الإذن بسكوت المولى فيما ذكرنا لأن السكوت يحتمل الرضا الرد فلا يثبت بالشك كما لو رأى أجنبياً ماله فسكت ولم ينهه أو رأى القاضي الصبي والمعتوه ولم يكن لهما ولي أو عبدهما، وكذا إذا رأى العبد يتزوج أو الأمة تتزوج، وكذا لو تلف مال غيره وهو ينظر لم يكن ذلك إذناً . قلنا : هذه التصرفات مبنية على عادات الناس وقد جرت العادة أن لا يرضى بتصرف عبده ينهاه ويؤدبه فإذا سكت دل على رضاه به وصار إذناً له لأجل دفع الضرر فصار كسكوت النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام
He said: And it is established by silence: if he sees his slave buying and selling, permission is established for the slave by the master's silence when he sees him buying and selling, and no prior indication of denial. There is no difference between selling an owned commodity of the master or of others with or without his permission, a valid or void sale; so in al-Hidayah and others. Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: If he sees him selling a specific owned commodity of the owner and remains silent, it is not permission. Likewise, if the mortgagee sees the mortgagor selling and remains silent, the mortgage is not invalidated. Al-Tahawi narrated from our companions that it is consent and invalidates the mortgage; as transmitted by Imam al-Zayla'i. The apparent of his speech is that he understood contradiction between the speech of al-Hidayah and Qadi Khan, but it is not so. Qadi Khan's statement that it does not become permission means with respect to that transaction which the silence coincided with, but it becomes permission for what follows it; and this is indicated by the mention of the mortgagee. He said in al-Bada'i': Permission by way of indication: as if he sees his slave buying and selling, he becomes permitted in trade according to us, except for the sale which the silence coincided with. Muhammad said: This is like if the master sees his Muslim slave buying wine or pigs and remains silent, he becomes permitted in trade, even though this purchase is not permissible; so too here. How can Qadi Khan's statement be interpreted contrary to what Muhammad mentioned in the original? In al-Muhit al-Burhani, Muhammad said in the original: If a man looks at his slave while he is buying and selling and does not forbid him from that, the slave becomes permitted in trade according to our three scholars. If he sees his slave selling a specific owned commodity of his wealth, he becomes permitted in trade, but his sale of the master's wealth is not valid. In Qadi Khan: Permission of the minor in trade and his father refuses; the permission of the judge is valid. If he sees his slave buying and selling and remains silent, it is not permission. Some contemporaries understood that the silence of the judge when he sees his slave buying and selling is not permission, unlike the silence of the master, as Imam al-Zayla'i understood, but it is not so. Rather, it means it is not permission for that which he was silent about, but becomes permission for what follows it, as before. If the master orders him to sell another's goods, he becomes permitted. If he sees his slave buying something and selling in his shop and remains silent until he sold a lot of that goods, it is permission, but the slave's sale of that goods is not binding on the master. If the master sees his slave buying something with the master's dirhams or dinars and does not forbid him, it becomes permission. If this price was from the master's wealth, the master has the right to return it, and the sale is not invalidated by the reclaim. If a foreign man gives the slave money to sell and sells while the master sees and does not forbid, it is permission, and that sale is valid against the owner of the goods. They differed about the liability of the sale: some said it returns to the commissioner, some said to the slave. In al-Muhit: If the master did not see him, the sale is valid and liability on the owner of the goods, because the liability of the interdicted slave when he acts as agent for another is on the principal. If he bought a slave with a stipulation of option, and the master sees him transacting and does not forbid, if then a debt befalls him, it is a rescission of the sale; otherwise not. If the sale is completed, he remains interdicted. The difference: permission does not conflict with the seller's option, because permission and the seller's option coexist and separate. Whoever sells a permitted slave with a stipulation of option, the slave remains permitted during the option period, so the seller's permission was not contrary to his option, so his option remains. As for permission with the buyer's option, they do not coexist: whoever buys a permitted slave with a stipulation of option, the permission becomes void. If the buyer permits with his option, his option falls. If the slave earns something, it is for the buyer. If he earns after possession, it is pure; before possession, he gives it in charity. Some said this is their view; according to Imam, the earning is for the seller. Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar said: Permission is not established by the master's silence in what we mentioned, because silence may indicate consent or rejection, so it is not established by doubt, just as if a stranger sees his wealth and remains silent and does not forbid, or if the judge sees a minor or an insane person and no guardian for them or their slave, likewise if he sees the slave marrying or the female slave marrying, likewise if the wealth of another perishes while he is watching, this is not permission. We say: These transactions are based on the customs of people, and the custom is that one who is not pleased with his slave's transaction forbids him and disciplines him; so if he remains silent, it indicates his consent, and it becomes permission for him for the sake of preventing harm, so it becomes like the silence of the Prophet (peace be upon him).
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses implicit permission (al-idhn bi al-dalālah), specifically permission established by the master's silence (sukūt). The Hanafi position, based on the Qur'anic principle of custom (al-‘urf) and the example of the Prophet's silence, holds that if a master sees his slave engaging in trade and does not object, the silence constitutes permission for future transactions, though not necessarily for the specific transaction witnessed. This is supported by Imam Muhammad's analogy: even if the slave buys prohibited items like wine, the silence still makes him a ma'dhun. The text reconciles apparent contradictions between al-Hidayah and Qadi Khan by clarifying that Qadi Khan's view (silence not being permission) applies only to the witnessed transaction, not future ones. A dispute is then presented: Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar argue that silence cannot establish permission because it is ambiguous (could be consent or rejection). The Hanafi response (beginning with 'qulnā') is that custom (al-‘ādah) determines that a master who disapproves would forbid and punish; silence thus indicates consent. This is analogous to how the Prophet's silence on certain matters is taken as approval. The passage also includes various sub-rulings: when the slave sells the master's property, involvement of third parties, option stipulations, and earning during the option period.

Key Terms
الإذن دلالة (al-idhn dalālah): Implicit permission inferred from the master's behavior, especially silence when seeing the slave trade.
السكوت (al-sukūt): Silence of the master when witnessing the slave's commercial activity, without a prior indication of denial.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission (idhn) to engage in trade, whether explicitly or implicitly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what does the master's silence when seeing his slave trade establish?
A) Permission for the specific transaction witnessed
B) Permission for future transactions only
C) No permission at all
D) Permission for both the witnessed and future transactions

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the master's silence when seeing his slave trade constitutes permission for future transactions, based on custom, unlike the Shafi'i view that requires explicit consent.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Master's Silence as Permission for Slave to Trade
Inter-madhhab dispute on silence as permission with proofs and counterproofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on silence as permission, explain the custom-based reasoning, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument.

Core Idea
Custom converts ambiguous silence into clear permission when the master has a right in the property.

When a master sees his slave buying and selling and remains silent, does that silence grant the slave permission to trade? The Hanafi school says yes—based on custom and harm prevention—while Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar say no, because silence is inherently ambiguous. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proof, the objections, and the key distinction that silence only permits future transactions, not the witnessed one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Position
State clearly that silence constitutes idhn (permission) for future transactions, but not for the specific sale observed. Note the nuance that the witnessed sale is invalid because harm is immediate.
2
Examine the Hanafi Dalil
The proof relies on custom (urf): a master who disapproves would normally forbid; silence thus indicates consent. Also analogy (qiyas) to the Prophet's silence (taqrir) and to the virgin's silence in marriage.
3
Consider the Shafi'i/Zufar Objection
They argue silence is ambiguous (yahamil al-rida wa al-radd) and cannot establish permission, analogizing to the silence of a judge or a stranger.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response distinguishes: the master has a right in the slave's property, so his silence has legal effect; the judge has no such right. Custom removes ambiguity because a disapproving master would act.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, the slave can validly trade after silence; under Shafi'i law, explicit permission is required for each transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون
١٥٩
ويشتري يصير مأذوناً في التجارة عندنا إلا في البيع الذي صادفه السكوت . وقال محمد رحمه الله : وهذا بمنزلة ما لو رأى المولى عبده المسلم يشتري الخمر أو الخنزير فسكت يصير مأذوناً في التجارة وإن كان لا يجوز هذا الشراء فكذا هنا، فكيف يجوز حمل كلام قاضيخان على خلاف ما ذكر محمد في الأصل؟ وفي المحيط البرهاني قال محمد في الأصل: إذا نظر الرجل إلى عبده وهو يبيع ويشتري ولم ينهه عن ذلك يصير العبد مأذوناً في التجارة عند علمائنا الثلاثة، وإذا رأى عبده يبيع عيناً من أعيان ماله يصير مأذوناً في التجارة ولكن لا يجوز بيعه مال المولى. وفي قاضيخان : إذن الصغير في التجارة وأبوه يأبي صح إذن القاضي إذا رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري فسكت لم يكن إذناً ا هـ . فهم بعض أهل العصر أن سكوت القاضي إذا رأى عبده يبيع ويشتري لا يكون اذناً بخلاف سكوت المولى كما فهم الإمام الزيلعي كما تقدم وليس كذلك، بل المراد لا يكون إذناً في الذي سكت عنده ويكون إذناً في الذي بعده كما تقدم، ولو أمره المولى أن يبيع متاع غيره يصير مأذوناً، ولو رأى مولى عبده يشتري شيئاً ويبيع في حانوته فسكت حتى باع متاعاً كثيراً من ذلك كان إذناً ولا ينفذ على المولى بيع العبد ذلك المتاع. ولو رأى المولى عبده يشتري شيئاً بدراهم المولى أو دنانيره فلم ينهه يصير إذناً، فإن كان هذا الثمن من مال المولى كان للمولى أن يرده ولا يبطل البيع بالاسترداد، ولو أن رجلاً أجنبياً دفع إلى عبده مالاً ليبيعه فباعه والمولى يراه ولم ينهه كان إذناً ويجوز ذلك البيع على صاحب المتاع. واختلفوا في عهدة البيع ؛ قيل يرجع إلى الآمر، وقيل إلى العبد.
وفي المحيط : وإن لم يره المولى جاز البيع والعهدة على صاحب المتاع لأن عهدة العبد المحجور متى توكل عن غيره يكون على الموكل ولو اشترى عبداً على أنه بالخيار فرآه يتصرف فلم ينهه فإن لحقه دين فهو نقض للبيع وإلا فلا، وإن تم البيع فهو محجور عليه. والفرق أن الإذن لا ينافي في خيار البائع لأن الإذن مع خيار البائع يجتمعان ويفترقان، فمن باع عبداً مأذوناً على أنه بالخيار بقي العبد مأذوناً له في مدة الخيار فلم يكن إذن البائع منافياً خياره فبقي خياره، وأما الأذن مع خيار المشتري لا يجتمعان فإن من اشترى مأذوناً على أنه بالخيار بطل الإذن، وإن أذن المشتري بالخياره سقط خياره، وإن كان العبد اكتسب شيئاً فهو للمشتري، فإن اكتسب بعد القبض طاب وقبل القبض يتصدق به ؛ قيل هذا قولهما، وعند الإمام الكسب للبائع ا هـ . وقال الإمام الشافعي وزفر : لا يثبت الإذن بسكوت المولى فيما ذكرنا لأن السكوت يحتمل الرضا والرد فلا يثبت بالشك كما لو رأى أجنبياً ماله فسكت ولم ينهه أو رأى القاضي الصبي والمعتوه ولم يكن لهما ولي أو عبدهما، وكذا إذا رأى العبد يتزوج أو الأمة تتزوج، وكذا لو تلف مال غيره وهو ينظر لم يكن ذلك إذناً . قلنا : هذه التصرفات مبنية على عادات الناس وقد جرت العادة أن لا يرضى بتصرف عبده ينهاه ويؤدبه فإذا سكت دل على رضاه به وصار إذناً له لأجل دفع الضرر فصار كسكوت النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عند أمر يعاينه، وكسكوت البكر والشفيع والمولى [؟] عند ما ماله یری يقسم بين الغانمين بخلاف ما إذا أكره لأنا لو جعلناه إجازة حصل ضرر عظيم وبخلاف القاضي فإنه لا حق له في مالهما فلا يكون سكوته إذناً فلا بد من التصريح. قال في العناية : فإن قيل: عين هذا التصرف الذي يراه يبيع فيه غير صحيح فكيف يصح غيره؟ أجيب بأن الضرر في التصرف الذي يراه يبعه محقق بإزالة ملكه عن بائعه في الحال فلا يثبت وفي غيره ليس محققاً لأن الدين قد يلحقه وقد لا يلحقه فصح فيه النهي . قيدنا بقولنا ولم يتقدم قرينة تنفيه قال في المحيط: لو قال لأهل السوق إذا رأيتم عبدي هذا يتجر فإني لا آذن له ثم رآه يتجر فسكت لا مأذوناً له لأنه متی ولو عبر بأن قال أعلمهم بالنهي لم يصر مأذوناً له بالسكوت ا هـ . بعد السكوت لكان أولى.
The Book of the Permitted Slave. 159. And he buys, he becomes permitted in trade according to us except in the sale that coincides with the silence. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This is like the case where the master sees his Muslim slave buying wine or pork and remains silent, so he becomes permitted in trade even though this purchase is not permissible. So how can the statement of Qadi Khan be taken as contrary to what Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl? And in al-Muhit al-Burhani, Muhammad said in al-Asl: If a man looks at his slave while he is selling and buying and does not forbid him from that, the slave becomes permitted in trade according to our three imams. And if he sees his slave selling a specific item of his property, he becomes permitted in trade, but he cannot sell the master's property. And in Qadi Khan: The permission of a minor in trade while his father refuses; the permission of the judge is valid. If he sees his slave selling and buying and remains silent, it is not permission. End quote. Some people of this age understood that the silence of the judge when seeing his slave selling and buying is not permission, unlike the silence of the master, as Imam al-Zayla'i understood, as previously mentioned. But it is not so. Rather, the meaning is that it does not constitute permission for the action at which he was silent, but it constitutes permission for subsequent actions, as previously mentioned. And if the master orders him to sell another's property, he becomes permitted. And if the master sees his slave buying something and selling in his shop, and remains silent until he sells much of that property, it is permission, and the slave's sale of that property is not binding on the master. And if the master sees his slave buying something with the master's dirhams or dinars and does not forbid him, it becomes permission. If that price was from the master's property, the master has the right to reclaim it, and the sale is not voided by reclaiming. And if a stranger gives money to a slave to sell it, and he sells it while the master sees him and does not forbid him, it is permission, and that sale is valid on the owner of the goods. And they differed regarding the warranty of the sale: it is said it reverts to the commander, and it is said to the slave. And in al-Muhit: If the master does not see him, the sale is valid and the warranty is on the owner of the goods, because the warranty of a restricted slave when he acts as an agent for another is on the principal. And if he buys a slave with a stipulation of a choice, and he sees him transacting and does not forbid him, if a debt is incurred, it is a rescission of the sale; otherwise not. If the sale is completed, he is still restricted. The difference is that permission does not conflict with the seller's choice because permission and the seller's choice can coexist and separate. So if one sells a permitted slave with a stipulation of choice, the slave remains permitted during the period of choice; thus the seller's permission does not contradict his choice, so his choice remains. As for permission with the buyer's choice, they do not coexist. If one buys a permitted slave with a stipulation of choice, the permission is void. And if the buyer permits the choice, his choice is waived. If the slave earns something, it is for the buyer: if earned after possession, it is pure; before possession, he gives it in charity. It is said this is their view, and according to the Imam, the earning is for the seller. End quote. Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar said: Permission is not established by the silence of the master in what we mentioned, because silence can imply either consent or rejection, so it is not established by doubt, just as if he saw a stranger's property and remained silent and did not forbid him, or if the judge sees a minor or an insane person who has no guardian or their slave, and likewise if he sees a male slave marrying or a female slave marrying, and likewise if another's property is lost while he is watching, that is not permission. We respond: These actions are based on the customs of people, and the custom is that one does not approve of his slave's action without forbidding and disciplining him. If he is silent, it indicates his consent to it and becomes permission for him to avoid harm. So it becomes like the silence of the Prophet (peace be upon him) when observing an action, and like the silence of a virgin, the intercessor, and the [؟] master when his property is seen distributed among the warriors, unlike the case of coercion, because if we made it a ratification, great harm would occur. And unlike the judge, because he has no right in the property of the two, so his silence is not permission; thus explicit statement is necessary. In al-Inayah: It may be objected: the specific action he sees, i.e., the sale, is invalid, so how can other actions be valid? The answer is that the harm in the action he sees, i.e., the sale, is realized by removing his ownership immediately, so it is not established. In other actions, it is not realized because debt may or may not be incurred, so prohibition is valid in them. We qualify our statement with the condition that there is no prior evidence negating it. In al-Muhit: If he said to the marketplace, 'If you see this slave of mine trading, I do not permit him,' then he sees him trading and remains silent, he does not become permitted, because if he [؟] even if he expressed by saying 'I inform them of the prohibition,' he does not become permitted by silence. End quote. After silence would be better.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal effect of a master's silence upon seeing his slave engage in commercial transactions. The passage opens with the Hanafi position: when a master sees his slave buying and selling and remains silent, the slave becomes permitted (ma'dhun) to trade, except for the specific sale witnessed. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani analogizes this to the case where a master sees his Muslim slave buying wine or pork—impermissible goods—yet silence still grants permission. This shows that silence is not tied to the validity of the observed act itself. The text then clarifies a nuance: the silence does not make the witnessed transaction itself valid, but it grants permission for future transactions. Several scenarios are explored: the slave selling the master's property, buying with the master's money, or acting as an agent for a third party. The key principle is that custom ('urf) dictates that a master who disapproves would normally forbid and discipline, so silence indicates consent. The Hanafi response to Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar argues that silence in this context is not ambiguous because of established custom. They analogize to the Prophet's silence when observing actions, and to other cases in fiqh where silence implies consent. The objection regarding the invalidity of the observed sale is answered by distinguishing between the immediate harm of that sale and the uncertain harm of future transactions. Finally, a condition is added: if the master had previously issued a general prohibition against the slave trading, subsequent silence does not constitute permission.

Key Terms
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission granted by the master to the slave to engage in commercial transactions, which can be express or implied by silence.
السكوت (al-sukut): Silence of the master when witnessing the slave's trade, which by custom indicates consent and thus constitutes permission.
العهدة (al-'uhdah): Warranty or liability in a sale; the responsibility for defects or claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a master seeing his slave buying and selling and remaining silent?
A) It does nothing; explicit permission is required.
B) It makes the witnessed sale valid but not future sales.
C) It grants permission for future transactions but not the witnessed one.
D) It cancels the slave's status as restricted.

Key Takeaway
Master's silence, supported by custom, grants the slave permission for future trade, but does not validate the specific sale observed.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Scope of General Permission and Gross Deficiency
Expansion of ruling on general permission and ghubn fahish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the scope of general permission for a slave and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding sales with gross deficiency.

Core Idea
General permission to trade includes sales with gross deficiency according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding general permission (idhn 'amm) granted to a slave. The key question is whether such permission allows the slave to sell with gross deficiency (ghubn fahish). The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this point.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of general permission
When the master says 'I have permitted you to trade' without specifying items, it is general and includes all commercial transactions.
The author notes that the word 'tijarah' (trade) with the definite article indicates generality.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that the slave may sell even with gross deficiency. This is because the sale occurs within a trade contract and may be a strategy for profit.
The Imam considers it trade, not a gift.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's ruling and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that such a sale is not permissible because it is effectively a gift, which requires specific authorization. They analogize to the father, executor, and judge.
They say the purpose of trade is profit, and this is loss-making.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
The Imam counters that the slave is not like a guardian because the slave's permission is general, and selling at a loss can attract customers for future profit.
What occurs within a trade contract takes the ruling of trade.
5
Note the extension to minors and insane persons
The same dispute applies to a permitted minor and insane person.
This shows the principle applies broadly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (فإن أذن له عاماً لا بشراء شيء بعينه يبيع ويشتري وعبر بالفاء دون الواو لأنها تقيد التفسير ولو قال فإن أذن بعقود لا يعقد لكان أولى لأنه يفيد الإذن العام والخاص والفارق بينهما ولأنه علم من الأول ضمناً لأنه إذا قال لعبده أذنت لك في التجارة يكون عاماً لأن التجارة اسم جنس محلى بالألف واللام فكان عاماً فيتناول جميع الأعيان كما لو أعطى العبد ثوباً وأمره مولاه ببيعه كان إذناً لأنه لا يمكن حمله على الاستخدام، فإذا صار مأذوناً له في جميع التجارات كان له أن يبيع ويشتري وإن كان فيه غبن فاحش عند الأمام. وقالا : لا يجوز بما لا يتغابن فيه لأنه تبرع وهذا لا الأب والوصي والقاضي، ولأن المقصود من التجارة الاسترباح وهذه خاسرة . وللإمام أن هذه تجارة لا تبرع لأنه وقع في ضمن عقد التجارة والواقع في ضمن شيء له حكم ذلك الشيء بخلاف الأب والوصي والقاضي لأن تصرفهم مقيد بالنظر، ولأن البيع بالغبن الفاحش من صنع التجارة لاستجلاب قلوب الناس ليربحوا في صفقة أخرى، وعلى هذا الخلاف بيع الصبي والمعتوه المأذون لهما. ولو مرض العبد المأذون له وحاباً فيه يعتبر من جميع المال إذا لم يكن عليه دين، وإن كان عليه دين فمن جميع ما بقي بعد الدين لأن الاقتصار في الحر على الثلث لأجل الورثة ولا وارث للعبد ولا يقال المولى بمنزلة الوارث لأنا نقول : رضي بسقوط الأذن فصار كالوارث إذا سقط حقه بخلاف غرمائه لأنهم لم يرضوا بسقوط حقهم فلا ينفذ محاباته في حقهم، وإن كان الدين محيطاً بما في يده يقال للمشتري أد جميع المحاباة وإلا فرد البيع كما في الحر. هذا إذا كان المولى صحيحاً، وإن كان مريضاً لا تصح محاباة العبد إلا من ثلث مال المولى كتصرفات المولى بنفسه لأن المولى باستدامة الإذن بعد ما نفسه فصار تصرفه كتصرف المولى والفاحش من المحاباة وغير الفاحش فيه سواء، فلا ينفذ الكل إلا من الثلث .
قال في المحيط : لو اشترى المأذون عبداً شراء فاسداً فأغل عبده كانت الغلة له ولا يتصدق بها، ولو رده على بائعها رده مع الغلة ويتصدق البائع بها. وقيل: عند الإمام لا يرد
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (If he grants him general permission, not for the purchase of a specific thing, he sells and buys. And he used the letter fa' instead of waw because it restricts the interpretation. If he had said 'if he grants permission for contracts, not a specific contract' it would be better, because it indicates general and specific permission and the difference between them, and because it is known from the first implicitly: when he says to his slave 'I have permitted you to trade,' it is general because 'trade' (al-tijarah) is a genus noun with the definite article, so it is general and includes all items, just as if a master gives a slave a garment and orders him to sell it, it is permission, because it cannot be interpreted as employment. So when he becomes permitted in all trades, he may sell and buy even if there is gross deficiency according to the Imam. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is not permissible to sell with a deficiency that people do not overlook, because it is a gift; and this is not so for the father, executor, and judge. And because the purpose of trade is profit, and this is loss-making. For the Imam, this is trade, not a gift, because it occurs within a trade contract, and what occurs within a thing has the ruling of that thing, unlike the father, executor, and judge because their transactions are restricted by the condition of benefit. And because selling with gross deficiency is part of the practice of trade to attract people's hearts so that they profit in another deal. And upon this is the difference regarding the minor and the insane person who are permitted. And if the permitted slave becomes sick and makes an inequitable transaction (muhabah), it is considered from all his property if he has no debt. If he has debt, it is from what remains after debt, because the restriction in a free person to one-third is for the sake of heirs, and the slave has no heir. It cannot be said the master is like an heir, because we say: he consented to the lapse of permission, so he has become like an heir whose right has lapsed, unlike his creditors because they did not consent to the lapse of their right. So his inequitable transactions are not valid against them. If the debt encompasses what is in his hand, the buyer is told to pay all the muhabah or return the sale, as in the case of a free person. This is if the master is healthy. If the master is sick, the slave's muhabah is not valid except from one-third of the master's property, like the master's own transactions, because the master by continuing the permission after (??) himself, so his action becomes like the master's action, and gross and non-gross muhabah are the same; thus all is only executed from one-third. In al-Muhit: If a permitted slave buys a slave through a void (fasid) purchase, and the slave earns (ghalla), the earnings are for him and he does not give them in charity. If he returns him to the seller, he returns him with the earnings, and the seller gives them in charity. It is said, according to the Imam, he does not return.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage discusses the scope of general permission (idhn 'amm) granted to a slave for trade. When the master says 'I have permitted you to trade' without specifying particular items, it is general and covers all types of commercial transactions. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that this includes sales even with gross deficiency (ghubn fahish), because such sales are still trade, not gifts; they may be strategic to attract customers. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that such a sale is effectively a gift, which requires specific authorization, drawing an analogy to the father, executor, and judge who cannot give away property without benefit. The dispute also applies to permitted minors and insane persons. The second part deals with the slave's inequitable transactions (muhabah) when he is permitted. If the slave is healthy and has no debt, a muhabah is valid from his entire property. If he has debt, it is from the surplus after debt, because creditors' rights take precedence. If the master is sick, the slave's muhabah is treated like the master's own transaction and is limited to one-third of the master's estate, because the permission is considered an extension of the master's own capacity. The final note from al-Muhit addresses earnings from a void purchase: the slave keeps the earnings and does not give charity; if returned, the seller must give the earnings in charity.

Key Terms
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fahish): Gross deficiency in a sale where the price is significantly lower than the market value, such that people would not normally tolerate it.
الإذن العام (al-idhn al-'amm): General permission to trade without specifying particular items or transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is a sale with gross deficiency by a permitted slave valid?
A) Because the master explicitly allowed such sales.
B) Because it occurs within a trade contract and is part of trade practice.
C) Because the slave is an agent with full authority regardless of loss.
D) Because the deficiency is slight and tolerable.

Key Takeaway
General permission to trade includes sales with gross deficiency according to Abu Hanifa, overruling the Sahibayn's objection that it is a gift.

Rulings on Muhabah by a Permitted Slave
Ruling on muhabah conditions and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on a permitted slave's inequitable transaction (muhabah) under different conditions of debt and master's illness.

Core Idea
A permitted slave's muhabah is valid from his entire property if healthy and debt-free, but restricted by creditors and the master's illness.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on the permitted slave's authority, now focusing on inequitable transactions (muhabah) that are effectively gifts. The ruling depends on several factors: the slave's health, the presence of debt, and the master's health.

Guided Path
1
Define muhabah
Muhabah is an inequitable transaction where the seller gives a significant price concession, effectively a gift.
It is distinct from ordinary sales with slight deficiency.
2
Rule when the slave is healthy and has no debt
The muhabah is considered from all the slave's property (i.e., valid in full).
Because the slave has no heir to protect; creditors' rights are absent.
3
Rule when the slave has debt
The muhabah is valid only from the surplus after paying debts.
Creditors' rights take precedence; they did not consent to losing their due.
4
Rule when the master is sick
The muhabah is limited to one-third of the master's estate, like the master's own gifts, because the permission continues from a sick master.
The slave's action is treated as an extension of the master's capacity.
5
Note the special case from al-Muhit
If the slave buys through a void (fasid) contract and the item earns profit, the slave keeps the profit and does not give charity; if returned, the seller gives charity.
This shows the slave's earnings are his own until valid return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (فإن أذن له عاماً لا بشراء شيء بعينه يبيع ويشتري وعبر بالفاء دون الواو لأنها تقيد التفسير ولو قال فإن أذن بعقود لا يعقد لكان أولى لأنه يفيد الإذن العام والخاص والفارق بينهما ولأنه علم من الأول ضمناً لأنه إذا قال لعبده أذنت لك في التجارة يكون عاماً لأن التجارة اسم جنس محلى بالألف واللام فكان عاماً فيتناول جميع الأعيان كما لو أعطى العبد ثوباً وأمره مولاه ببيعه كان إذناً لأنه لا يمكن حمله على الاستخدام، فإذا صار مأذوناً له في جميع التجارات كان له أن يبيع ويشتري وإن كان فيه غبن فاحش عند الأمام. وقالا : لا يجوز بما لا يتغابن فيه لأنه تبرع وهذا لا الأب والوصي والقاضي، ولأن المقصود من التجارة الاسترباح وهذه خاسرة . وللإمام أن هذه تجارة لا تبرع لأنه وقع في ضمن عقد التجارة والواقع في ضمن شيء له حكم ذلك الشيء بخلاف الأب والوصي والقاضي لأن تصرفهم مقيد بالنظر، ولأن البيع بالغبن الفاحش من صنع التجارة لاستجلاب قلوب الناس ليربحوا في صفقة أخرى، وعلى هذا الخلاف بيع الصبي والمعتوه المأذون لهما. ولو مرض العبد المأذون له وحاباً فيه يعتبر من جميع المال إذا لم يكن عليه دين، وإن كان عليه دين فمن جميع ما بقي بعد الدين لأن الاقتصار في الحر على الثلث لأجل الورثة ولا وارث للعبد ولا يقال المولى بمنزلة الوارث لأنا نقول : رضي بسقوط الأذن فصار كالوارث إذا سقط حقه بخلاف غرمائه لأنهم لم يرضوا بسقوط حقهم فلا ينفذ محاباته في حقهم، وإن كان الدين محيطاً بما في يده يقال للمشتري أد جميع المحاباة وإلا فرد البيع كما في الحر. هذا إذا كان المولى صحيحاً، وإن كان مريضاً لا تصح محاباة العبد إلا من ثلث مال المولى كتصرفات المولى بنفسه لأن المولى باستدامة الإذن بعد ما نفسه فصار تصرفه كتصرف المولى والفاحش من المحاباة وغير الفاحش فيه سواء، فلا ينفذ الكل إلا من الثلث .
قال في المحيط : لو اشترى المأذون عبداً شراء فاسداً فأغل عبده كانت الغلة له ولا يتصدق بها، ولو رده على بائعها رده مع الغلة ويتصدق البائع بها. وقيل: عند الإمام لا يرد
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (If he grants him general permission, not for the purchase of a specific thing, he sells and buys. When Silence is Legally Effective as Permission
Explaining when silence constitutes permission and when it does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which silence constitutes permission in Hanafi fiqh and identify the exceptions.

Core Idea
Silence is permission only when the person has a right, is not coerced, and has not previously prohibited the act.

In Hanafi law, silence is sometimes treated as a valid expression of consent, especially in transactions. This lesson examines the specific contexts where silence is considered permission (idhn/ijaazah) and the important exceptions that limit this rule. Understanding these nuances prevents erroneous assumptions about consent in daily dealings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its examples
The passage gives cases where silence is permission: a master seeing a situation, a virgin's silence, a pre-emptor's silence, a master with no wealth seeing his property divided. Note that each involves a person who has a right or interest in the matter.
These are the default cases establishing the rule.
2
Recognize the exceptions
The passage explicitly excludes coercion and the judge's silence. Coercion causes great harm, and the judge has no personal right in the wealth. Also, silence is not permission if a prior prohibition exists.
3
Examine the objection and its answer
An objection is raised: if the specific transaction seen is invalid, how can silence validate other transactions? The answer distinguishes between certain harm (immediate loss) and uncertain harm (possible debt).
This shows the Hanafi reliance on evaluating harm.
4
Understand the qualification about prior prohibition
A case from al-Muhit illustrates: if a master publicly announces a prohibition and later sees the slave trading but remains silent, the silence does not grant permission because the prohibition negates the presumption of consent.
The rule only applies when no contrary indication preceded.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school generally does not accept silence as consent without an explicit sign. Hanafis rely on custom and avoidance of hardship.
This ikhtilaf is reflected in the objection and answer structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصير عند أمر يعاينه، وكسكوت البكر والشفيع والمولى العديم عند ما ماله یری يقسم بين الغانمين بخلاف ما إذا أكره لأنا لو جعلناه إجازة حصل ضرر عظيم وبخلاف القاضي فإنه لا حق له في مالهما فلا يكون سكوته إذناً فلا بد من التصريح. قال في العناية : فإن قيل: عين هذا التصرف الذي يراه يبيع فيه غير صحيح فكيف يصح غيره؟ أجيب بأن الضرر في التصرف الذي يراه يبعه محقق بإزالة ملكه عن بائعه في الحال فلا يثبت وفي غيره ليس محققاً لأن الدين قد يلحقه وقد لا يلحقه فصح فيه النهي . قيدنا بقولنا ولم يتقدم قرينة تنفيه قال في المحيط: لو قال لأهل السوق إذا رأيتم عبدي هذا يتجر فإني لا آذن له ثم رآه يتجر فسكت لا مأذوناً له لأنه متی ولو عبر بأن قال أعلمهم بالنهي لم يصر مأذوناً له بالسكوت ا هـ . بعد السكوت لكان أولى.
He becomes [permitted] due to a matter he witnesses, and similarly the silence of a virgin, the pre-emptor, and the master who has no wealth when his property is seen to be divided among the conquerors, contrary to if he is coerced, because if we made it acceptance great harm would occur, and contrary to the judge because he has no right in their wealth, so his silence is not permission, thus explicit statement is necessary. He said in al-‘Inaya: If it is said: This specific transaction that he sees he sells is invalid, so how can other than it be valid? The answer is that the harm in the transaction he sees to sell is certain by removing his ownership from its seller immediately, so it is not established, but in other transactions it is not certain, because debt may or may not afflict him, so prohibition was valid in it. We restricted by our saying 'and no contrary indication preceded'. He said in al-Muhit: If he said to the market people, 'If you see this slave of mine trading, I do not permit him,' then he saw him trading and remained silent, he is not permitted, because [when]... and if he said, 'I inform them of the prohibition,' he does not become permitted by silence. End. After silence would be better.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage discusses the principles governing when silence is considered legally effective as permission (idhn). The text begins with examples: a master who witnesses a situation, a virgin's silence, a pre-emptor's silence, and a master with no wealth when his property is being divided among soldiers. In each case, silence constitutes permission (ijaazah). However, this is not the case when there is coercion, as harm would be great. Also, a judge's silence is not permission because he has no right to their wealth. The author then cites al-‘Inaya addressing an objection: if the specific transaction seen is invalid, how can silence make other transactions valid? The answer distinguishes between certain harm (immediate loss of ownership) and uncertain harm (possibility of debt). The passage then adds a qualification: the ruling applies only if no evidence negates permission. Al-Muhit reports a case: if a master publicly declares that he does not permit his slave to trade, and later sees the slave trading and remains silent, the silence does not grant permission. The text concludes with a remark that informing others of the prohibition is superior to silence.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukoot): Remaining silent in a situation where consent is usually indicated, treated as permission in specific contexts.
إجازة (ijaazah): Post-ratification or permission that validates a transaction after the fact.
شفيع (shafee'): Pre-emptor: the neighbour or partner who has the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) in property sales.
قاضي (qadi): Judge in an Islamic court; his silence is not considered permission because he has no personal right in the disputants' wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following does silence NOT constitute permission according to this passage?
A) A virgin being asked for her consent in marriage.
B) A pre-emptor being asked to exercise his right of pre-emption.
C) A judge being asked to authorize a transaction of minors.
D) A master with no wealth watching his property being divided among soldiers.

Key Takeaway
Silence is legally effective as permission in Hanafi law only when the person has a right, is not coerced, and has not previously prohibited the act.

Ghubn Fahish and the Scope of General Trade Permission
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of sales with excessive fraud by a permitted slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ghubn fahish in a sale by a permitted slave and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A loss-making sale with ghubn fahish is still considered valid trade by Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn view it as a voidable gift.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of general trade permission granted to a slave. When a slave sells goods at a price far below market value (ghubn fahish), does the sale remain valid? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples offer differing answers based on their understanding of the nature of trade and the limits of permission.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the ruling and the condition
The passage first establishes that general permission (idhn 'amm) to trade covers all items and all contracts. Then it introduces the dispute: when the slave sells with ghubn fahish, what is the ruling?
Ghubn fahish is defined as a price disparity that people would not normally tolerate.
2
2. Understand the dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that the sale is valid because it is a trade contract; the occurrence of ghubn fahish does not change its nature. The Sahibayn argue that such a sale is effectively a gift (tabarru') and invalid because it is loss-making and not the purpose of trade.
The Sahibayn also draw an analogy to a father, executor, or judge who cannot make loss-making sales due to their duty of care.
3
3. Follow the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: the sale is a trade contract; what occurs within a contract takes the ruling of that contract. He also notes that loss-making sales can be strategic for future profit. The Sahibayn's proof: the purpose of trade is profit, and a loss-making sale negates that purpose; the slave has no authority to make gifts.
The text also mentions that the same dispute applies to a permitted minor or insane person.
4
4. Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the sale is executed and the price stands; under the Sahibayn, the sale is suspended pending the master's ratification. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is typically Abu Hanifa's view.
This affects legal practice: if a slave sells below market, the buyer acquires ownership under Abu Hanifa, but under Sahibayn the master may rescind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (قال رحمه الله: (فإن أذن له عاماً لا بشراء شيء بعينه يبيع ويشتري وعبر بالفاء دون الواو لأنها تقيد التفسير ولو قال فإن أذن بعقود لا يعقد لكان أولى لأنه يفيد الإذن العام والخاص والفارق بينهما ولأنه علم من الأول ضمناً لأنه إذا قال لعبده أذنت لك في التجارة يكون عاماً لأن التجارة اسم جنس محلى بالألف واللام فكان عاماً فيتناول جميع الأعيان كما لو أعطى العبد ثوباً وأمره مولاه ببيعه كان إذناً لأنه لا يمكن حمله على الاستخدام، فإذا صار مأذوناً له في جميع التجارات كان له أن يبيع ويشتري وإن كان فيه غبن فاحش عند الأمام. وقالا : لا يجوز بما لا يتغابن فيه لأنه تبرع وهذا لا الأب والوصي والقاضي، ولأن المقصود من التجارة الاسترباح وهذه خاسرة . وللإمام أن هذه تجارة لا تبرع لأنه وقع في ضمن عقد التجارة والواقع في ضمن شيء له حكم ذلك الشيء بخلاف الأب والوصي والقاضي لأن تصرفهم مقيد بالنظر، ولأن البيع بالغبن الفاحش من صنع التجارة لاستجلاب قلوب الناس ليربحوا في صفقة أخرى، وعلى هذا الخلاف بيع الصبي والمعتوه المأذون لهما.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: If he gives him general permission not for buying a specific thing, he may sell and buy, and he used 'fa' instead of 'waw' because it restricts interpretation. If he said 'If he permits contracts without specification' it would be better because it indicates both general and specific permission and the difference between them, and because it is known from the first implicitly, because when he says to his slave 'I have permitted you to trade,' it is general because 'trade' is a common noun with alif and lam, so it is general and covers all items, just as if he gave the slave a garment and ordered him to sell it, it is permission because it cannot be interpreted as hiring. When he becomes permitted in all trade, he may sell and buy even if there is excessive fraud (ghubn fahish) according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. But the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: It is not permissible if there is what people do not tolerate, because it is a gift, and this is not the case for the father, executor, and judge, and because the purpose of trade is profit, and this is loss-making. For the Imam, this is trade, not a gift, because it occurs under a trade contract, and what occurs under something has the ruling of that thing, unlike the father, executor, and judge because their actions are restricted by consideration, and because selling with excessive fraud is part of trade policy to attract people so they profit in another transaction. This dispute also applies to the sale by a minor and an insane person who are permitted.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage deals with the legal effect of general permission (idhn 'amm) given to a slave for trade. The text notes that the phrase 'fa-in adhina lahu' uses the particle 'fa' for sequential connection. General permission, such as saying 'I have permitted you to trade,' covers all types of merchandise. The major issue discussed is whether the slave may sell at a price involving ghubn fahish (excessive fraud). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that it is permissible because the transaction is still a trade, albeit a loss-making one, and such sales can be strategic for future profit. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two disciples) disagree, arguing that such a sale is effectively a gift (tabarru') and not a valid trade, and that the slave, unlike a guardian (father, executor, judge) who is bound to act for the ward's best interest, does not have such restriction. The ruling also applies to a permitted minor or insane person. The dispute highlights a key difference in how the Hanafi imams view the nature of a loss-making sale within trade.

Key Terms
إذن عام (idhn 'amm): General permission to trade, not restricted to specific items or transactions.
غبن فاحش (ghubn fahish): Excessive price disparity that normal people would not tolerate; considered a form of fraud.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary act of giving or charity without consideration; here, a sale at a grossly low price is deemed analogous to a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school's relied-upon position, what is the effect of ghubn fahish on a sale by a slave who has received general permission to trade?
A) The sale is void automatically.
B) The sale is valid and binding.
C) The sale is suspended pending the master's ratification.
D) The sale is valid only if the price is not less than cost.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a permitted slave's sale with ghubn fahish is valid trade, not a gift, as per the dominant view of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Illness of the Permitted Slave and Muhabat
Ruling on the validity and limits of muhabat by a sick permitted slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on muhabat by a sick permitted slave and apply the conditions (debt, master's health) that determine the effective source of payment.

Core Idea
A slave's muhabat is from his full wealth if no debts and master healthy, but limited to one-third if master ill.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we examine how the Hanafi school treats a permitted slave's sale below market value (muhabat) when either the slave or the master is sick. The ruling depends critically on the presence of debts and the master's health, and the reasoning distinguishes the slave's situation from a free person's will due to the absence of heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is muhabat by a ma'dhun slave who is ill. The ruling is that it is valid but the payment source varies.
The slave's illness triggers special rules not applicable when he is healthy.
2
Determine the effect of debts
If the slave has debts, the muhabat is taken from what remains after paying debts, because creditors' rights take precedence.
The master's consent does not override creditors' rights.
3
Consider the master's health
If the master is healthy, the slave's muhabat is from his own entire wealth (no one-third limit). If the master is ill, it is only from one-third of the master's wealth, like the master's own bequests.
The reasoning: the master's permission after his own illness makes the slave's transaction akin to the master's transaction.
4
Understand the rationale for no one-third limit
A free person's will is limited to one-third to protect heirs. The slave has no heir, so no such limit applies unless the master, who would inherit, is ill and his consent is deemed incomplete.
This is the key distinction from a free person's testamentary disposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو مرض العبد المأذون له وحاباً فيه يعتبر من جميع المال إذا لم يكن عليه دين، وإن كان عليه دين فمن جميع ما بقي بعد الدين لأن الاقتصار في الحر على الثلث لأجل الورثة ولا وارث للعبد ولا يقال المولى بمنزلة الوارث لأنا نقول : رضي بسقوط الأذن فصار كالوارث إذا سقط حقه بخلاف غرمائه لأنهم لم يرضوا بسقوط حقهم فلا ينفذ محاباته في حقهم، وإن كان الدين محيطاً بما في يده يقال للمشتري أد جميع المحاباة وإلا فرد البيع كما في الحر. هذا إذا كان المولى صحيحاً، وإن كان مريضاً لا تصح محاباة العبد إلا من ثلث مال المولى كتصرفات المولى بنفسه لأن المولى باستدامة الإذن بعد ما نفسه فصار تصرفه كتصرف المولى والفاحش من المحاباة وغير الفاحش فيه سواء، فلا ينفذ الكل إلا من الثلث.
If the permitted slave becomes ill and makes a partial sale (muhabat), it is considered from all of his wealth if he has no debt. If he has debt, it is from all that remains after the debt, because the restriction of a free person to one-third is for the sake of heirs, and the slave has no heir. It should not be said that the master is like an heir, because we say he consented to the withdrawal of permission, so he becomes like an heir when his right is withdrawn, unlike his creditors because they did not consent to the withdrawal of their right, so his partial sales are not effective against them. If the debt encompasses what is in his hand, the purchaser is told: pay all the muhabat, otherwise return the sale, as in the free person. This is if the master is healthy. If the master is ill, the slave's muhabat is not valid except from one-third of the master's wealth, like the master's own transactions, because the master's continuation of permission after his own [illness] makes his transaction like the master's transaction. And the excessive and non-excessive muhabat are the same in this, so none of it is executed except from one-third.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of muhabat (selling at a price below market as a favor) by a permitted slave who is sick, and also when the master is sick. The general rule: if the sick slave has no debts, his muhabat is effective from his entire wealth (not just one-third) because he has no heir to protect. If he has debts, the muhabat is taken from the remaining wealth after paying debts, as creditors' rights take precedence. The master is not considered an heir here because he voluntarily gave permission, so his right is waived. However, if the master is sick, then the slave's muhabat is restricted to one-third of the master's wealth, because the master's permission after his own illness causes the slave's transaction to be attributed to the master's estate, and any muhabat (whether excessive or not) is limited to one-third of the master's wealth, similar to the master's own bequests. If the slave's debts consume all his assets, the purchaser must pay the muhabat amount or return the goods, as if the debtor were free.

Key Terms
محاباة (muhabat): A sale at a price lower than market value, effectively a partial gift.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a permitted slave falls ill and his master is healthy, and the slave has no debt, how is his muhabat (partial sale) treated?
A) It is valid only from one-third of his wealth.
B) It is valid from all his wealth.
C) It is invalid because the slave is sick.
D) It requires the master's ratification.

Key Takeaway
A sick slave's muhabat is from his full wealth unless he has debts, and if the master is ill, it is from the master's one-third.
