Profit from a Defective Purchase by the Permitted Slave: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ghalla from defective purchase by ma'dhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the profit (ghalla) from a defective purchase by a ma'dhun, explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the rule on charity.

Core Idea
Profit from a defective purchase belongs to the possessor of the original ownership; the slave does not give charity, the seller does.

This passage from al-Muhit addresses a specific scenario: a permitted slave (ma'dhun) buys another slave with a defective (fasid) contract, and the purchased slave generates profit. The ruling hinges on a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding whether the profit is returned with the original and who must give it in charity. Understanding this nuance is crucial for grasping how Hanafi law treats the effects of fasid contracts, especially in the context of slave transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the contract type
Recognize that the buyer is a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) and the purchase is fasid (defective). The asset generates ghalla (profit).
Fasid contracts are voidable but can produce legal effects until rescinded.
2
State the basic ruling on profit ownership
The profit belongs to the buyer (ma'dhun) and he does not give charity. If he returns the defective slave, he returns it with the profit, and the seller must give the profit in charity.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on returning the profit
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the profit is not returned because gain follows ownership of the original. The Sahibayn hold that profit must be returned because it follows the original contract.
The text says 'قيل' indicating a transmitted difference.
4
Understand the charity ruling and its rationale
The seller gives the profit in charity because he is eligible (free), while the ma'dhun slave is not eligible for charity, so he does not give it.
5
Apply the ruling to a similar scenario
If a ma'dhun buys a slave with a fasid contract, the profit is his; if returned, the seller must give the profit in charity. This distinguishes from a free person's transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في المحيط : لو اشترى المأذون عبداً شراء فاسداً فأغل عبده كانت الغلة له ولا يتصدق بها، ولو رده على بائعها رده مع الغلة ويتصدق البائع بها. وقيل: عند الإمام لا يرد ملك الغلة لأن الأصل يكون عنده الكسب لمن كان له الملك في الأصل، وعندهما الكسب متى حدث قبل تقرر الملك يدور النماء بدوران الأصل بخلاف تلك المسألة لأنه حين حدث الكسب في يد البائع لم يكن له حق الملك وهو حق الاسترداد حتى يسدي الحق إلى الكسب. والفرق بين البائع والمأذون وإن استفاد كل واحد منهما الكسب بملك خبيث أن العبد ليس من أهل الصدقة فلا يتصدق، والبائع من أهلها فيتصدق . اشترى من العبد بيعاً فاسداً ثم باعه من مضارب العبد جاز ولم يكن فسخاً للبيع الأول كما لو باع من أجنبي فلا يثبت النقص بالشك. ولو باع جارية بعبد ودفع الجارية ولم يقبض العبد حتى حدث بها عيب فلا يخلو إما أن تعيب قبل هلاك العبد أو بعده ، وكل وجه لا يخلوا من ثلاثة أوجه : إما إن تعيب بآفة سماوية أو بفعل المشتري أو بفعل أجنبي، أما إذا حدث بها عيب بآفة سماوية بأن ذهبت عينها ثم هلك العبد فالمأذون بالخيار إن شاء قبض جاريته ولا يتبع بنقصانها، وإن شاء ضمنه قيمة جاريته يوم قبضها لأن الجارية حين قبضت كانت مضمونة بالعبد لا بالقيمة لأن النقصان صحيح للمشتري، والملك متى كان صحيحاً كان مضموناً على القابض ضمان حدث في عقد وهو الثمن والأوصاف لا تفرد بالعقد فلا تفرد بضمان العقد لأنه وجب على مشتري الجارية رد الجارية كما قبض سليمة عن العيب، وكان عليه رد قيمتها يوم قبضها لأنها دخلت ضمانه في القبض. ولو هلك العبد ثم ذهبت عينها فإن أخذها ضمنه نصف قيمتها لأن يوم العبد لما هلك صارت الجارية مضمونة على مشتريها بالقيمة ولأن النقصان إنما حدث بعد فساد الملك فيها لأن الملك قد فسد في الجارية بهلاك العبد والملك الفاسد مضمون على القابض بالقبض لا بالعقد، والأوصاف تفرد بالقبض فيفرد بضمان القبص كما في الرهن والغصب. وأما إذا تعيب بفعل المشتري بأن قطع يدها أو فقأ عينها فهو كما لو تعيب بآفة سماوية في التضمين لأن المشتري جنى على ملكه وجناية المالك على مملوكه هدر فلم يخلف بدلاً فصار كأنه مات بآفة سماوية، وإن تعينت بفعل أجنبي بأن قطع يدها أو وطئها بشبهة فأخذ أرشها وعقرها أو ولدت من غير سيدها؛ فإن كان قبل هلاك العبد لم يكن للعبد إلا قيمتها يوم العقبد لأنه لما هلك العبد فسد البيع في الجارية فوجب على مشتري الجارية ردها للفساد وقد عجز عن ردها حكماً لأنه حدث بعد القبض زيادة منفصلة من الجارية في ملك صحيح، ومثل هذه الزيادة تمنع انفساخ الملك في الأصل، فإذا تعذر فسخ البيع في الجارية صار المشتري عاجزاً عن رده قيمتها .. وإن كان بعد هلاك العبد أخذ الجارية وعقرها وولدها وأرشها إن شاء من المشتري، وإن شاء من الجاني لأن الزيادة المنفصلة لا تمنع انفساخ البيع في ملك فاسد كما لو وقع البيع في الجارية فاسداً في الابتداء ثم حدث منها زيادة منفصلة كان للبائع حق الاسترداد في الأصل فسرى ذلك الحق إلى الزوائد ثم إن شاء ضمن المشتري لأن النقصان لو حدث بآفة سماوية كان له تضمينه فإذا حدث بفعله أولى، فإن شاء ضمن الجاني البحر الرائق ج / م١١
He said in al-Muhit: If the permitted slave bought a slave with a defective purchase (fasad) and then his slave produced profit, the profit belongs to him and he does not give it in charity. If he returns it to its seller, he returns it with the profit, and the seller gives it in charity. It is said: According to the Imam, he does not return ownership of the profit because the principle with him is that gain is for the one who has ownership in the original. According to the two [disciples], whenever gain occurs before the establishment of ownership, the increase follows the original contract. Contrary to that issue, because when the gain occurred in the hand of the seller, he had no right of ownership, but a right of recovery, until the right is fulfilled regarding the gain. The difference between the seller and the permitted slave, even though each gained profit through a corrupt ownership, is that the slave is not eligible for charity, so he does not give it, while the seller is eligible and gives it. He [the slave] bought from another slave a defective sale, then sold it to the mudarib of the slave, it is valid and is not a rescission of the first sale, just as if he sold to a stranger, so loss is not established by doubt. If he sold a slave-girl for a slave and handed over the slave-girl but did not take possession of the slave until a defect occurred in her, then either the defect occurred before the destruction of the slave or after. Each case has three possibilities: either the defect occurred by a heavenly affliction, or by the act of the buyer, or by the act of a third party. If a defect occurs by a heavenly affliction such as losing an eye, then the slave dies, the permitted slave has the option: he may take possession of his slave-girl and not claim compensation for the defect, or he may claim the value of the slave-girl on the day he took possession. Because when the slave-girl was taken possession, she was guaranteed by the slave, not by the value, because the defect is valid for the buyer. When ownership is valid, it is guaranteed on the possessor as a guarantee arising from contract, being the price, and attributes are not separated by contract, so they are not separately subject to contractual guarantee. Because the buyer of the slave-girl was obliged to return her as she was taken free from defect, and he was obliged to return her value on the day he took possession because she entered his guarantee upon taking possession. If the slave dies and then she loses an eye, then if he takes her, he guarantees half her value because from the day the slave died, the slave-girl is guaranteed on her buyer by value, and because the defect occurred after the corruption of ownership in her, because ownership became corrupted in the slave-girl by the death of the slave, and corrupted ownership is guaranteed on the possessor by takeover, not by contract, and attributes are separated by takeover, so they are separately subject to guarantee of takeover, as in pledge and usurpation. As for if she is defective by the act of the buyer, such as cutting off her hand or gouging out her eye, it is like a defect by heavenly affliction in terms of liability, because the buyer harmed his own property, and the harm by an owner on his property is invalid, so it leaves no substitute, so it is as if she died by a heavenly affliction. If she is defective by the act of a third party, such as cutting off her hand or having intercourse by doubtful right, and he takes her arsh (compensation for injury) and 'aqr (dower for intercourse) or she gives birth from someone other than her owner; then if it was before the slave died, the [ma'dhun] only has her value on the day of the contract, because when the slave died, the sale became void regarding the slave-girl, so the buyer of the slave-girl was obliged to return her due to invalidity, but he is legally unable to return her because after possession a separate increase occurred in valid ownership, and such increase prevents the dissolution of ownership in the original. If the dissolution of the sale regarding the slave-girl becomes impossible, the buyer becomes unable to return her value... And if it was after the slave died, he may take the slave-girl, her 'aqr, child, and arsh from the buyer if he wishes, or from the wrongdoer, because a separate increase does not prevent dissolution of a corrupted ownership, just as if the sale of the slave-girl was defective from the beginning and then a separate increase occurred, the seller has the right to reclaim the original, and that right extends to the increases. Then if he wishes, he may claim from the buyer, because if the defect had occurred by heavenly affliction he could claim compensation from him, so if it occurs by his act it is more so. And if he wishes, he may claim from the wrongdoer.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Muhit provides detailed rules on transactions involving the permitted slave when there is a defective purchase (fasad) and subsequent defects or options. The first scenario: the slave buys another slave in a defective contract, and the purchased slave generates profit (ghalla). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the profit belongs to the buyer (the ma'dhun) and he does not need to give it in charity when returning the slave; the seller must give the profit in charity. The two disciples differ, holding that profit follows the ownership of the original. The text then discusses a case where the ma'dhun buys a slave-girl for a slave, and a defect occurs before delivery of the slave. There are three categories of defect: heavenly (natural), buyer's act, or third party act. For each, the text outlines the option available to the ma'dhun and the measure of compensation. For instance, if a heavenly defect occurs before the slave's death, the ma'dhun can either keep the slave-girl without compensation or claim her value on the day of possession. If after the slave's death, the guarantee changes. The rules also cover acts by the buyer (e.g., injuring the slave-girl) which are considered harmless to the buyer's own property, and acts by a third party, where the ma'dhun can seek compensation either from the buyer or the third party. The passage concludes with a discussion of separate increases (like birth of a child) and how they affect the right of rescission.

Key Terms
شراء فاسد (shira' fasad): A purchase that is defective but not void; it can produce legal effects like ownership of benefits.
غلة (ghalla): Profit or income from an asset, e.g., wages, rent, produce.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by the owner to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the seller give the profit in charity while the ma'dhun does not?
A) The ma'dhun is not eligible for charity (ahl al-sadaqa).
B) The seller is the original owner so he must purify the profit.
C) The ma'dhun is a slave so he cannot own property.
D) The profit belongs to the seller so he must give it in charity.

Key Takeaway
The profit from a fasid purchase by a ma'dhun is owned by him, but upon rescission, the profit must be returned, and the seller, being eligible, gives it in charity.

Options for Defect in the Permitted Slave's Exchange: Before and After Destruction of Counter-Value
Ruling with proof on options for defect in an exchange by ma'dhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on khiyar al-'ayb when a defect occurs in a slave-girl before or after the destruction of the counter-value (slave), distinguishing between heavenly affliction, buyer's act, and third party act.

Core Idea
The timing of the defect relative to the destruction of the counterpart determines whether the option is for value or for rescission with compensation.

This passage from al-Muhit presents a detailed scenario: a ma'dhun sells a slave-girl for a slave, but a defect occurs before the slave is delivered. The ruling depends on when the defect happens (before or after the slave's death) and the cause (heavenly, buyer's act, or third party act). Understanding these distinctions is essential for handling defective sales in exchange contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and the sequence of events
A ma'dhun sells a slave-girl for a slave. He delivers the slave-girl but does not receive the slave. Then a defect occurs in the slave-girl.
The slave (counter-value) may die before or after the defect.
2
Determine the timing: before or after the slave's death
Two timelines: defect occurs before the slave dies, or after. This affects the nature of ownership (valid vs corrupted).
3
Classify the cause of defect: heavenly, buyer's act, third party act
Three possibilities. Each has different rules on compensation and options.
The buyer's act is considered like heavenly for liability because it is a harm on his own property.
4
Apply the rule for heavenly defect before the slave's death
The ma'dhun has option: either take the slave-girl without compensation for the defect, or claim the value of the slave-girl on the day of possession (because she was guaranteed by the slave, not by value).
The guarantee is by the slave (counter-value), so the defect is compensated by the value of the cancelled contract.
5
Apply the rule for heavenly defect after the slave's death
If the slave dies first, the ownership becomes corrupted, and the slave-girl is guaranteed by her value. The ma'dhun can claim half her value if she loses an eye (partial defect).
Because the guarantee shifts from the slave to the value after the slave's death.

والفرق بين البائع والمأذون وإن استفاد كل واحد منهما الكسب بملك خبيث أن العبد ليس من أهل الصدقة فلا يتصدق، والبائع من أهلها فيتصدق . اشترى من العبد بيعاً فاسداً ثم باعه من مضارب العبد جاز ولم يكن فسخاً للبيع الأول كما لو باع من أجنبي فلا يثبت النقص بالشك. ولو باع جارية بعبد ودفع الجارية ولم يقبض العبد حتى حدث بها عيب فلا يخلو إما أن تعيب قبل هلاك العبد أو بعده ، وكل وجه لا يخلوا من ثلاثة أوجه : إما إن تعيب بآفة سماوية أو بفعل المشتري أو بفعل أجنبي، أما إذا حدث بها عيب بآفة سماوية بأن ذهبت عينها ثم هلك العبد فالمأذون بالخيار إن شاء قبض جاريته ولا يتبع بنقصانها، وإن شاء ضمنه قيمة جاريته يوم قبضها لأن الجارية حين قبضت كانت مضمونة بالعبد لا بالقيمة لأن النقصان صحيح للمشتري، والملك متى كان صحيحاً كان مضموناً على القابض ضمان حدث في عقد وهو الثمن والأوصاف لا تفرد بالعقد فلا تفرد بضمان العقد لأنه وجب على مشتري الجارية رد الجارية كما قبض سليمة عن العيب، وكان عليه رد قيمتها يوم قبضها لأنها دخلت ضمانه في القبض. ولو هلك العبد ثم ذهبت عينها فإن أخذها ضمنه نصف قيمتها لأن يوم العبد لما هلك صارت الجارية مضمونة على مشتريها بالقيمة ولأن النقصان إنما حدث بعد فساد الملك فيها لأن الملك قد فسد في الجارية بهلاك العبد والملك الفاسد مضمون على القابض بالقبض لا بالعقد، والأوصاف تفرد بالقبض فيفرد بضمان القبص كما في الرهن والغصب. وأما إذا تعيب بفعل المشتري بأن قطع يدها أو فقأ عينها فهو كما لو تعيب بآفة سماوية في التضمين لأن المشتري جنى على ملكه وجناية المالك على مملوكه هدر فلم يخلف بدلاً فصار كأنه مات بآفة سماوية، وإن تعينت بفعل أجنبي بأن قطع يدها أو وطئها بشبهة فأخذ أرشها وعقرها أو ولدت من غير سيدها؛ فإن كان قبل هلاك العبد لم يكن للعبد إلا قيمتها يوم العقبد لأنه لما هلك العبد فسد البيع في الجارية فوجب على مشتري الجارية ردها للفساد وقد عجز عن ردها حكماً لأنه حدث بعد القبض زيادة منفصلة من الجارية في ملك صحيح، ومثل هذه الزيادة تمنع انفساخ الملك في الأصل، فإذا تعذر فسخ البيع في الجارية صار المشتري عاجزاً عن رده قيمتها .. وإن كان بعد هلاك العبد أخذ الجارية وعقرها وولدها وأرشها إن شاء من المشتري، وإن شاء من الجاني لأن الزيادة المنفصلة لا تمنع انفساخ البيع في ملك فاسد كما لو وقع البيع في الجارية فاسداً في الابتداء ثم حدث منها زيادة منفصلة كان للبائع حق الاسترداد في الأصل فسرى ذلك الحق إلى الزوائد ثم إن شاء ضمن المشتري لأن النقصان لو حدث بآفة سماوية كان له تضمينه فإذا حدث بفعله أولى، فإن شاء ضمن الجاني البحر الرائق ج / م١١
He said in al-Muhit: If the permitted slave bought a slave with a defective purchase (fasad) and then his slave produced profit, the profit belongs to him and he does not give it in charity. If he returns it to its seller, he returns it with the profit, and the seller gives it in charity. It is said: According to the Imam, he does not return ownership of the profit because the principle with him is that gain is for the one who has ownership in the original. According to the two [disciples], whenever gain occurs before the establishment of ownership, the increase follows the original contract. Contrary to that issue, because when the gain occurred in the hand of the seller, he had no right of ownership, but a right of recovery, until the right is fulfilled regarding the gain. The difference between the seller and the permitted slave, even though each gained profit through a corrupt ownership, is that the slave is not eligible for charity, so he does not give it, while the seller is eligible and gives it. He [the slave] bought from another slave a defective sale, then sold it to the mudarib of the slave, it is valid and is not a rescission of the first sale, just as if he sold to a stranger, so loss is not established by doubt. If he sold a slave-girl for a slave and handed over the slave-girl but did not take possession of the slave until a defect occurred in her, then either the defect occurred before the destruction of the slave or after. Each case has three possibilities: either the defect occurred by a heavenly affliction, or by the act of the buyer, or by the act of a third party. If a defect occurs by a heavenly affliction such as losing an eye, then the slave dies, the permitted slave has the option: he may take possession of his slave-girl and not claim compensation for the defect, or he may claim the value of the slave-girl on the day he took possession. Because when the slave-girl was taken possession, she was guaranteed by the slave, not by the value, because the defect is valid for the buyer. When ownership is valid, it is guaranteed on the possessor as a guarantee arising from contract, being the price, and attributes are not separated by contract, so they are not separately subject to contractual guarantee. Because the buyer of the slave-girl was obliged to return her as she was taken free from defect, and he was obliged to return her value on the day he took possession because she entered his guarantee upon taking possession. If the slave dies and then she loses an eye, then if he takes her, he guarantees half her value because from the day the slave died, the slave-girl is guaranteed on her buyer by value, and because the defect occurred after the corruption of ownership in her, because ownership became corrupted in the slave-girl by the death of the slave, and corrupted ownership is guaranteed on the possessor by takeover, not by contract, and attributes are separated by takeover, so they are separately subject to guarantee of takeover, as in pledge and usurpation. As for if she is defective by the act of the buyer, such as cutting off her hand or gouging out her eye, it is like a defect by heavenly affliction in terms of liability, because the buyer harmed his own property, and the harm by an owner on his property is invalid, so it leaves no substitute, so it is as if she died by a heavenly affliction. If she is defective by the act of a third party, such as cutting off her hand or having intercourse by doubtful right, and he takes her arsh (compensation for injury) and 'aqr (dower for intercourse) or she gives birth from someone other than her owner; then if it was before the slave died, the [ma'dhun] only has her value on the day of the contract, because when the slave died, the sale became void regarding the slave-girl, so the buyer of the slave-girl was obliged to return her due to invalidity, but he is legally unable to return her because after possession a separate increase occurred in valid ownership, and such increase prevents the dissolution of ownership in the original. If the dissolution of the sale regarding the slave-girl becomes impossible, the buyer becomes unable to return her value... And if it was after the slave died, he may take the slave-girl, her 'aqr, child, and arsh from the buyer if he wishes, or from the wrongdoer, because a separate increase does not prevent dissolution of a corrupted ownership, just as if the sale of the slave-girl was defective from the beginning and then a separate increase occurred, the seller has the right to reclaim the original, and that right extends to the increases. Then if he wishes, he may claim from the buyer, because if the defect had occurred by heavenly affliction he could claim compensation from him, so if it occurs by his act it is more so. And if he wishes, he may claim from the wrongdoer.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Muhit provides detailed rules on transactions involving the permitted slave when there is a defective purchase (fasad) and subsequent defects or options. The first scenario: the slave buys another slave in a defective contract, and the purchased slave generates profit (ghalla). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the profit belongs to the buyer (the ma'dhun) and he does not need to give it in charity when returning the slave; the seller must give the profit in charity. The two disciples differ, holding that profit follows the ownership of the original. The text then discusses a case where the ma'dhun buys a slave-girl for a slave, and a defect occurs before delivery of the slave. There are three categories of defect: heavenly (natural), buyer's act, or third party act. For each, the text outlines the option available to the ma'dhun and the measure of compensation. For instance, if a heavenly defect occurs before the slave's death, the ma'dhun can either keep the slave-girl without compensation or claim her value on the day of possession. If after the slave's death, the guarantee changes. The rules also cover acts by the buyer (e.g., injuring the slave-girl) which are considered harmless to the buyer's own property, and acts by a third party, where the ma'dhun can seek compensation either from the buyer or the third party. The passage concludes with a discussion of separate increases (like birth of a child) and how they affect the right of rescission.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option to rescind a sale due to a defect in the subject matter.
ضمان القبض (daman al-qabd): Guarantee arising from possession; the possessor is liable for the value.
ملك فاسد (milk fasid): Corrupted ownership; occurs when the contract is valid but the consideration fails (e.g., death of one party's subject).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a heavenly defect occurs in the slave-girl after the slave (counter-value) has died, what is the ma'dhun entitled to claim?
A) The full value of the slave-girl on the day of possession.
B) The full value of the slave-girl on the day of contract.
C) A fraction of the value corresponding to the defect (e.g., half for loss of one eye).
D) Nothing, because the ownership is corrupted.

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's option for a defect in an exchange contract depends critically on when the defect occurs relative to the destruction of the counter-value: before destruction, full value claim; after, fractional compensation.

Effects of Third Party Acts and Separate Increases on the Permitted Slave's Option
Continuation of ruling with proof on third party acts and separate increases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the ma'dhun's options when a defect is caused by a third party and when the slave-girl gives birth, distinguishing between before and after the destruction of the counter-value.

Core Idea
A separate increase (like birth) in valid ownership prevents rescission, but in corrupted ownership it does not, and the ma'dhun can claim compensation from either the buyer or the wrongdoer.

This passage continues the analysis of options when a slave-girl is defective due to a third party's act or when she gives birth. The key distinction is whether the separate increase (e.g., child) occurred before or after the destruction of the counter-value (slave). This lesson explores the reasoning and practical application of these nuanced rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: third party act causing defect or birth
A third party injures the slave-girl or has intercourse (shubha) leading to children, or she gives birth from someone else.
The defect includes physical injury (arsh) or loss of value (aqr for intercourse).
2
If the defect occurs before the slave's death (valid ownership)
The ma'dhun can only claim the value of the slave-girl on the day of the contract, not the full compensation, because the separate increase (e.g., child) prevents rescission.
The increase occurred in valid ownership, so the original cannot be returned; only its value can be claimed.
3
If the defect occurs after the slave's death (corrupted ownership)
The ma'dhun can choose to claim compensation from either the buyer or the wrongdoer (third party). He can take the slave-girl, her child, the aqr, and the arsh all together.
Because the separate increase does not block rescission in corrupted ownership; the increase may be taken as well.
4
Understand the rationale for the difference
In valid ownership, the increase is like a new property that cannot be separated from the original's rescission; in corrupted ownership, the original is already voidable, so increases can be claimed.
This is analogous to a fasid sale from the start: the seller can reclaim the original and its increases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في المحيط : لو اشترى المأذون عبداً شراء فاسداً فأغل عبده كانت الغلة له ولا يتصدق بها، ولو رده على بائعها رده مع الغلة ويتصدق البائع بها. وقيل: عند الإمام لا يرد ملك الغلة لأن الأصل يكون عنده الكسب لمن كان له الملك في الأصل، وعندهما الكسب متى حدث قبل تقرر الملك يدور النماء بدوران الأصل بخلاف تلك المسألة لأنه حين حدث الكسب في يد البائع لم يكن له حق الملك وهو حق الاسترداد حتى يسدي الحق إلى الكسب. والفرق بين البائع والمأذون وإن استفاد كل واحد منهما الكسب بملك خبيث أن العبد ليس من أهل الصدقة فلا يتصدق، والبائع من أهلها فيتصدق . اشترى من العبد بيعاً فاسداً ثم باعه من مضارب العبد جاز ولم يكن فسخاً إن تعيب بآفة سماوية أو بفعل المشتري أو بفعل أجنبي، أما إذا حدث بها عيب بآفة سماوية بأن ذهبت عينها ثم هلك العبد فالمأذون بالخيار إن شاء قبض جاريته ولا يتبع بنقصانها، وإن شاء ضمنه قيمة جاريته يوم قبضها لأن الجارية حين قبضت كانت مضمونة بالعبد لا بالقيمة لأن النقصان صحيح للمشتري، والملك متى كان صحيحاً كان مضموناً على القابض ضمان حدث في عقد وهو الثمن والأوصاف لا تفرد بالعقد فلا تفرد بضمان العقد لأنه وجب على مشتري الجارية رد الجارية كما قبض سليمة عن العيب، وكان عليه رد قيمتها يوم قبضها لأنها دخلت ضمانه في القبض. ولو هلك العبد ثم ذهبت عينها فإن أخذها ضمنه نصف قيمتها لأن يوم العبد لما هلك صارت الجارية مضمونة على مشتريها بالقيمة ولأن النقصان إنما حدث بعد فساد الملك فيها لأن الملك قد فسد في الجارية بهلاك العبد والملك الفاسد مضمون على القابض بالقبض لا بالعقد، والأوصاف تفرد بالقبض فيفرد بضمان Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Muhit provides detailed rules on transactions involving the permitted slave when there is a defective purchase (fasad) and subsequent defects or options. The first scenario: the slave buys another slave in a defective contract, and the purchased slave generates profit (ghalla). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the profit belongs to the buyer (the ma'dhun) and he does not need to give it in charity when returning the slave; the seller must give the profit in charity. Key Terms
زيادة منفصلة (ziyadat munfasila): A separate increase that is physically distinct from the original, such as a child born from a slave-girl.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for a physical injury to a slave or free person, measured by the reduction in value.
عقر (aqr): Dower or compensation for unlawful intercourse with a slave-girl; usually the market value of her lost virginity or compensation for use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a third party injures a slave-girl (before the slave's death) and she later gives birth, what does the ma'dhun receive?
A) The value of the girl on the day of the contract.
B) The value of the girl plus the value of the child.
C) The arsh and aqr from the third party.
D) The girl, the child, and the arsh from the buyer.

Key Takeaway
A separate increase in valid ownership blocks rescission, limiting the ma'dhun to the contract day value; in corrupted ownership, increases can be reclaimed from the buyer or wrongdoer.

Defects in a Fasid Sale by a Ma'dhun: Timing and Causes
Defect rulings in a fasid sale by a ma'dhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for defects (ʿuyūb) in a fasid sale by a maʿdhūn, distinguishing the buyer's liability based on timing and cause of the defect.

Core Idea
Possession in corrupt ownership creates liability for defects, just as in pledge and usurpation.

In a fasid sale, a defect affecting the sold item triggers nuanced options. The ruling depends on when the defect occurs relative to the destruction of the countervalue, and whether it is caused by divine act, the buyer, or an outsider. This lesson unpacks the detailed scenario of a slave girl sold for a male slave, focusing on the core legal reasoning and the qiyas to pledge and usurpation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the transaction
A ma'dhun (permitted slave) sells a slave girl (jariya) for a male slave (abd). The buyer takes possession of the jariya but not the abd. Identify the key actors: seller (ma'dhun), buyer, and later the slave owner.
The ma'dhun is acting as a trader with limited permission.
2
Classify the defect scenario by timing and cause
The defect can occur before or after the abd dies. Within each, the cause can be divine affliction (sama'iyya), buyer's act, or outsider's act. List the 3×2 = 6 possible combinations.
Timing affects whether the jariya is guaranteed by the abd (before death) or by value (after death).
3
Apply the rule for divine affliction before abd's death
Before the abd dies, the jariya is guaranteed by the abd. The ma'dhun has an option: take back the jariya without compensation for the defect, or claim its value on the day of delivery. Understand why: the defect occurred while ownership was valid (sahih), so the jariya is guaranteed by the price (the abd).
Descriptions (awsaf) are not independent in the guarantee.
4
Apply the rule for divine affliction after abd's death
After the abd dies, ownership becomes corrupt (fasid). The jariya is now guaranteed by value via possession. The ma'dhun can take the jariya and claim half its value. Note the qiyas to pledge (rahn) and usurpation (ghasb): possession in corrupt ownership creates liability for descriptions.
This is the key analogical reasoning: possession (qabd) in fasid ownership is like possession in pledge/usurpation.
5
Extend to buyer's act and outsider's act
If the buyer causes the defect, it is treated like a divine affliction because his assault on his own property is legally void. If an outsider causes it, the ma'dhun can claim from either the buyer or the outsider, with different consequences depending on timing (before/after abd's death). Study the sub-rulings for each.
The outsider's act may add liabilities like dower (aqr) and indemnity (arsh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملك الغلة لأن الأصل يكون عنده الكسب لمن كان له الملك في الأصل، وعندهما الكسب متى حدث قبل تقرر الملك يدور النماء بدوران الأصل بخلاف تلك المسألة لأنه حين حدث الكسب في يد البائع لم يكن له حق الملك وهو حق الاسترداد حتى يسدي الحق إلى الكسب. والفرق بين البائع والمأذون وإن استفاد كل واحد منهما الكسب بملك خبيث أن العبد ليس من أهل الصدقة فلا يتصدق، والبائع من أهلها فيتصدق . اشترى من العبد بيعاً فاسداً ثم باعه من مضارب العبد جاز ولم يكن فسخاً للبيع الأول كما لو باع من أجنبي فلا يثبت النقص بالشك. ولو باع جارية بعبد ودفع الجارية ولم يقبض العبد حتى حدث بها عيب فلا يخلو إما أن تعيب قبل هلاك العبد أو بعده ، وكل وجه لا يخلوا من ثلاثة أوجه : إما إن تعيب بآفة سماوية أو بفعل المشتري أو بفعل أجنبي، أما إذا حدث بها عيب بآفة سماوية بأن ذهبت عينها ثم هلك العبد فالمأذون بالخيار إن شاء قبض جاريته ولا يتبع بنقصانها، وإن شاء ضمنه قيمة جاريته يوم قبضها لأن الجارية حين قبضت كانت مضمونة بالعبد لا بالقيمة لأن النقصان صحيح للمشتري، والملك متى كان صحيحاً كان مضموناً على القابض ضمان حدث في عقد وهو الثمن والأوصاف لا تفرد بالعقد فلا تفرد بضمان العقد لأنه وجب على مشتري الجارية رد الجارية كما قبض سليمة عن العيب، وكان عليه رد قيمتها يوم قبضها لأنها دخلت ضمانه في القبض. ولو هلك العبد ثم ذهبت عينها فإن أخذها ضمنه نصف قيمتها لأن يوم العبد لما هلك صارت الجارية مضمونة على مشتريها بالقيمة ولأن النقصان إنما حدث بعد فساد الملك فيها لأن الملك قد فسد في الجارية بهلاك العبد والملك الفاسد مضمون على القابض بالقبض لا بالعقد، والأوصاف تفرد بالقبض فيفرد بضمان القبص كما في الرهن والغصب. وأما إذا تعيب بفعل المشتري بأن قطع يدها أو فقأ عينها فهو كما لو تعيب بآفة سماوية في التضمين لأن المشتري جنى على ملكه وجناية المالك على مملوكه هدر فلم يخلف بدلاً فصار كأنه مات بآفة سماوية، وإن تعينت بفعل أجنبي بأن قطع يدها أو وطئها بشبهة فأخذ أرشها وعقرها أو ولدت من غير سيدها؛ فإن كان قبل هلاك العبد لم يكن للعبد إلا قيمتها يوم العقبد لأنه لما هلك العبد فسد البيع في الجارية فوجب على مشتري الجارية ردها للفساد وقد عجز عن ردها حكماً لأنه حدث بعد القبض زيادة منفصلة من الجارية في ملك صحيح، ومثل هذه الزيادة تمنع انفساخ الملك في الأصل، فإذا تعذر فسخ البيع في الجارية صار المشتري عاجزاً عن رده قيمتها .. وإن كان بعد هلاك العبد أخذ الجارية وعقرها وولدها وأرشها إن شاء من المشتري، وإن شاء من الجاني لأن الزيادة المنفصلة لا تمنع انفساخ البيع في ملك فاسد كما لو وقع البيع في الجارية فاسداً في الابتداء ثم حدث منها زيادة منفصلة كان للبائع حق الاسترداد في الأصل فسرى ذلك الحق إلى الزوائد ثم إن شاء ضمن المشتري لأن النقصان لو حدث بآفة سماوية كان له تضمينه فإذا حدث بفعله أولى، فإن شاء ضمن الجاني لأن الجاني صار جانباً على ملكه لإعادة الجارية إلى قديم ملكه بالفسخ. ولو حدث بها عيبان أحدهما قبل هلاك العبد والثاني بعد هلاكه فعلى هذا القياس لأن العيبين لو حدثا قبل هلاك العبد يتخير المأذون حتى لو اختار أخذ الجارية لا يكون له ضمان النقصان، ولو حدثا بعد هلاك العبد متى أخذ الجارية فله تضمين نقصان العيبين جميعاً، فإذا حدث أحدهما قبل هلاكه والآخر بعد هلاكه كان لكل واحد منهما حكم نفسه .
هذا كله إذا تعيبت الجارية في يد مشتريها، وأما إذا حدث فيها زيادة فلا يخلو إما إن كانت الزيادة منفصلة كالولد والأرش، أو كانت متصلة كالسمن والجمال. فإن كانت منفصلة، فإن ولدت قبل هلاك العبد ثم مات العبد ينظر ؛ إن كان الولد قائماً ليس للمأذون
في
أخذ الجارية لأن الزيادة المنفصلة الحادثة بعد القبض في ملك صحيح تمنع انفساخ العقد الأصل، وإن هلك الولد والأرش كان للعبد أن يأخذ الجارية ولا يتبعه بنقصان الولادة والجناية إن شاء وإن شاء ضمنه قيمتها لأن المانع من انفساخ العقد قد ارتفع وهو الزيادة فصارت كأنها لم تكن والنقصان قائم لأن الولادة في بنات آدم سبب النقصان وأنه عيب على كل حال فله أن يضمنه قيمة الجارية. ولو كان مكان الجارية شاة فنتجت في يده قبل هلاك العبد لم يكن للعبد خيار ويأخذ الشاة لأنه لا نقصان لأن الولادة في البهائم ليست بعيب . وإن هلكت الزيادة بفعل أجنبي فهو كما كان الولد قائماً لأن الولد مات وأخلف بدلاً، والفائت إلى خلف كالقائم حكماً وإن هلكت بفعل المشتري بأن أعتق المشتري أو ولد الجارية ثم هلك العبد لم يكن للمأذون على الجارية سبيل لأن الولد يسلم للمشتري من وجه فإن مولى له يرث منه إذا مات ولم يكن له عصبة أقرب منه فيؤدي إلى الربا فلا يجوز فسخ العيب في الجارية فإن مات الولد المعتق وترك ولداً كان للعبد أن يأخذ الجارية إن شاء ولا يتبعه بنقصانها، وكذلك إن ترك ولداً لا يخير ولاؤه المشتري بأن كان المعتق تزوج بأمة لرجل وحدث منها ولد ثم أعتق مولى الأمة الولد لأن المانع من فسخ العقد في الجارية هو الولد وقد زال هذا المانع بلا خلف وهذه الزيادة من خصائص مسائل هذا فيجب حفظها. وكذلك إذا قتل الولد المشتري فله الخيار بين الفسخ والتضمين وهذا لا يشكل على الرواية التي قال بأن الولادة عيب لازم في بنات آدم وذلك لأن القتل بمنزلة الموت لأن المقتول ميت بأجله، ولو مات الولد في يد مشتري الجارية يتخير المأذون فكذا هذا وإنما يشكل على الرواية التي قال بأن الولادة ليست بعيب إذا لم توجب نقصاناً لأن الولد مات ولم يخلف بدلاً لأن المشتري جنى على ملكه الصحيح وجناية المالك على ملكه هدر فصار كما لو مات الولد حتف أنفه . والجواب عنه أن الولد مات وأخلف بدلاً من دمه لأن جناية المشتري على الولد إنما تكون ملاقياً ملكه ما دام ملكه في الجارية متقرراً، فأما إذا انفسخ ملكه في الجارية بأن أخذ الجارية ولم يضمنه النقصان كانت الجناية على الولد ملاقياً ملك المأذون من وجه لأن الولد تبع
Ownership of produce because according to him (Abu Hanifa) the earning belongs to whom the original ownership belongs, and according to them (the two) the earning whenever it occurs before the establishment of ownership follows the origin, unlike that issue because when the earning occurred in the hand of the seller, he did not have the right of ownership, which is the right of recovery, until he assigns the right to the earning. And the difference between the seller and the ma'dhun, even though each of them acquires earning through bad ownership, is that the slave is not eligible for charity so he does not give charity, while the seller is eligible so he gives charity. He bought from a slave with a fasid (defective) sale then sold it to the mudarib of the slave: it is permissible and is not a rescission of the first sale, just as if he sold to a stranger, so the deficiency is not established by doubt. And if he sold a slave girl for a slave and delivered the slave girl but did not receive the slave until a defect occurred in her, then it is either before the destruction of the slave or after, and each aspect does not lack three possibilities: either the defect is by a divine affliction, or by the act of the buyer, or by the act of an outsider. As for if a defect occurs by a divine affliction such as her eye going blind then the slave dies: the ma'dhun has the option: if he wishes he may take his slave girl and not follow her with compensation for her deficiency, and if he wishes he may claim the value of his slave girl on the day he delivered her because when the slave girl was delivered she was guaranteed by the slave not by the value, because the deficiency is valid for the buyer, and when ownership is valid it is guaranteed upon the taker as a guarantee occurring in the contract, which is the price, and descriptions are not isolated by contract so they are not isolated by the guarantee of the contract because it became obligatory upon the buyer of the slave girl to return the slave girl as he received it free from defect, and upon him to return its value on the day he received it because it entered his guarantee upon taking possession. And if the slave dies then her eye goes blind: if he takes her he claims half its value because when the slave died the slave girl became guaranteed upon its buyer by value, and because the deficiency occurred after the corruption of ownership in her, because ownership in the slave girl became corrupted by the death of the slave, and corrupt ownership is guaranteed upon the taker by possession not by contract, and descriptions are isolated by possession so they are isolated by the guarantee of possession, as in pledge and usurpation. And as for if it becomes defective by the act of the buyer such as cutting her hand or gouging her eye: it is like a defect by a divine affliction in terms of compensation because the buyer assaulted his own property and the assault of the owner on his property is null, so no replacement remains, so it is as if she died by a divine affliction. And if it becomes defective by the act of an outsider such as cutting her hand or having intercourse with her under suspicion, and he takes her indemnity (arsh) and her dower (aqr), or she gives birth from other than her owner: if this was before the destruction of the slave, the slave (seller) does not have except her value on the day of the contract, because when the slave died the sale in the slave girl became fasid, so it became obligatory upon the buyer of the slave girl to return her due to corruption, and he is legally incapable of returning her because after possession a separate increase from the slave girl occurred in valid ownership, and such an increase prevents the dissolution of ownership in the origin. So when the rescission of the sale in the slave girl is impossible, the buyer becomes incapable of returning her value... And if it was after the destruction of the slave, he (the ma'dhun) takes the slave girl and her dower, her offspring, and her indemnity if he wishes from the buyer, and if he wishes from the assailant, because a separate increase does not prevent the dissolution of sale in corrupt ownership, just as if the sale in the slave girl was fasid initially then a separate increase occurred from her: the seller has the right of recovery in the origin, so that right extends to the increments. Then if he wishes he claims compensation from the buyer because if the deficiency had occurred by a divine affliction he would have had the right to claim it, so if it occurs by his act it is more appropriate. If he wishes he claims from the assailant because the assailant became an assault on his ownership in order to return the slave girl to its previous ownership by rescission. And if two defects occur, one before the destruction of the slave and the second after his destruction: according to this analogy, because if both defects occur before the destruction of the slave the ma'dhun has the option; even if he chooses to take the slave girl he does not have compensation for the deficiency, and if they occur after the destruction of the slave, whenever he takes the slave girl he has the right to claim compensation for both defects, so if one occurs before his destruction and the other after, each has its own ruling. All this if the slave girl becomes defective in the hand of her buyer. As for if an increase occurs in her: either the increase is separate like offspring and indemnity, or connected like fat and beauty. If it is separate: if she gives birth before the destruction of the slave then the slave dies, it is considered: if the offspring is existing, the ma'dhun has no way to take the slave girl because the separate increase occurring after possession in valid ownership prevents the dissolution of the original contract. And if the offspring and indemnity perish, the slave (seller) may take the slave girl and does not follow her with compensation for the deficiency of birth and the assault if he wishes, and if he wishes he claims its value because the obstacle to the dissolution of the contract has been removed, which is the increase, so it becomes as if it never was, while the deficiency remains because childbirth in daughters of Adam is a cause of deficiency and it is a defect in all cases, so he has the right to claim the value of the slave girl. And if the item were a sheep and it gave birth in his hand before the destruction of the slave, the slave does not have an option and he takes the sheep because there is no deficiency, because birth in animals is not a defect. And if the increase perishes by the act of an outsider, it is as if the offspring existed because the offspring died and left a replacement, and what is lost to a replacement is like existing in legal effect. And if it perishes by the act of the buyer, such as the buyer emancipating the offspring of the slave girl, then the slave dies: the ma'dhun has no way to the slave girl because the offspring becomes the property of the buyer in a sense, for he has a patron (mawla) who inherits from him when he dies and he has no closer agnates, and this leads to riba (usury), so rescission due to defect in the slave girl is not permitted. If the emancipated offspring dies and leaves a child, the slave has the option to take the slave girl if he wishes and does not follow her with compensation for her deficiency. Similarly, if he leaves a child whose patronage does not go to the buyer, such as if the emancipated one married a slave woman of a man and she gave birth to a child, then the master of the slave woman emancipates the child, because the obstacle to rescission of the contract in the slave girl is the offspring, and this obstacle has been removed without replacement. And this increase is from the specific issues of this section, so it must be memorized. And similarly, if the buyer kills the offspring, he has the option between rescission and claiming compensation. This is not problematic according to the riwaya that states childbirth is an inherent defect in daughters of Adam, because killing is like death, since the killed dies by his appointed term. And if the offspring dies in the hand of the buyer of the slave girl, the ma'dhun has the option, so likewise here. It is only problematic according to the riwaya that states childbirth is not a defect if it does not cause a deficiency, because the offspring died and left no replacement, since the buyer assaulted his own valid ownership and the assault of the owner on his property is null, so it becomes as if the offspring died naturally. The answer to this is that the offspring died and left a replacement from its blood money, because the buyer's assault on the offspring only meets his ownership as long as his ownership in the slave girl is established. But if his ownership in the slave girl is dissolved by his taking the slave girl and not claiming compensation for the deficiency, then the assault on the offspring meets the ownership of the ma'dhun in a sense, because the offspring is a follower.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is a detailed expansion (sharh) on rulings concerning the ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) in cases of fasid (defective) sales, particularly when a slave girl is sold for a male slave and defects or increases occur. The commentator first addresses a minor ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding ownership of earnings (kisb): Abu Hanifa holds that earnings belong to the owner of the asset, while the Sahibayn say that if the earning occurs before ownership is fully established, it follows the origin. The difference between a seller and a ma'dhun is that the seller can give charity from ill-gotten gains, but a slave cannot. 

The main discussion begins with a ruling: if a ma'dhun buys from a slave in a fasid sale and then sells to the slave's mudarib (partner), it is valid and does not rescind the first sale. 

The core of the passage analyzes a scenario where a ma'dhun sells a slave girl (jariya) for a male slave (abd). The buyer takes possession of the jariya but not the abd until a defect appears in the jariya. The ruling depends on the timing of the defect relative to the death of the abd, and the cause of the defect: divine affliction (sama'iyya), act of the buyer, or act of an outsider. 

If the defect occurs before the abd dies, the ma'dhun has an option: take back the jariya without compensation for the defect, or claim its value on the day of delivery. If the defect occurs after the abd dies, the ma'dhun can take the jariya and claim half its value if the defect is from divine affliction. The reasoning hinges on whether the jariya was guaranteed by the abd (before his death) or by value (after his death) due to the corruption of ownership. 

If the buyer causes the defect, it is treated like a divine affliction because the buyer's assault on his own property is legally void (hadar). If an outsider causes the defect, the ma'dhun can claim from the buyer or the assailant, with different rules depending on whether the defect occurs before or after the abd's death. 

The passage then discusses increases (ziyada) such as offspring. Separate increases (e.g., birth) before the abd's death prevent rescission; after the abd's death, they do not. The rulings also consider the type of animal (sheep vs human) and whether the increase perishes or is disposed of by the buyer (e.g., emancipation). 

Finally, the commentator addresses two riwayas (transmissions) about whether childbirth is a defect. One says it is always a defect; the other says it is not if it does not cause a decrease. The answer is that the offspring's death leaves a replacement (blood money) from the buyer's assault, so the ma'dhun can still claim.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade, with the master's permission to conduct transactions on his own behalf.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is defective in its conditions or subject matter, making it invalid unless corrected, but not wholly void (batil).
العيب (al-'ayb): A defect in a commodity that reduces its value or desirability, entitling the buyer to an option (khiyar) in a sale.
الآفة السماوية (al-afah al-sama'iyya): A divine affliction; a natural defect not caused by human action.
الضمان بالقبض (al-daman bi al-qabd): Liability by possession; the taker of an item is responsible for its safety and descriptions while in his control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where the defect occurs after the abd dies, what is the ma'dhun's entitlement?
A) He may take back the jariya and claim half its value.
B) He may take back the jariya without any compensation.
C) He must accept the jariya as is and cannot claim anything.
D) He may claim the full value from the buyer.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, liability for defects depends on whether they occur before or after the countervalue is destroyed, with possession creating guarantee in corrupt ownership.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
10 min
Increases and the Childbirth Defect Debate in a Fasid Sale
Rulings on increases and the childbirth defect debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules on separate and connected increases (ziyada) in a fasid sale, and evaluate the two riwayas on whether childbirth is a defect.

Core Idea
Separate increases before destruction prevent rescission; after destruction, they do not. Childbirth as a defect is debated within the school.

When an increase occurs in the sold item—like offspring, fat, or beauty—the rules differ depending on whether the increase is separate or connected, and on its timing relative to the destruction of the countervalue. This lesson also tackles the intra-school debate: is childbirth (wilada) a defect? The commentary offers a resolution.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish separate vs. connected increases
Separate increases (ziyada munfasila) are things like offspring, indemnity, or dower. Connected increases (mutasila) are like fattening or beauty. Identify which category applies in the scenario.
Connected increases are not considered independent and do not affect rescission.
2
Analyze separate increase before abd's death
If the jariya gives birth before the abd dies, the offspring is a separate increase in valid ownership. It prevents rescission; the ma'dhun cannot take back the jariya. If the offspring perishes, the obstacle is removed and the ma'dhun may take back the jariya without compensation for the birth deficiency.
The offspring existing blocks rescission; its removal (by death or loss) restores the option.
3
Analyze separate increase after abd's death
If the birth occurs after the abd dies, ownership is corrupt (fasid). A separate increase does not prevent rescission. The ma'dhun can take back the jariya, her offspring, dower, and indemnity from either the buyer or the assailant.
Corrupt ownership is flexible: increases do not block return.
4
Handle special cases: sheep, outsider's act, buyer's disposal
If the item is a sheep, birth is not a defect, so the ma'dhun can always take back without compensation. If the increase perishes by an outsider's act, it is treated as existing (legal fiction). If the buyer disposes of the offspring (e.g., emancipates it), the ma'dhun loses access to the jariya if the disposal leads to riba concerns.
These sub-rules show the complexity of Hanafi fiqh.
5
Evaluate the two riwayas on childbirth as a defect
One riwaya says childbirth is always a defect in daughters of Adam. Another says it is not a defect if it does not cause a decrease in value. The commentary resolves the second riwaya's difficulty by noting that if the offspring dies and leaves a replacement (blood money from the buyer's assault), the ma'dhun still has an option because the death with replacement is like death without replacement? Actually the answer: the offspring died and left a replacement from its blood money, so the obstacle (lack of replacement) is removed.
This illustrates how later Hanafi jurists reconcile conflicting transmissions.

ملك الغلة لأن الأصل يكون عنده الكسب لمن كان له الملك في الأصل، وعندهما الكسب متى حدث قبل تقرر الملك يدور النماء بدوران الأصل بخلاف تلك المسألة لأنه حين حدث الكسب في يد البائع لم يكن له حق الملك وهو حق الاسترداد حتى يسدي الحق إلى الكسب. والفرق بين البائع والمأذون وإن استفاد كل واحد منهما الكسب بملك خبيث أن العبد ليس من أهل الصدقة فلا يتصدق، والبائع من أهلها فيتصدق . اشترى من العبد بيعاً فاسداً ثم باعه من مضارب العبد جاز ولم يكن فسخاً للبيع الأول كما لو باع من أجنبي فلا يثبت النقص بالشك. ولو باع جارية بعبد ودفع الجارية ولم يقبض العبد حتى حدث بها عيب فلا يخلو إما أن تعيب قبل هلاك العبد أو بعده ، وكل وجه لا يخلوا من ثلاثة أوجه : إما إن تعيب بآفة سماوية أو بفعل المشتري أو بفعل أجنبي، أما إذا حدث بها عيب بآفة سماوية بأن ذهبت عينها ثم هلك العبد فالمأذون بالخيار إن شاء قبض جاريته ولا يتبع بنقصانها، وإن شاء ضمنه قيمة جاريته يوم قبضها لأن الجارية حين قبضت كانت مضمونة بالعبد لا بالقيمة لأن النقصان صحيح للمشتري، والملك متى كان صحيحاً كان مضموناً على القابض ضمان حدث في عقد وهو الثمن والأوصاف لا تفرد بالعقد فلا تفرد بضمان العقد لأنه وجب على مشتري الجارية رد الجارية كما قبض سليمة عن العيب، وكان عليه رد قيمتها يوم قبضها لأنها دخلت ضمانه في القبض. ولو هلك العبد ثم ذهبت عينها فإن أخذها ضمنه نصف قيمتها لأن يوم العبد لما هلك صارت الجارية مضمونة على مشتريها بالقيمة ولأن النقصان إنما حدث بعد فساد الملك فيها لأن الملك قد فسد في الجارية بهلاك العبد والملك الفاسد مضمون على القابض بالقبض لا بالعقد، والأوصاف تفرد بالقبض فيفرد بضمان القبص كما في الرهن والغصب. وأما إذا تعيب بفعل المشتري بأن قطع يدها أو فقأ عينها فهو كما لو تعيب بآفة سماوية في التضمين لأن المشتري جنى على ملكه وجناية المالك على مملوكه هدر فلم يخلف بدلاً فصار كأنه مات بآفة سماوية، وإن تعينت بفعل أجنبي بأن قطع يدها أو وطئها بشبهة فأخذ أرشها وعقرها أو ولدت من غير سيدها؛ فإن كان قبل هلاك العبد لم يكن للعبد إلا قيمتها يوم العقبد لأنه لما هلك العبد فسد البيع في الجارية فوجب على مشتري الجارية ردها للفساد وقد عجز عن ردها حكماً لأنه حدث بعد القبض زيادة منفصلة من الجارية في ملك صحيح، ومثل هذه الزيادة تمنع انفساخ الملك في الأصل، فإذا تعذر فسخ البيع في الجارية صار المشتري عاجزاً عن رده قيمتها .. وإن كان بعد هلاك العبد أخذ الجارية وعقرها وولدها وأرشها إن شاء من المشتري، وإن شاء من الجاني لأن الزيادة المنفصلة لا تمنع انفساخ البيع في ملك فاسد كما لو وقع البيع في الجارية فاسداً في الابتداء ثم حدث منها زيادة منفصلة كان للبائع حق الاسترداد في الأصل فسرى ذلك الحق إلى الزوائد ثم إن شاء ضمن المشتري لأن النقصان لو حدث بآفة سماوية كان له تضمينه فإذا حدث بفعله أولى، فإن شاء ضمن الجاني لأن الجاني صار جانباً على ملكه لإعادة الجارية إلى قديم ملكه بالفسخ. ولو حدث بها عيبان أحدهما قبل هلاك العبد والثاني بعد هلاكه فعلى هذا القياس لأن العيبين لو حدثا قبل هلاك العبد يتخير المأذون حتى لو اختار أخذ الجارية لا يكون له ضمان النقصان، ولو حدثا بعد هلاك العبد متى أخذ الجارية فله تضمين نقصان العيبين جميعاً، فإذا حدث أحدهما قبل هلاكه والآخر بعد هلاكه كان لكل واحد منهما حكم نفسه .
هذا كله إذا تعيبت الجارية في يد مشتريها، وأما إذا حدث فيها زيادة فلا يخلو إما إن كانت الزيادة منفصلة كالولد والأرش، أو كانت متصلة كالسمن والجمال. فإن كانت منفصلة، فإن ولدت قبل هلاك العبد ثم مات العبد ينظر ؛ إن كان الولد قائماً ليس للمأذون
في
أخذ الجارية لأن الزيادة المنفصلة الحادثة بعد القبض في ملك صحيح تمنع انفساخ العقد الأصل، وإن هلك الولد والأرش كان للعبد أن يأخذ الجارية ولا يتبعه بنقصان الولادة والجناية إن شاء وإن شاء ضمنه قيمتها لأن المانع من انفساخ العقد قد ارتفع وهو الزيادة فصارت كأنها لم تكن والنقصان قائم لأن الولادة في بنات آدم سبب النقصان وأنه عيب على كل حال فله أن يضمنه قيمة الجارية. ولو كان مكان الجارية شاة فنتجت في يده قبل هلاك العبد لم يكن للعبد خيار ويأخذ الشاة لأنه لا نقصان لأن الولادة في البهائم ليست بعيب . وإن هلكت الزيادة بفعل أجنبي فهو كما كان الولد قائماً لأن الولد مات وأخلف بدلاً، والفائت إلى خلف كالقائم حكماً وإن هلكت بفعل المشتري بأن أعتق المشتري أو ولد الجارية ثم هلك العبد لم يكن للمأذون على الجارية سبيل لأن الولد يسلم للمشتري من وجه فإن مولى له يرث منه إذا مات ولم يكن له عصبة أقرب منه فيؤدي إلى الربا فلا يجوز فسخ العيب في الجارية فإن مات الولد المعتق وترك ولداً كان للعبد أن يأخذ الجارية إن شاء ولا يتبعه بنقصانها، وكذلك إن ترك ولداً لا يخير ولاؤه المشتري بأن كان المعتق تزوج بأمة لرجل وحدث منها ولد ثم أعتق مولى الأمة الولد لأن المانع من فسخ العقد في الجارية هو الولد وقد زال هذا المانع بلا خلف وهذه الزيادة من خصائص مسائل هذا فيجب حفظها. وكذلك إذا قتل الولد المشتري فله الخيار بين الفسخ والتضمين وهذا لا يشكل على الرواية التي قال بأن الولادة عيب لازم في بنات آدم وذلك لأن القتل بمنزلة الموت لأن المقتول ميت بأجله، ولو مات الولد في يد مشتري الجارية يتخير المأذون فكذا هذا وإنما يشكل على الرواية التي قال بأن الولادة ليست بعيب إذا لم توجب نقصاناً لأن الولد مات ولم يخلف بدلاً لأن المشتري جنى على ملكه الصحيح وجناية المالك على ملكه هدر فصار كما لو مات الولد حتف أنفه . والجواب عنه أن الولد مات وأخلف بدلاً من دمه لأن جناية المشتري على الولد إنما تكون ملاقياً ملكه ما دام ملكه في الجارية متقرراً، فأما إذا انفسخ ملكه في الجارية بأن أخذ الجارية ولم يضمنه النقصان كانت الجناية على الولد ملاقياً ملك المأذون من وجه لأن الولد تبع
Ownership of produce because according to him (Abu Hanifa) the earning belongs to whom the original ownership belongs, and according to them (the two) the earning whenever it occurs before the establishment of ownership follows the origin, unlike that issue because when the earning occurred in the hand of the seller, he did not have the right of ownership, which is the right of recovery, until he assigns the right to the earning. And the difference between the seller and the ma'dhun, even though each of them acquires earning through bad ownership, is that the slave is not eligible for charity so he does not give charity, while the seller is eligible so he gives charity. He bought from a slave with a fasid (defective) sale then sold it to the mudarib of the slave: it is permissible and is not a rescission of the first sale, just as if he sold to a stranger, so the deficiency is not established by doubt. And if he sold a slave girl for a slave and delivered the slave girl but did not receive the slave until a defect occurred in her, then it is either before the destruction of the slave or after, and each aspect does not lack three possibilities: either the defect is by a divine affliction, or by the act of the buyer, or by the act of an outsider. As for if a defect occurs by a divine affliction such as her eye going blind then the slave dies: the ma'dhun has the option: if he wishes he may take his slave girl and not follow her with compensation for her deficiency, and if he wishes he may claim the value of his slave girl on the day he delivered her because when the slave girl was delivered she was guaranteed by the slave not by the value, because the deficiency is valid for the buyer, and when ownership is valid it is guaranteed upon the taker as a guarantee occurring in the contract, which is the price, and descriptions are not isolated by contract so they are not isolated by the guarantee of the contract because it became obligatory upon the buyer of the slave girl to return the slave girl as he received it free from defect, and upon him to return its value on the day he received it because it entered his guarantee upon taking possession. And if the slave dies then her eye goes blind: if he takes her he claims half its value because when the slave died the slave girl became guaranteed upon its buyer by value, and because the deficiency occurred after the corruption of ownership in her, because ownership in the slave girl became corrupted by the death of the slave, and corrupt ownership is guaranteed upon the taker by possession not by contract, and descriptions are isolated by possession so they are isolated by the guarantee of possession, as in pledge and usurpation. And as for if it becomes defective by the act of the buyer such as cutting her hand or gouging her eye: it is like a defect by a divine affliction in terms of compensation because the buyer assaulted his own property and the assault of the owner on his property is null, so no replacement remains, so it is as if she died by a divine affliction. And if it becomes defective by the act of an outsider such as cutting her hand or having intercourse with her under suspicion, and he takes her indemnity (arsh) and her dower (aqr), or she gives birth from other than her owner: if this was before the destruction of the slave, the slave (seller) does not have except her value on the day of the contract, because when the slave died the sale in the slave girl became fasid, so it became obligatory upon the buyer of the slave girl to return her due to corruption, and he is legally incapable of returning her because after possession a separate increase from the slave girl occurred in valid ownership, and such an increase prevents the dissolution of ownership in the origin. So when the rescission of the sale in the slave girl is impossible, the buyer becomes incapable of returning her value... And if it was after the destruction of the slave, he (the ma'dhun) takes the slave girl and her dower, her offspring, and her indemnity if he wishes from the buyer, and if he wishes from the assailant, because a separate increase does not prevent the dissolution of sale in corrupt ownership, just as if the sale in the slave girl was fasid initially then a separate increase occurred from her: the seller has the right of recovery in the origin, so that right extends to the increments. Then if he wishes he claims compensation from the buyer because if the deficiency had occurred by a divine affliction he would have had the right to claim it, so if it occurs by his act it is more appropriate. If he wishes he claims from the assailant because the assailant became an assault on his ownership in order to return the slave girl to its previous ownership by rescission. And if two defects occur, one before the destruction of the slave and the second after his destruction: according to this analogy, because if both defects occur before the destruction of the slave the ma'dhun has the option; even if he chooses to take the slave girl he does not have compensation for the deficiency, and if they occur after the destruction of the slave, whenever he takes the slave girl he has the right to claim compensation for both defects, so if one occurs before his destruction and the other after, each has its own ruling. All this if the slave girl becomes defective in the hand of her buyer. As for if an increase occurs in her: either the increase is separate like offspring and indemnity, or connected like fat and beauty. If it is separate: if she gives birth before the destruction of the slave then the slave dies, it is considered: if the offspring is existing, the ma'dhun has no way to take the slave girl because the separate increase occurring after possession in valid ownership prevents the dissolution of the original contract. And if the offspring and indemnity perish, the slave (seller) may take the slave girl and does not follow her with compensation for the deficiency of birth and the assault if he wishes, and if he wishes he claims its value because the obstacle to the dissolution of the contract has been removed, which is the increase, so it becomes as if it never was, while the deficiency remains because childbirth in daughters of Adam is a cause of deficiency and it is a defect in all cases, so he has the right to claim the value of the slave girl. And if the item were a sheep and it gave birth in his hand before the destruction of the slave, the slave does not have an option and he takes the sheep because there is no deficiency, because birth in animals is not a defect. And if the increase perishes by the act of an outsider, it is as if the offspring existed because the offspring died and left a replacement, and what is lost to a replacement is like existing in legal effect. And if it perishes by the act of the buyer, such as the buyer emancipating the offspring of the slave girl, then the slave dies: the ma'dhun has no way to the slave girl because the offspring becomes the property of the buyer in a sense, for he has a patron (mawla) who inherits from him when he dies and he has no closer agnates, and this leads to riba (usury), so rescission due to defect in the slave girl is not permitted. If the emancipated offspring dies and leaves a child, the slave has the option to take the slave girl if he wishes and does not follow her with compensation for her deficiency. Similarly, if he leaves a child whose patronage does not go to the buyer, such as if the emancipated one married a slave woman of a man and she gave birth to a child, then the master of the slave woman emancipates the child, because the obstacle to rescission of the contract in the slave girl is the offspring, and this obstacle has been removed without replacement. And this increase is from the specific issues of this section, so it must be memorized. And similarly, if the buyer kills the offspring, he has the option between rescission and claiming compensation. This is not problematic according to the riwaya that states childbirth is an inherent defect in daughters of Adam, because killing is like death, since the killed dies by his appointed term. And if the offspring dies in the hand of the buyer of the slave girl, the ma'dhun has the option, so likewise here. It is only problematic according to the riwaya that states childbirth is not a defect if it does not cause a deficiency, because the offspring died and left no replacement, since the buyer assaulted his own valid ownership and the assault of the owner on his property is null, so it becomes as if the offspring died naturally. The answer to this is that the offspring died and left a replacement from its blood money, because the buyer's assault on the offspring only meets his ownership as long as his ownership in the slave girl is established. But if his ownership in the slave girl is dissolved by his taking the slave girl and not claiming compensation for the deficiency, then the assault on the offspring meets the ownership of the ma'dhun in a sense, because the offspring is a follower.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is a detailed expansion (sharh) on rulings concerning the ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) in cases of fasid (defective) sales, particularly when a slave girl is sold for a male slave and defects or increases occur. The commentator first addresses a minor ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding ownership of earnings (kisb): Abu Hanifa holds that earnings belong to the owner of the asset, while the Sahibayn say that if the earning occurs before ownership is fully established, it follows the origin. The difference between a seller and a ma'dhun is that the seller can give charity from ill-gotten gains, but a slave cannot. 

The main discussion begins with a ruling: if a ma'dhun buys from a slave in a fasid sale and then sells to the slave's mudarib (partner), it is valid and does not rescind the first sale. 

The core of the passage analyzes a scenario where a ma'dhun sells a slave girl (jariya) for a male slave (abd). The buyer takes possession of the jariya but not the abd until a defect appears in the jariya. The ruling depends on the timing of the defect relative to the death of the abd, and the cause of the defect: divine affliction (sama'iyya), act of the buyer, or act of an outsider. 

If the defect occurs before the abd dies, the ma'dhun has an option: take back the jariya without compensation for the defect, or claim its value on the day of delivery. If the defect occurs after the abd dies, the ma'dhun can take the jariya and claim half its value if the defect is from divine affliction. The reasoning hinges on whether the jariya was guaranteed by the abd (before his death) or by value (after his death) due to the corruption of ownership. 

If the buyer causes the defect, it is treated like a divine affliction because the buyer's assault on his own property is legally void (hadar). If an outsider causes the defect, the ma'dhun can claim from the buyer or the assailant, with different rules depending on whether the defect occurs before or after the abd's death. 

The passage then discusses increases (ziyada) such as offspring. Separate increases (e.g., birth) before the abd's death prevent rescission; after the abd's death, they do not. The rulings also consider the type of animal (sheep vs human) and whether the increase perishes or is disposed of by the buyer (e.g., emancipation). 

Finally, the commentator addresses two riwayas (transmissions) about whether childbirth is a defect. One says it is always a defect; the other says it is not if it does not cause a decrease. The answer is that the offspring's death leaves a replacement (blood money) from the buyer's assault, so the ma'dhun can still claim.

Key Terms
الزيادة المنفصلة (al-ziyada al-munfasila): A separate increase that is independent of the original item, such as offspring or indemnity.
الزيادة المتصلة (al-ziyada al-muttasila): A connected increase that is not independent, such as fattening or beauty.
الرواية (al-riwaya): A transmission of an opinion from the Hanafi founding imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) via different chains.
الأرش (al-arsh): Indemnity or compensation for a defect or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the resolution provided for the second riwaya, what allows the ma'dhun to still have an option when the buyer kills the offspring?
A) The offspring is considered a defect regardless.
B) The offspring dies and leaves a replacement in the form of blood money.
C) The buyer's act is treated as a divine affliction.
D) The ma'dhun never has an option in this case.

Key Takeaway
Separate increases in valid ownership block rescission, but in corrupt ownership they do not; the childbirth defect debate is resolved by the principle of replacement.

Timing of Defects and Compensation for the Ma'dhun
Ruling on defect timing in sale by ma'dhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for defects occurring before and after the slave's death to determine the ma'dhun's right to compensation.

Core Idea
The timing of a defect relative to the slave's death determines whether the ma'dhun can claim compensation.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario where a ma'dhun slave purchases a female slave who develops two defects at different times — one before the death of the slave buyer and one after. The Hanafi school has distinct rules for defects based on when they occur, because the death of the slave retroactively voids the sale. Understanding this timing is crucial for correctly applying the option and compensation rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timing of each defect
Determine when each defect occurred relative to the slave's death: before or after.
The timing is the sole factor affecting compensation.
2
Apply the pre-death rule
If a defect occurred before the slave's death, the ma'dhun has the option to rescind the contract or keep the female slave without claiming compensation for that defect.
The pre-death defect is considered part of the contract's risk.
3
Apply the post-death rule
If a defect occurred after the slave's death, the ma'dhun can claim compensation for the full deficiency caused by that defect when he takes the female slave.
Post-death defects are compensable because the contract is retroactively voided.
4
Synthesize the overall ruling
Combine the rulings for each defect based on its timing. The ma'dhun's rights are determined independently for each defect.
No single ruling applies to both defects; each is judged separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حدث بها عيبان أحدهما قبل هلاك العبد والثاني بعد هلاكه فعلى هذا القياس لأن العيبين لو حدثا قبل هلاك العبد يتخير المأذون حتى لو اختار أخذ الجارية لا يكون له ضمان النقصان، ولو حدثا بعد هلاك العبد متى أخذ الجارية فله تضمين نقصان العيبين جميعاً، فإذا حدث أحدهما قبل هلاكه والآخر بعد هلاكه كان لكل واحد منهما حكم نفسه .
And if two defects occur, one before the destruction of the slave and the second after his destruction, then according to this analogy: because if both defects occurred before the destruction of the slave, the ma'dhun has the option; even if he chooses to take the female slave, he does not have guarantee of the deficiency. And if both occurred after the destruction of the slave, whenever he takes the female slave, he has the right to claim compensation for the deficiency of both defects. So if one occurs before his destruction and the other after his destruction, each has its own ruling.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that extends the previous analogy to a scenario where the female slave develops two defects: one before the death of the slave (the buyer) and one after. The ma'dhun slave who bought the female slave is the subject. The ruling: each defect is treated according to the time it occurred. If both defects occurred before the slave's death, the ma'dhun has the option to take the female slave without compensation for the deficiency, because the contract is still valid. If both occurred after death, he can take her with compensation for both defects. If one occurred before and one after, each is judged by its own time: the pre-death defect gives no compensation, the post-death defect gives compensation. This follows the general principle that the slave's death retroactively invalidates the sale, making defects after death compensable.

Key Terms
عيب ('ayb): A defect in the female slave that reduces her value or desirability.
هلاك (halak): Destruction or death of the slave (the buyer in this context).
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a female slave has defect A before the slave's death and defect B after, what is the ma'dhun's right regarding compensation?
A) He can claim compensation for both defects
B) He cannot claim compensation for either defect
C) He can claim compensation only for defect A
D) He can claim compensation only for defect B

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's right to compensation for defects in the purchased female slave depends on whether the defect occurred before or after the slave's death.

Separate Increase and Its Effect on the Option of Defect
Effect of separate increase on option for defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a separate increase in a sold item prevents rescission due to defect, and determine when rescission becomes possible after the increase perishes.

Core Idea
A separate increase blocks rescission; but if it perishes, the impediment lifts and the option returns.

In the previous lesson we discussed defects in the sold item. But what if the female slave gives birth or receives a dowry after being purchased? This lesson explores how such a 'separate increase' affects the right to rescind the contract. The general rule: as long as the increase exists, rescission is blocked; once it disappears, the option is reinstated. However, the details depend on how the increase perishes and whether the deficiency remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the ma'dhun's option to rescind a sale due to a defect in the female slave. The ruling depends on the presence of a separate increase.
The increase must be separate (e.g., child, arsh) as opposed to connected (e.g., fatness).
2
Apply the general rule for separate increase
If the separate increase exists at the time of the slave's death, rescission is blocked because the increase is a new independent right. If the increase perishes, the impediment is removed.
The increase must have occurred after valid possession (qabd).
3
Distinguish cases based on how the increase perishes
Natural death, act of a third party, or act of the buyer each have different consequences. Also consider animal births (no deficiency).
For a third party, the substitute (e.g., blood money) keeps the impediment in place.
4
Analyze the buyer's act of emancipating or killing the child
Emancipation creates a new obstacle (inheritence leading to riba), preventing rescission. Killing the child triggers an intra-Hanafi dispute resolved by qiyas.
The text provides a detailed response to the narration that birth is not a defect.
5
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
The final ruling gives the ma'dhun the option: absolve the buyer of child's value and take the female slave, or claim the value of the female slave.
This is based on the principle that the child is a follow-up (taba) to the female slave.
