الولادة والجناية إن شاء وإن شاء ضمنه قيمتها لأن المانع من انفساخ العقد قد ارتفع وهو الزيادة فصارت كأنها لم تكن والنقصان قائم لأن الولادة في بنات آدم سبب النقصان وأنه عيب على كل حال فله أن يضمنه قيمة الجارية. ولو كان مكان الجارية شاة فنتجت في يده قبل هلاك العبد لم يكن للعبد خيار ويأخذ الشاة لأنه لا نقصان لأن الولادة في البهائم ليست بعيب . وإن هلكت الزيادة بفعل أجنبي فهو كما كان الولد قائماً لأن الولد مات وأخلف بدلاً، والفائت إلى خلف كالقائم حكماً وإن هلكت بفعل المشتري بأن أعتق المشتري أو ولد الجارية ثم هلك العبد لم يكن للمأذون على الجارية سبيل لأن الولد يسلم للمشتري من وجه فإن مولى له يرث منه إذا مات ولم يكن له عصبة أقرب منه فيؤدي إلى الربا فلا يجوز فسخ العيب في الجارية فإن مات الولد المعتق وترك ولداً كان للعبد أن يأخذ الجارية إن شاء ولا يتبعه بنقصانها، وكذلك إن ترك ولداً لا يخير ولاؤه المشتري بأن كان المعتق تزوج بأمة لرجل وحدث منها ولد ثم أعتق مولى الأمة الولد لأن المانع من فسخ العقد في الجارية هو الولد وقد زال هذا المانع بلا خلف وهذه الزيادة من خصائص مسائل هذا فيجب حفظها. وكذلك إذا قتل الولد المشتري فله الخيار بين الفسخ والتضمين وهذا لا يشكل على الرواية التي قال بأن الولادة عيب لازم في بنات آدم وذلك لأن القتل بمنزلة الموت لأن المقتول ميت بأجله، ولو مات الولد في يد مشتري الجارية يتخير المأذون فكذا هذا وإنما يشكل على الرواية التي قال بأن الولادة ليست بعيب إذا لم توجب نقصاناً لأن الولد مات ولم يخلف بدلاً لأن المشتري جنى على ملكه الصحيح وجناية المالك على ملكه هدر فصار كما لو مات الولد حتف أنفه . والجواب عنه أن الولد مات وأخلف بدلاً من دمه لأن جناية المشتري على الولد إنما تكون ملاقياً ملكه ما دام ملكه في الجارية متقرراً، فأما إذا انفسخ ملكه في الجارية بأن أخذ الجارية ولم يضمنه النقصان كانت الجناية على الولد ملاقياً ملك المأذون من وجه لأن الولد تبع للجارية لأنه متولد ومتفرع عنها، ولهذا ملك بسبب ملك الجارية وانفساخ الملك في الأصل يوجب انفساخ الملك في التبع فصار جانياً على ملك المأذون على هذا الاعتبار فيضمن قيمة الولد من وجه فصح أن الولد مات وأخلف بدلاً من وجه فيتخير، وإن شاء أبرأه عن قيمة الولد ويأخذ الجارية، وإن شاء لم يبره وضمنه قيمة الجارية.
All this is if the female slave becomes defective in the hand of her purchaser. As for if an increase occurs in her, then it is either separate like a child or arsh, or connected like fatness and beauty. If it is separate, and she gives birth before the destruction of the slave and then the slave dies, we look: if the child is existing, the ma'dhun does not have the right to take the female slave because the separate increase occurring after possession in valid ownership prevents the rescission of the original contract. And if the child and arsh perish, then the slave has the right to take the female slave and does not follow it with compensation for the deficiency of birth and injury if he wishes, and if he wishes he may claim its value because the impediment to rescission—which was the increase—has been removed, so it becomes as if it never existed while the deficiency remains—because birth in daughters of Adam is a cause of deficiency and is a defect in any case—so he may claim the value of the female slave. And if instead of a female slave there was a sheep, and it gave birth in his hand before the destruction of the slave, then the slave does not have an option and he takes the sheep because there is no deficiency, since birth in animals is not a defect. And if the increase perishes by the act of a third party, then it is as if the child were existing because the child died and left a substitute, and that which ceases into a substitute is like existing in legal effect. And if it perishes by the act of the purchaser—such as the purchaser emancipates the child of the female slave and then the slave dies—then the ma'dhun has no way to the female slave because the child is secured to the purchaser from one perspective: for his master inherits from him if he dies and he has no closer agnate, leading to riba, so the rescission due to defect in the female slave is not permissible. Then if the emancipated child dies and leaves a child of his own, the slave may take the female slave if he wishes and does not follow it with compensation for her deficiency. Similarly, if it leaves a child whose patronage does not go to the purchaser—such as if the deceased married a female slave of a man and she gave birth to a child, then the master of the female slave emancipated the child—because the impediment to rescission of the contract regarding the female slave is the child, and this impediment has been removed without a substitute. And this increase is among the peculiarities of these issues, so they must be memorized. Similarly, if the purchaser kills the child, then he has the option between rescission and claiming compensation. This is not problematic according to the narration that says birth is a necessary defect in daughters of Adam, because killing is equivalent to death, since the slain dies at his appointed time. And if the child died in the hand of the purchaser of the female slave, the ma'dhun has the option, so too this. It is only problematic according to the narration that says birth is not a defect if it does not cause deficiency, because the child died and left no substitute, since the purchaser acted against his own valid ownership, and the owner's injury upon his own property is of no consequence, so it is as if the child died a natural death. The answer to this is that the child died and left a substitute from his blood, because the purchaser's injury upon the child only falls upon his ownership as long as his ownership in the female slave is established. But when his ownership in the female slave is rescinded—such as by taking the female slave without claiming compensation for the deficiency—then the injury upon the child falls upon the ownership of the ma'dhun from one perspective, because the child is a follow-up to the female slave, being generated and branched from her. For this reason, he owned due to ownership of the female slave, and rescission of ownership in the original necessitates rescission of ownership in the follow-up. So he becomes an aggressor upon the ownership of the ma'dhun from this consideration, and thus he guarantees the value of the child from one perspective. So it is established that the child died and left a substitute from one perspective, and so he has the option: if he wishes, he may absolve him of the value of the child and take the female slave; if he wishes, he does not absolve him and claims the value of the female slave.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the effect of a separate increase (such as a child or arsh/dowry) in the female slave when the slave (buyer) dies. The key principle: a separate increase occurring after valid possession prevents the rescission of the sale due to defect, because the increase is an independent right. However, if the increase perishes, the impediment is removed and rescission becomes possible. The text distinguishes between increase perishing by natural causes, by a third party, or by the buyer's own act. Special cases: for a sheep giving birth, there is no deficiency, so the buyer may take the sheep. If the buyer emancipates the child and then the slave dies, the ma'dhun cannot take the female slave because the child is now free and his inheritance goes to his master, which could lead to riba; but if the freed child dies leaving his own child, the impediment is removed. If the buyer kills the child, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to one narration, killing is like death and the buyer has the option; according to another, killing by the owner is without compensation, but the Hanafi response argues that the child is a follow-up to the female slave, so the buyer becomes liable to the ma'dhun. Thus the correct ruling gives the ma'dhun the option to either take the female slave and absolve the buyer of the child's value, or claim the value of the female slave without absolving. This highlights the complexity of rights when ownership is retroactively invalidated.

Key Terms
زيادة منفصلة (ziyada munfasila): A separate increase that is not physically attached, such as a child or arsh (dowry).
أرش (arsh): A compensation paid for a defect or injury, or a dowry in the context of marriage.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, here due to a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where the buyer kills the child of the female slave, what is the correct Hanafi ruling according to the text?
A) The buyer has no option because he killed his own property
B) The buyer has the option because killing is like death and the child left a substitute
C) The buyer must pay the value of the child and also take the female slave
D) The sale is void automatically

Key Takeaway
A separate increase occurring after valid possession prevents rescission; but if the increase perishes and a deficiency remains, the option returns depending on how it perished.

Connected Increase in a Female Slave: Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on connected increase in a slave sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on connected increase in a female slave and explain why Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf disagreed on the timing of the return.

Core Idea
A connected increase does not prevent rescission, but a reported view restricts return until after the slave's death.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq deals with a classic Hanafi quandary: when a ma'dhun (permitted slave) purchases a female slave, and she increases in beauty (a connected increase), can the ma'dhun reclaim her if the male slave (the object of exchange) dies? The general ruling allows return with the increase, but a reported view from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf restricts return until after death, analogizing to dower. We will trace the reasoning and the critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the disputed scenario
State the general rule: the ma'dhun may take the female slave with her connected increase. The disputed scenario is whether this is allowed before the male slave's death.
2
Locate the opposing view
According to a report, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say it is not permissible to demand return before the death of the male slave.
The text says 'قيل عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف' (and it is said at Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
3
Analyze the opposing proof (qiyas)
They analogize to the dower case: if the dower increases and then divorce occurs before consummation, the husband cannot return half without the wife's consent.
The analogy is that the increase (beauty) should prevent return, just as increase in dower prevents the husband from reclaiming half.
4
Examine the Hanafi response (distinction)
The text distinguishes: in the dower case, the nullification of the woman's right to the increase is intentional (by the husband's divorce). Here, the nullification is unintentional (by the slave's death).
The difference is that the ma'dhun did not cause the death, whereas the husband caused the divorce.
5
Determine the final position
The most correct view is that this matter is subject to disagreement (al-ashah anna hadha ala al-khilaf). The practical consequence: according to the reported view, the ma'dhun may only reclaim after death; according to the general view, he may reclaim before death.
The text later mentions that if the slave develops a defect and is returned, the return is intentional and thus connected increase may prevent rescission in that case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كانت الزيادة متصلة بأن ازدادت الجارية حسناً وجمالاً أو ذهب البياض الذي في عينها قبل هلاك العبد أو بعده أخذها بزيادتها وقيل عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله تعالى لا يجوز استردادها قبل هلاك الغلام لما عرف من اختلافهم في الصداق زاد في يده بعد القبض ثم طلقها قبل الدخول لا يكون للزوج رد نصف الصداق إلا برضا المرأة عندهما، وقيل هذا قولهم جميعاً، فهما فرقا بين هذه المسألة ومسألة الصداق. والفرق أن في الصداق حق المرأة في الزيادة ولو بطل فإنما يبطل قصداً بإيقاع الزوج بالطلاق وهو لا يملك إبطال حقها قصداً، فأما حق مشتري الجارية في الزيادة لو بطل فإنما يبطل حكماً لا بقصد المأذون لأن بطلان حقه في الزيادة مضاف إلى موت الغلام وموت الغلام ما كان بصنع المأذون وقد ثبت حكماً للشيء وضرورة ثبوته وإن كان لا يثبت قصداً والأصح أن هذا على الخلاف لأنه ذكر بعد هذا أن العبد لو لم يمت لكن حدث به عيب فرد العيب كان للمأذون أن يسترد الجارية وإن كانت الزيادة المنفصلة حدثت قبل الرد فحق الرد وحق مشتري الجارية في الزيادة، هذا لو بطل إنما يبطل قصداً لأنه يبطل برد العبد ورد العبد كان بقصده، أن الرد بخيار الرؤية والرد بالعيب قبل القبض بمنزلة الموت من حيث منع فسخ العقد لأن العقد ينفسخ بهذا الرد كما ينفسخ بموت العبد قبل القبض.
As for if the increase is connected—such as the female slave becomes better in appearance and beauty, or the whiteness in her eye disappears—whether before or after the destruction of the slave, he may take her with her increase. And it is said that according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, it is not permissible to demand her return before the destruction of the boy (the slave), due to what is known of their disagreement regarding the dower: if it increased in his hand after possession and then he divorced her before consummation, the husband does not have the right to return half of the dower except with the woman's consent according to them. And it is said that this is the saying of all of them. So they distinguished between this issue and the issue of dower. The difference is that in the dower, the woman has a right to the increase, and if it is nullified, it is nullified intentionally by the husband's divorce, and he does not have the power to intentionally nullify her right. As for the right of the purchaser of the female slave to the increase, if it is nullified, it is nullified by operation of law, not by the intent of the ma'dhun, because the nullification of his right to the increase is attributed to the death of the boy, and the death of the boy was not by the action of the ma'dhun. And it has been established legally that a thing and the necessity of its establishment, even if it is not established intentionally, and the most correct is that this is according to the disagreement, because it is mentioned after this that if the slave did not die but a defect occurred in him and he returned the defect, then the ma'dhun may demand the return of the female slave even if the separate increase occurred before the return—so the right of return and the right of the purchaser of the female slave to the increase—if this is nullified, it is nullified intentionally because it is nullified by the return of the slave, and the return of the slave was by his intent. That the return by option of sight and return due to defect before possession are equivalent to death in terms of preventing rescission of the contract, because the contract is rescinded by this return just as it is rescinded by the death of the slave before possession.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage addresses connected increase (such as beauty) in the female slave. The general ruling: whether the increase occurs before or after the slave's death, the ma'dhun may take the female slave with her increase; the connected increase does not prevent rescission. However, there is a reported disagreement from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding return before the slave's death: they analogize to the case of dower where, if the dower increases and then the husband divorces before consummation, he cannot return half the dower without the woman's consent. The text explains the distinguishing factor: in the dower case, the nullification of the woman's right to the increase is intentional (by the husband's divorce), whereas in this case, the nullification of the purchaser's right to the increase is unintentional (due to death). The correct position is that there is still a disagreement (al-khilaf), because later it is mentioned that if the slave does not die but develops a defect and the buyer returns the defective slave, the ma'dhun can still reclaim the female slave even if separate increase occurred before the return—but that would be an intentional nullification by the buyer's return. The text notes that return due to option of sight or defect before possession is equivalent to death in effect. Thus the ruling for connected increase is nuanced: the ma'dhun's right to reclaim the female slave with her increase is affirmed, but there is disagreement on whether that right exists before the slave's death, with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf seemingly restricting it.

Key Terms
زيادة متصلة (ziyada muttasila): A connected increase that is physically attached, such as beauty, fatness, or recovery from a defect.
صداق (sadaq): The dower given by the husband to the wife in marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reported view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, when is it not permissible to demand the return of the female slave with connected increase?
A) Before the slave's death
B) After the slave's death
C) Never
D) Only if the increase is significant

Key Takeaway
Connected increase does not prevent rescission in Hanafi law, but an intra-school disagreement exists regarding the timing of the return before the counter-value's death.

Khiyar al-Shart: Stipulated Option in Sales by the Ma'dhun
Ruling on khiyar al-shart and emancipation in a ma'dhun's sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that khiyar al-shart in one countervalue implies the same option in the other and determine when emancipation of a female slave is valid or invalid in such sales.

Core Idea
Stipulating an option in one item of a sale binds the other; rescission cannot be partial.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario: a ma'dhun slave sells another slave with a three-day option, and the purchased item includes a female slave. The text explores how the option ties the two countervalues together and affects the buyer's right to emancipate. Understanding this principle is essential for correctly applying khiyar al-shart in multi-item sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The ma'dhun sells a slave with a three-day stipulated option. The purchased item includes a female slave. Determine the legal effects on the female slave when the buyer takes possession, or when defects arise, or when mutual rescission occurs.
The ma'dhun is a slave permitted to trade.
2
Apply the key principle
Note that stipulating an option in one countervalue (the slave sold) is automatically a stipulation of option in the other countervalue (the female slave). Because the option is for rescission, and rescission cannot be partial—both items must be returned together.
This is derived via qiyas: the illa is the inseparability of rescission.
3
Analyze possession and emancipation scenarios
If the buyer emancipates the female slave after possession but before the slave's death, the emancipation is invalid because the seller still has an option. If before possession and before the slave's death, it is valid (sale is valid). If after the slave's death, the sale becomes corrupt (fasad) and does not confer ownership before possession, so emancipation is invalid.
Corrupt sale (bay' fasid) before possession does not transfer ownership.
4
Examine defect returns and rescission
If the buyer discovers a defect in the purchased slave and returns it (by judgment, consent, or option), or if they mutually rescind (taqayala), the female slave leaves the buyer's ownership. Any subsequent emancipation is invalid because he no longer owns her.
Return due to defect or mutual rescission nullifies the sale ab initio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان العبد المأذون باع بالخيار ثلاثة أيام في الذي اشتراه وولدت الجارية في يد المشتري منه أو من غيره أو قطع يدها، فإن رد العبد بخياره أخذ الجارية وأرشها وعقرها وولدها لأن اشتراط الخيار في العبد اشتراط الخيار في الجارية لأن الخيار إنما يشترط للفسخ ولا يمكنه فسخ في أحد البدلين دون الآخر فيكون اشتراط الخيار في أحد البدلين اشتراطاً للخيار في الآخر، ولهذا لو أعتق مشتري الجارية بعد القبض لا ينفذ عتقها لأن للبائع خيار شرط في الجارية والمشتري متى قبض المتشري وللبائع فيه خيار شرط يكون المشتري مضموناً عليه بالقيمة، ولو لم يقبض الجارية حتى أعتقها قبل هلاك العبد جاز عتقه، فإن أعتقها بعد هلاك العبد لم يجز عتقه لأن قبل هلاك العبد عتق ملك نفسه لأن البيع صحيح في الجارية وبعد هلاك العبد فسد البيع في الجارية والبيع الفاسد قبل القبض لا يفيد الملك. ولو قبض الجارية ووجد المأذون بالعبد المبيع عيباً قبل القبض أو بعده فرده بقضاء أو رضا أو خيار رؤية أو شرط ثم أعتق الجارية لم يجز عتقه، وكذا لو تقايلا انفسخ العيب من كل وجه فزالت الجارية عن ملكه فصار معتقاً ما لم يملك فإذا بهلاك العبد لا ينفسخ وإنما يفسد، فمتى وبين العقد.
And if the ma'dhun slave sold with an option of three days regarding what he purchased, and the female slave gave birth in the hand of the purchaser from him or from another, or (the purchaser) cut off her hand, then if the slave returns (the defective item) by his option, he takes the female slave, her arsh, her dower, and her child, because stipulating the option in the slave is a stipulation of the option in the female slave, because the option is only stipulated for rescission, and he cannot rescind one of the two countervalues without the other; thus stipulating the option in one countervalue is a stipulation of the option in the other. For this reason, if the purchaser of the female slave emancipates her after possession, his emancipation is not effective because the seller has a stipulated option in the female slave, and whenever the purchaser takes possession of the purchased item while the seller has a stipulated option, the purchaser is held liable for the value. And if he did not take possession of the female slave until he emancipated her before the death of the slave, his emancipation is valid. But if he emancipates her after the death of the slave, his emancipation is not valid, because before the death of the slave, he emancipated his own property—since the sale is valid regarding the female slave—and after the death of the slave, the sale becomes corrupt, and a corrupt sale before possession does not confer ownership. And if he took possession of the female slave and the ma'dhun found a defect in the sold slave before or after possession, and he returned him by judgment or consent or option of sight or condition, and then he emancipated the female slave, his emancipation is not valid. Similarly, if they mutually rescinded (taqayala), the contract is rescinded from every aspect, so the female slave leaves his ownership, and he becomes an emancipator of what he does not own. So when the destruction of the slave does not rescind but rather corrupts, then when and between the contract? (text cut off).
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where the ma'dhun slave sells something with a condition of option (khiyar al-shart) for three days, and the item he purchased includes a female slave that gives birth or is injured in the buyer's hand. The key principle: stipulating an option in one item of the exchange (e.g., the slave sold) is considered a stipulation of the option in the other item (the female slave), because the option is for rescission and cannot be exercised partially. Therefore, if the ma'dhun exercises his option to return the defective item (the slave), he also retrieves the female slave along with any increase (child, arsh, dower). The passage then addresses emancipation: if the buyer emancipates the female slave after taking possession, the emancipation is not valid because the seller still has a stipulated option. However, if the buyer emancipates before taking possession but before the slave's death, it is valid because the sale is valid. After the slave's death, the sale becomes corrupt (fasad), and a corrupt sale before possession does not transfer ownership, so emancipation is invalid. Next, if the buyer finds a defect in the slave he purchased and returns it (by judgment, consent, or option), then his subsequent emancipation of the female slave is invalid because ownership has been revoked. Similarly, if they mutually agree to cancel the sale, the female slave leaves his ownership. The final part is cut off but mentions that destruction of the slave does not rescind but rather corrupts the contract.

Key Terms
خيار شرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulated option allowing one party to rescind the sale within a specified period.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A corrupt sale, which is invalid but may transfer ownership after possession under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the buyer's emancipation of the female slave valid?
A) After possession and before the slave's death
B) Before possession and after the slave's death
C) Before possession and before the slave's death
D) After the slave's death regardless of possession

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-shart in one item of an exchange extends to all items because rescission cannot be partial; thus, full ownership is suspended until the option period ends.

Authorized Slave's Right to Appoint Agents
Commentary on ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an authorized slave appointing agents, identify the conditions under which such agency is valid, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on set-off when the agent sells to his own debtor.

Core Idea
An authorized slave may appoint agents for trade; set-off by a slave-agent is valid only according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling of the authorized slave appointing agents for sale and purchase. The author, Ibn Nujaym, expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq and clarifies that the permissibility is based on the customary practice of merchants (istihsan). The ruling is absolute, but practical nuances arise when the slave has debts or when the appointed agent is a creditor. A key intra-Hanafi dispute concerns whether the agent can effect a set-off when selling to his own debtor. By the end of this lesson, you will grasp the scope of the slave's agency and the preferred position in the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
State the rule: The authorized slave may appoint agents for sale and purchase. This is permissible by istihsan based on merchant custom.
The ruling is absolute (applicable whether the slave has debt or not).
2
Note the conditions and preferences
Identify that if the slave has no debt, it is preferable to appoint the master rather than the creditor. Also, the agent can be free or slave, and the sale can be for cash or credit.
The master is the principal administrator (asil fil-tasarruf).
3
Locate the proof
The proof for permissibility is istihsan supported by the practice of merchants (sani' al-tujjar). No explicit text is cited.
Istihsan is a valid source in Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the set-off dispute
If the appointed slave-agent sells to a person who owes the agent a debt, does it become a set-off? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes; Abu Yusuf disagrees. Understand the reasoning: the agent has the power to discharge the buyer from the price.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if set-off is not recognized, the buyer must still pay the full price to the principal.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn) is the relied-upon view in the school, as confirmed in al-Hidayah.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويوكل بهما) أي يجوز أن يوكل بالبيع والشراء لأنهما من توابع الإجارة فلعله لا يتمكن من مباشرة الكل فيحتاج إلى المعين وأطلق في قوله يوكل فشمل ما إذا كان عليه دين أو لا، كان الدين مستغرقاً أو لا، وكل المولى أو غريم العبد مع أن الظاهر إذا لم يكن عليه دين توكيل المولى لأن المولى أصيل في التصرف ولا ينفذ توكيل غريم العبد لأنه ربما يقبض لنفسه فيتضرر البقية، فلو قال ويوكل غير غريم ومولى حيث لا دين لكان أولى. قال في المبسوط : مسائل توكيل المأذون على وجوه : أحدها في توكيل المأذون الثاني في توكيل غريم مولاه بالخصومة والثالث في توكيل الغريم عبداً لمولى المأذون في قبض ما على المولى من الدين. الأول وللعبد أن يوكل غيره بالبيع والشراء بنقد أو نسيئة لأنه من صنيع التجار، وإن وكل عبداً مأذوناً حراً ببيع متاعه فباعه من رجل له على المأمور دين صار قصاصاً عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف، لأن عندهما الوكيل يملك إبراء المشتري عن الثمن وعنده لا. والوكيل مع الموكل إذا باعاه معاً فبيع الموكل أولى . الثاني إذا كان على العبد دين فوكل الغريم مولاه بقبضه لم يجز ولم يبرأ العبد من الدين لأن المولى في قبض الدين من عبده عامل لنفسه. والأصل أن العامل لنفسه لا يصلح أن يكون نائباً عن غيره وذكر في كتاب الوكالة أنه قبضه، فإن عاين الشهود قبضه رده إن كان قائماً وإن هلك لم يضمن لأن قبضه بإذن المديون والغريم. الرابع في توكيل الشريك العبد والأجنبي المولى في دينهما فالأول للعبد التاجر ولرجل آخر على رجل ألف درهم فوكل الشريك العبد بقبضه لم تجز الوكالة وما قبضه يكون بينهما نصفين ولو هلك المقبوض في يده هلك من مالهما الثاني من الثالث إذا كان للمأذون ولشريكه على رجل ألف درهم مجحدها فوكلا المولى بالخصومة مع غريمها جاز كما لو وكل المأذون مولاه بالخصومة وإن أقر المولى في مجلس القاضي بإيفاء الخصم لهما جاز إقراره كان على العبد دين أو لا لأن إقرار الوكيل على الموكل بالخصومة على موكله جائز . ثم أحد الشريكين على صاحبه؟ لا يخلو إما إن كذبا الوكيل في إقراره أو صدقاه أو كذبه أحدهما وصدقه الآخر، فإن صدقاه في إقراره فلا يرجع أحدهما على صاحبه بشيء، وكذا إن كذباه. وإن صدقه أحدهما وادعى على العبد ديناً رجع الشريك في رقبته بنصف حصته، وإن كان على دين لم يرجع على العبد ولا على مولاه حتى يقضي دينه، فإن فضل فيصح فيما يفضل عن دين الغريم وإن صدقه الشريك وكذبه العبد لم يرجع أحدهما على صاحبه بشيء، سواء كان على العبد دين أو لا . ولو وكل الشريك العبد بالخصومة فأقر أن الشريك قد استوفى حقه برئ الغريم من نصف الدين ولا ضمان على العبد ويقبض العبد نصف الدين ويكون بينه وبين شريكه ولو ادعى شريك العبد أن العبد قبض حقه فوكل العبد مولاه بخصومته أو بعض غرمائه فأقر الوكيل باستيفاء العبد فللشريك أن يأخذ العبد بربع الدين ويرجع على المديون بربعه، وكذا لو أقر العبد بذلك . وإذا وكل مأذوناً يشتري له بالنسيئة لم تجز الوكالة ولو لم يذكر النسيئة جاز استحساناً، فإذا حل الأجل يكون للبائع يأخذ الثمن من العبد ثم يرجع العبد بما أدى على الموكل ولو كان الوكيل صبياً أو محجوراً أو معتوهاً ثم أدرك أو أفاق لم تعد العهدة إليه لأنهما وقت العقد ليس من أهل العهدة بخلاف الوكيل إذا جن فأفاق أو أسلم المرتد تعود العهدة عليهما لأنهما وقت العهدة أهلها. من الخامس لو كان على المولى دين لرجل فوكل عبده بقبض ذلك جاز، فإن أقر بقبض ذلك والهلاك في يده صدق لأن العبد فيما يقبض عامل لغيره لا لنفسه، لرجل على عبدين مأذونين في التجارة دين فوكل أحدهما بقبضه جازت الوكالة لأن العبد يصلح وكيلاً للأجنبي بقبض الدين من مولاه، ولو أقر بقبضه صدق فيه مع يمينه، وإن نكل اليمين لزمه ذلك . على العبد دين فوكل الغريم ابن العبد أو أباه أو عبد أبيه أو مكاتبه فأقر الوكيل بقبض ذلك صدق لأنه لو وكل عبده بذلك جاز، فلو وكل ابنه بذلك لكان أولى .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: '(And he appoints an agent for both)' meaning it is permissible for him to appoint an agent for sale and purchase because they are from the accompaniments of agency; perhaps he cannot manage everything himself so he needs an assistant. And he made absolute in his saying 'he appoints an agent' so it includes whether or not he has a debt, whether the debt is encompassing or not, whether the agent is the master or the creditor of the slave, even though the apparent is that if there is no debt, the agency of the master (is preferred) because the master is principal in disposition and the agency of the slave's creditor is not valid because perhaps he will collect for himself and harm the rest. So if he had said 'and he appoints an agent other than the creditor and master where there is no debt' it would have been better. He said in al-Mabsut: The issues of the authorized slave's agency are of several types: first, the agency of the authorized slave; second, the agency of the creditor of his master for litigation; third, the agency of the creditor of a slave of the master's for collecting what the master owes. The first: The slave may appoint another to sell and buy for cash or credit because it is the practice of merchants. If he appoints a free authorized slave to sell his goods, and he sells them to a man who owes the appointed slave a debt, it becomes a set-off according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf, because according to them the agent has the power to discharge the buyer from the price, while according to him he does not. And if the agent and the principal sell together, the principal's sale takes precedence. The second: If the slave has a debt, and he appoints the creditor to collect it from the master, it is not valid and the slave is not absolved of the debt because the master, in collecting the debt from his slave, is acting for himself. The principle is that one acting for himself is not suitable to be a deputy for another. It is mentioned in the Book of Agency that if he collects it, and witnesses see the collection, he returns it if it is still in existence; if it perishes he does not guarantee because his collection was with the permission of the debtor and the creditor. The fourth: concerning the agency of the slave's partner and the outsider and the master regarding their debt. As for the first: the trading slave and another man have a claim of a thousand dirhams against another man, so the partner appoints the slave to collect it: the agency is not valid, and what he collects is split equally between them; if what was collected perishes in his hand, it perishes from both their wealths. As for the second of the third: if the authorized slave and his partner have a thousand dirhams against a man who denies it, and they appoint the master to litigate with their debtor, it is valid, just as if the authorized slave appointed his master to litigate. And if the master acknowledges in court that the opponent has paid them, his acknowledgment is valid whether the slave has debt or not, because the acknowledgment of an agent against his principal concerning litigation is valid regarding his principal. Then (the question arises) does one partner have recourse against the other? It is either that both deny the agent's acknowledgment, or both affirm it, or one denies and the other affirms. If both affirm his acknowledgment, one does not have recourse against the other for anything, similarly if both deny. If one affirms and claims a debt against the slave, the partner has recourse against the slave's person for half of his share; and if there is a debt (on the slave), he does not have recourse against the slave nor his master until he pays his debt; then if there is a surplus, it is valid regarding what remains after the creditor's debt. If the partner affirms and the slave denies, one does not have recourse against the other for anything, whether the slave has debt or not. And if the slave's partner appoints the slave for litigation, and he acknowledges that the partner has received his right, the debtor is absolved of half the debt and there is no liability on the slave; the slave collects half the debt and it is between him and his partner. And if the partner of the slave claims that the slave collected his right, and the slave appoints his master or some of his creditors for litigation, and the agent acknowledges that the slave has collected, then the partner may take the slave for a quarter of the debt and have recourse against the debtor for a quarter; similarly if the slave himself acknowledges that. And if one appoints an authorized slave to buy for him on credit, the agency is not valid; but if he does not mention credit, it is valid by istihsan. Then when the term comes due, the seller may take the price from the slave, and then the slave has recourse against the principal for what he paid. And if the agent was a child, or interdicted, or insane, and then comes of age or recovers, the liability does not revert to him because at the time of the contract they were not legal persons for liability, unlike if the agent becomes insane and then recovers, or an apostate becomes Muslim, the liability reverts to them because at the time of liability they were legal persons. From the fifth: If the master has a debt to a man, and he appoints his slave to collect it, it is valid; and if he acknowledges receipt and its loss in his hand, he is believed because the slave, in what he collects, acts for another, not for himself. A man has a debt against two authorized trading slaves; he appoints one of them to collect it: the agency is valid because the slave is suitable as an agent for an outsider to collect debt from his master. And if he acknowledges receipt, he is believed with his oath; if he refuses the oath, it becomes obligatory upon him. If the slave has a debt, and the creditor appoints the slave's son, or his father, or his father's slave, or his mukatab to collect, and the agent acknowledges receipt, he is believed because if he had appointed his own slave for that, it would be valid, so appointing his son is even more appropriate.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn phrase 'ويوكل بهما' (and he appoints an agent for both) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The commentator explains that an authorized slave (al-madhn) has the right to appoint agents for sale and purchase because such actions are integral to trade and the slave may not be able to do everything personally. The ruling is absolute: it applies whether the slave has debt or not, and whether the debt covers all his property or not. However, there is a nuance: if the slave has no debt, it is preferable for him to appoint his master as agent rather than his creditor, because the master is the rightful administrator of the slave's affairs; appointing a creditor may harm other creditors. The commentator then details various scenarios from al-Mabsut. The first scenario: the slave may appoint another person (free or slave) to sell and buy for cash or credit, as this is customary for merchants. If he appoints an authorized slave and that appointed slave sells to someone who owes the appointed slave a debt, it becomes a set-off (qisas) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), but Abu Yusuf disagrees because he does not consider the agent to have the power to discharge the buyer from the price. If both the principal and agent sell together, the principal's sale takes precedence. The second scenario: if the slave has a debt and he appoints the creditor to collect the debt from the master, it is not valid; the slave remains liable. The reason is that the master, in collecting from his own slave, acts for himself, and one acting for himself cannot be a deputy for another. If the creditor nonetheless collects and witnesses see it, he must return the collected property if it still exists; if it perishes, he is not liable because he collected with permission. The fourth scenario: regarding the agency of the slave's partner and an outsider. If a partner appoints the slave to collect a joint debt, the agency is invalid; the collected amount is split between them. If the partner and the slave jointly appoint the master to litigate, it is valid. The master's acknowledgment of payment is binding on both partners regarding their shares. The passage also discusses the effects of the master's acknowledgment when partners have a claim against a debtor. If both partners affirm the acknowledgment, no recourse. If one affirms and claims a debt against the slave, the affirming partner can take recourse against the slave's person for half his share, but if the slave has debts, he cannot take recourse until those debts are paid. Further scenarios cover the slave appointing his master or creditors for litigation, and the rulings on acknowledgment. The passage also addresses the validity of appointing an authorized slave to buy on credit: the agency is invalid if credit is explicitly mentioned, but valid if not mentioned (by istihsan). The liability of the slave-agent and the principal for the price is explained. Finally, if the master owes a debt, he can appoint his slave to collect it; the slave is believed if he acknowledges receipt. Similarly, a creditor of two authorized slaves can appoint one to collect; the appointed slave is believed with oath. The passage concludes with cases where the creditor appoints the slave's relatives as agents; such agency is valid and the agent's acknowledgment is accepted.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-madhūn): A slave who has been given permission (idhn) by his master to engage in trade; he has limited capacity to conduct business within the scope of permission.
توكيل (tawkīl): The act of appointing another person as an agent (wakīl) to perform a legal act on one's behalf.
قصاص (qisās): Set-off; the mutual cancellation of debts when two parties owe each other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When an authorized slave has no debt, who is the most appropriate person for him to appoint as agent for sale and purchase?
A) His master
B) His creditor
C) Any other authorized slave
D) An outsider

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave may appoint agents for trade based on istihsan, but the validity of set-off by the agent depends on whether the majority position (allowing set-off) is followed.

Agency and Confession by the Permitted Slave
Rulings on wakalah and iqrar with a ma'dhun slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings of agency involving a ma'dhun slave and apply them to scenarios of confession and debt collection.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun slave can be an agent in trade, but his agency and confession have specific effects on debts and partners.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq details the complex rules of wakalah with a ma'dhun slave. It covers multiple scenarios: agency for debt collection, appointment of the master as agent, effects of confession by the agent, purchase on credit, and capacity issues. Understanding these rulings is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi fiqh in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Partner appoints ma'dhun to collect joint debt
Identify the ruling: the agency is invalid, but any collected amount is shared equally between the partners.
The slave's act is valid as a possession but not as an agent for both.
2
Master appointed as agent for litigation
Note that when the ma'dhun and his partner appoint the master to litigate, it is valid, and the master's confession in court binds both principals.
The confession is valid even if the slave has debts.
3
Effects of the agent's confession
Examine the three scenarios: both affirm, both belie, or one affirms. Determine the recourse accordingly.
If one affirms and claims a debt from the slave, he can take the slave in payment after other debts.
4
Agency for purchase on credit
State that specifying credit invalidates the agency; otherwise, it is valid by istihsan, and the seller can claim the price from the slave.
The slave then recovers from the principal.
5
Special cases: capacity and third parties
Understand that a child or interdicted person cannot be an agent, but if they later gain capacity, the guarantee does not return. Also, a slave can be agent for a stranger against his master, and a creditor can appoint the slave's relative as agent.
These rulings are based on the slave's ability to act for others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولرجل آخر على رجل ألف درهم فوكل الشريك العبد بقبضه لم تجز الوكالة وما قبضه يكون بينهما نصفين ولو هلك المقبوض في يده هلك من مالهما الثاني من الثالث إذا كان للمأذون ولشريكه على رجل ألف درهم مجحدها فوكلا المولى بالخصومة مع غريمها جاز كما لو وكل المأذون مولاه بالخصومة وإن أقر المولى في مجلس القاضي بإيفاء الخصم لهما جاز إقراره كان على العبد دين أو لا لأن إقرار الوكيل على الموكل بالخصومة على موكله جائز . ثم أحد الشريكين على صاحبه؟ لا يخلو إما إن كذبا الوكيل في إقراره أو صدقاه أو كذبه أحدهما وصدقه الآخر، فإن صدقاه في إقراره فلا يرجع أحدهما على صاحبه بشيء، وكذا إن كذباه. وإن صدقه أحدهما وادعى على العبد ديناً رجع الشريك في رقبته بنصف حصته، وإن كان على دين لم يرجع على العبد ولا على مولاه حتى يقضي دينه، فإن فضل فيصح فيما يفضل عن دين الغريم وإن صدقه الشريك وكذبه العبد لم يرجع أحدهما على صاحبه بشيء، سواء كان على العبد دين أو لا . ولو وكل الشريك العبد بالخصومة فأقر أن الشريك قد استوفى حقه برئ الغريم من نصف الدين ولا ضمان على العبد ويقبض العبد نصف الدين ويكون بينه وبين شريكه ولو ادعى شريك العبد أن العبد قبض حقه فوكل العبد مولاه بخصومته أو بعض غرمائه فأقر الوكيل باستيفاء العبد فللشريك أن يأخذ العبد بربع الدين ويرجع على المديون بربعه، وكذا لو أقر العبد بذلك . وإذا وكل مأذوناً يشتري له بالنسيئة لم تجز الوكالة ولو لم يذكر النسيئة جاز استحساناً، فإذا حل الأجل يكون للبائع يأخذ الثمن من العبد ثم يرجع العبد بما أدى على الموكل ولو كان الوكيل صبياً أو محجوراً أو معتوهاً ثم أدرك أو أفاق لم تعد العهدة إليه لأنهما وقت العقد ليس من أهل العهدة بخلاف الوكيل إذا جن فأفاق أو أسلم المرتد تعود العهدة عليهما لأنهما وقت العهدة أهلها. من الخامس لو كان على المولى دين لرجل فوكل عبده بقبض ذلك جاز، فإن أقر بقبض ذلك والهلاك في يده صدق لأن العبد فيما يقبض عامل لغيره لا لنفسه، لرجل على عبدين مأذونين في التجارة دين فوكل أحدهما بقبضه جازت الوكالة لأن العبد يصلح وكيلاً للأجنبي بقبض الدين من مولاه، ولو أقر بقبضه صدق فيه مع يمينه، وإن نكل اليمين لزمه ذلك . على العبد دين فوكل الغريم ابن العبد أو أباه أو عبد أبيه أو مكاتبه فأقر الوكيل بقبض ذلك صدق لأنه لو وكل عبده بذلك جاز، فلو وكل ابنه بذلك لكان أولى .
Another man has a thousand dirhams owed by a man, so the partner appointed the slave as agent to collect it: the agency is not valid, and what he collects is half between them. If what is collected perishes in his hand, it perishes from their wealth. Second from third: if the ma'dhun and his partner have a thousand dirhams denied by a man, and they appoint the master to litigate with the debtor, it is valid, just as if the ma'dhun appointed his master to litigate. If the master confesses in the judge's court that the opponent has paid them, his confession is valid, whether the slave has a debt or not, because the confession of the agent regarding his principal in litigation is valid. Then one partner regarding the other? It is not devoid: either both belie the agent in his confession, or both affirm him, or one belies him and the other affirms. If both affirm his confession, then neither partner has recourse against the other for anything; likewise if both belie. If one affirms him and claims a debt against the slave, the partner has recourse against his person for half his share; if the slave has a debt, he does not have recourse against the slave nor against his master until his debt is paid; if there is a surplus, it is valid regarding what exceeds the debtor's debt. If the partner affirms and the slave belies, neither partner has recourse against the other for anything, whether the slave has a debt or not. If the partner appoints the slave to litigate, and he confesses that the partner has received his right, the debtor is released from half the debt, and there is no liability on the slave; the slave collects half the debt, and it is between him and his partner. If the partner of the slave claims that the slave collected his right, so the slave appoints his master or some of his creditors to litigate, and the agent confesses that the slave has collected, then the partner may take the slave for a quarter of the debt and has recourse against the debtor for a quarter; likewise if the slave so confesses. If one appoints a ma'dhun to buy for him on credit, the agency is not valid; if he does not mention credit, it is valid by istihsan. When the term arrives, the seller may take the price from the slave, then the slave recovers what he paid from the principal. If the agent was a child, interdicted, or insane, then he attains majority or recovers sanity, the guarantee does not return to him because at the time of contract they were not qualified for guarantee; unlike the agent if he becomes insane then recovers, or an apostate becomes Muslim, the guarantee returns to them because at the time of guarantee they were qualified for it. From the fifth: if the master has a debt owed by a man, and he appoints his slave to collect it, it is valid. If he confesses to collecting it and its destruction in his hand, he is believed because the slave in what he collects acts for another, not for himself. A man has a debt against two ma'dhun slaves, and he appoints one of them to collect it: the agency is valid because a slave can act as agent for a stranger to collect a debt from his master. If he confesses to collecting it, he is believed with an oath; if he refuses the oath, it is binding upon him. A slave has a debt, so the creditor appoints the slave's son, father, his father's slave, or his mukatib as agent, and the agent confesses to collecting it: he is believed because if he had appointed his own slave for that, it would be valid; if he appointed his son, it would be more so.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on agency (wakalah) involving a ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted to trade). It states multiple scenarios: First, if a partner appoints the slave to collect a debt owed to both, the agency is invalid because the slave cannot be agent for both in a joint claim? Actually the ruling says it does not proceed, but any collection is shared. If the master is appointed as agent by the slave and partner to litigate, it is valid. The master's confession of payment in court is binding on both principals. The passage then details the consequences of the slave's confession as agent: if both partners affirm, no recourse; if both belie, no recourse; if one affirms and claims a debt from the slave, he can take the slave in payment. There is also a case where the slave is appointed to litigate and confesses that the partner received his right; the debtor is freed from half. Another scenario: if the slave's partner claims the slave collected his right, and the slave appoints the master as agent who confesses, the partner can take the slave for a quarter and also pursue the debtor. The passage then discusses agency for purchase on credit: it is invalid if specified, valid if not specified by istihsan, and the seller can take the price from the slave who then recovers from the principal. It also covers agency by a child or interdicted person, and the effect of later sanity or majority. Finally, it discusses the master appointing his slave to collect a debt, and the slave's confession being accepted; agency between two slaves; and a creditor appointing a relative of the slave as agent. Throughout, the key principle is that the ma'dhun can act as agent in trade-related matters, and his iqrar (confession) as agent binds the principal, with details depending on debts and conflicting claims.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): Appointing someone to act on one's behalf in a legal matter.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession or admission of a right or debt.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
النسيئة (al-nasi'ah): Credit sale; payment deferred to a future date.
العهدة (al-'uhdah): Guarantee or responsibility; the liability of an agent for the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a partner appoints a ma'dhun slave to collect a joint debt, and the slave collects the amount, what is the distribution according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The entire collected amount belongs to the appointing partner.
B) The collected amount is split equally between the slave and the partner.
C) The collected amount is split equally between the two partners.
D) The collected amount is forfeited because the agency was invalid.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave can be an agent in trade, but his agency and confession have specific effects that depend on the context and the relationship of the parties.

Confession by the Permitted Slave
Ruling with proof on a ma'dhun's confession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a ma'dhun's confession and identify which confessions are valid based on their relation to trade.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's confession is valid only if it pertains to trade; otherwise it is restricted.

The ma'dhun (permitted slave) can engage in trade, but his legal capacity to admit liabilities (iqrar) is not absolute. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that his confessions are effective only when they are necessary supplements to trade. Understanding this principle helps distinguish valid confessions (e.g., for debt or usurpation) from invalid ones (e.g., for a debt owed to his master).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The passage begins by stating that a ma'dhun may confess to debt, usurpation, and deposit. This is the default ruling – it is valid. Note that the text does not say 'always'; the subsequent conditions qualify this generality.
2
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The ruling is justified by necessity: if confessions were not accepted, nobody would deal with the ma'dhun. Thus, iqrar is considered a supplement (min tawabi' al-tijarah) to trade. This principle determines which confessions are valid.
This is a talil based on custom and need, not a direct text.
3
Differentiate trade-related from non-trade confessions
Debt (dayn) and usurpation (ghasb) are trade-related because they involve compensatory liability (daman al-mu'awadah). In contrast, confessing to intercourse in marriage is not trade-related, so its effect is delayed until after freedom.
The text contrasts a corrupt sale (where uqr is immediate) with marriage (where uqr is delayed).
4
Examine the effect of existing debts
The ma'dhun's own debts affect the validity of his confessions. For example, if he confesses to an item belonging to his master, it is valid only if he has no debts; otherwise, it is invalid because it harms creditors. Similarly, a confession to a debt for his master is invalid regardless of debts.
This shows that the ma'dhun's iqrar is subject to the rights of creditors.
5
Apply to specific scenarios
The text provides numerous sub-rulings: confessions to a mukatib, to relatives (son, father), etc. The key is whether the confession benefits the ma'dhun's trade or harms others' rights. If the addressee is not a legitimate trade counterparty, the confession is usually invalid.
For instance, confession to a debt for his own mukatib is valid because the mukatib can have a debt against the master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويقر بدين وغصب ووديعة) لأن الإقرار من توابع التجارة لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لم يعامله أحد فلا بد من قبول إمراره فيما هو من باب التجارة والإقرار بالدين منه وكذا بالغصب لأن ضمان الغصب ضمان معاوضة عندنا لأنه يملك المغصوب بالضمان فكان من باب التجارة ومن باب المعاوضة، وكذا لو أقر به أحد المتعاوضين كان شريكه مطالباً به . ولو اشترى جارية شراء فاسداً فأقر أنه وطئها يجب عليه العقر للحال لأن لزومه باعتبار الشراء إذ لولاه لوجب الحد دون العقر بخلاف ما إذ أقر أنه وطئها بالنكاح حيث لا يظهر وجوب العقر في الحال في حق المولى ويؤخذ به بعد الحرية لأنه ليس من باب التجارة لأنه لا يجد بداً منه فكان من توابعها ولوازمها. وأطلق في قوله «أقر وما بعده» فشمل ما إذا أقر للمولى أو لغيره. وما إذا كان عليه دين أو لا، وما إذا كان في صحته أو مرضه أو الأول وهو ما إذا أقر للمولى. قال في المبسوط : إذا أقر المأذون بعين في يده لمولاه إن لم يكن عليه دين جاز، وكذا العبد مولاه وإلا فلا لأن الإقرار لم يلاق حق أحد وقد يفيد المولى فائدة إذا لحق للعبد دين لا يتعلق به حق الغرماء ولو أقر بدين لمولاه لا يجوز كان عليه دين أو لا، لأن المولى لا يستحق عليه دين ومثله لو أقر لعبد مولاه المحجور. ولو أقر لعبد مولاه المأذون بدين؛ إن كان على المقر له دين لا يصح إقراره لأنه إقرار للمولى، ولو أقر بألفين لمكاتب مولاه ولا دين عليه يصح كما لو أقر للمولى، ولو كان عليه دين لا يصح. ولو أقر بالدين لمكاتب مولاه صح كان على المقر له دين أو لا لأن المكاتب يصح أن يثبت له دين على مولاه فعلى عبد مولاه أولى ولو أقر لابن مولاه أو لأبيه بوديعة أو دين وعليه دين صدق، ولو أقر لابن نفسه أو لأبيه أو لمكاتب أبيه لم يصح عليه دين أو لا عند الإمام.
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): "And he confesses to debt, usurpation, and deposit," because confession is from the supplements of trade. For if his confession were not valid, no one would deal with him, so it is necessary to accept his pronouncement in what is from the category of trade. Confession of debt is from that, and likewise of usurpation, because liability for usurpation is a compensatory liability according to us (Hanafis), since the usurper owns the usurped object by liability, so it is from the category of trade and compensation. Likewise, if one of two transactors confesses to it, his partner is pursued for it. If he buys a slave-girl in a corrupt sale, and he confesses to having intercourse with her, the dower (uqr) is immediately obligatory because its obligation is based on the purchase; otherwise, the penalty would be hadd, not dower. Unlike if he confesses to intercourse in marriage, where the obligation of dower does not appear immediately regarding the master, and it is taken after freedom because it is not from trade, as he cannot avoid it, so it is from its supplements and necessities. And he (the author) made his statement 'he confesses and what follows' absolute, including whether he confesses to the master or to others, whether he has a debt or not, whether he is in health or illness, or the first case being confession to the master. He said in al-Mabsut: If the ma'dhun confesses to an item in his hand belonging to his master, if he has no debt, it is valid; likewise the slave to his master; otherwise no, because the confession did not meet anyone's right, and it may benefit the master if the slave later incurs a debt not related to creditors' rights. If he confesses to a debt owed to his master, it is not valid whether he has a debt or not, because the master does not have a claim of debt against him. Similarly, if he confesses to a slave of his master who is interdicted. If he confesses to a debt for the ma'dhun slave of his master: if the confessor (the one in whose favor) has a debt, his confession is not valid because it is a confession to the master. If he confesses two thousand to his master's mukatib and there is no debt, it is valid, just as if he confessed to the master; if there is a debt, it is not valid. If he confesses to a debt for his master's mukatib, it is valid whether the confessor has a debt or not, because the mukatib can have a debt against his master, so to his master's slave is more appropriate. If he confesses to a deposit or debt for his master's son or father while having a debt, he is truthful. If he confesses to his own son, father, or his father's mukatib, it is not valid whether he has a debt or not according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the capacity of a ma'dhun (permitted slave) to make confessions (iqrar) regarding debts, usurpation (ghasb), and deposits (wadi'ah). The text states that iqrar is a necessary supplement to trade; without it, no one would transact with him. The Hanafi school distinguishes between compensatory liability (daman al-mu'awadah) for ghasb, which is considered trade-related, and other confessions not related to trade. A specific case: if he buys a slave-girl in a corrupt sale and confesses to intercourse, the obligatory dower (uqr) is immediately due because it stems from the purchase; whereas if he confesses to intercourse in marriage, it is not immediate because it is not trade. Then the text elaborates on who the confession is made to: to the master, to other slaves, to relatives, etc., with details dependent on whether the ma'dhun has debts. For example, confession to an item for the master is valid only if no debt; confession to a debt for the master is void. Various sub-rulings are given from al-Mabsut and al-Asl, including confessions to mukatib, free relatives, etc. The overall principle is that iqrar must be trade-related to be binding on the ma'dhun's estate.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession or admission of a right, debt, or liability.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation; wrongful taking of another's property.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): Deposit; property entrusted for safekeeping.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
العقر (al-uqr): Dower or compensation for intercourse with a slave-girl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a ma'dhun confesses to a debt owed to his own master?
A) It is valid only if he has no other debts.
B) It is valid regardless of debts.
C) It is not valid whether he has debts or not.
D) It is valid if the master confirms it.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's confession is valid only when it is a necessary supplement to his trade, such as debts, usurpation, or deposits arising from commercial dealings.

The Authorized Slave Buying Food: Conditions and Validity
Expansion on the ruling of the authorized slave buying food.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an authorized slave's purchase of food is valid, and identify the scenarios where it is suspended or invalid.

Core Idea
An authorized slave may buy food even with debt, but without debt, a mortgage to the master is necessary for the purchase to be valid.

This lesson focuses on a commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq on the ruling of the authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) buying food. The text clarifies that the rule applies broadly, regardless of the slave's debt status or existing mortgages. However, a specific condition emerges: if the slave has no debt, the purchase is valid only if he mortgages something to his master. This reveals the Hanafi concern with financial reliability and the master's protection of his interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is the authorized slave buying food. The default ruling is that it is generally valid, as the commentary begins by stating the rule broadly.
The ma'dhun has capacity to trade within authorization.
2
Recognize the scope of the rule
The ruling applies whether the slave has debt or not, whether the debt is comprehensive (mustaghriq) or not, and whether there is a mortgage with the master or with some creditors.
The text uses 'أما إذا لم يكن لا عليه دين' to signal a special scenario.
3
Note the exception for the no-debt scenario
If the slave has no debt, validity depends on giving a mortgage to the master. Without it, the purchase is invalid.
This is a condition (shart) for validity in that specific case.
4
Understand the reasoning
The reasoning is: if the slave is not fit to be a guarantor (i.e., not trustworthy), he cannot be a mortgagee either. So the master requires security to protect his interests.
This is based on caution (ihtiyat) to prevent loss to the master.
5
Identify the suspended case
If the slave mortgages with some creditors instead of the master, the transaction is suspended (tawaquf) pending further resolution, as mentioned in the foundational text (al-Asl).
Tawaquf means the contract is not immediately enforceable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال يشتري أن طعاماً . يعني عليه دين أو لا، كان مستغرقاً أو لا رهن عند المولى أو عند بعض الغرماء. أما إذا لم يكن لا عليه دين يصح إن برهن من المولى، وكذا لو لم يكن عليه دين لأنه إذا لم يصلح أن يكون عدلاً لا يصلح أن يكون مرتهناً، فلو رهن من بعض الغرماء يتوقف كما ذكر في الأصل.
He said: He buys food. Meaning, whether he has debt or not, whether it is comprehensive or not, with a mortgage with the master or with some of the creditors. But if he has no debt, it is valid if he mortgages from the master. Likewise, if he has no debt, because if he is not fit to be a guarantor, he is not fit to be a mortgagee. So if he mortgages from some of the creditors, it is suspended as mentioned in the original.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling that the authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) buys food. The commentator explains that the ruling is general: it applies regardless of whether the slave has debts or not, whether the debts are comprehensive (mustaghriq) or not, and whether there is a mortgage (rahn) with the master or with some creditors. The text then addresses a special scenario: if the slave has no debt, the purchase is valid only if he gives a mortgage to the master. The reasoning given is that if the slave is not a qualified guarantor (i.e., not trustworthy), he cannot be a mortgagee either. If he instead mortgages with some creditors, the transaction is suspended (tawaquf) pending further resolution, as mentioned in the foundational text (al-Asl). This highlights the Hanafi view that the authorized slave's freedom to trade is limited by considerations of financial reliability.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who is authorized by his master to engage in trade.
الرهن (al-rahn): A mortgage or pledge given as security for a debt.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission given by the master to the slave to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The purchase is invalid.
B) The purchase is valid but the master can rescind.
C) The purchase is valid only if the slave mortgages with creditors.
D) The purchase is suspended until the master approves.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an authorized slave's food purchase hinges on the presence or absence of debt: without debt, a mortgage to the master is required; with debt, the purchase is generally valid regardless of mortgage.

Permissible Transactions for the Authorized Slave
List of permissible transactions with justification and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which commercial transactions an authorized slave may undertake and explain why mufawada is excluded.

Core Idea
An authorized slave may engage in any transaction that is customary trade practice, except those involving suretyship.

The authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) enjoys a broad commercial capacity, akin to a free merchant, within the scope of the permission granted by the master. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details specific transactions, from hiring to sharecropping to partnerships, and clarifies the boundary: anything that falls within the practice of merchants (min sina'at al-tujjar) is permissible, while contracts requiring suretyship (kafala) are not. The ruling is supported by a hadith linking agriculture to trade, reinforcing the principle that productivity is commerce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed transactions
Read the passage and list all commercial activities explicitly stated as permissible: hiring, mudaraba, sharecropping, musaqah, renting, and 'inan partnership.
These are not exhaustive but illustrative of trade practices.
2
Recognize the underlying principle
Each activity is justified because it is 'min sina'at al-tujjar' (from the practice of merchants). Understand that the slave's authorization covers any act that is customary in trade.
This principle extends beyond the listed examples.
3
Locate and analyze the dalil
Find the hadith 'al-zari' tajir rabbihi' (the farmer is a merchant of his Lord). Identify how this serves as proof that agricultural and related activities are trade.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for establishing permissibility.
4
Identify the exception: mufawada
Note that mufawada partnership is explicitly prohibited. Understand the reason: it includes kafala (suretyship), which is not within the slave's authorization.
If entered, it is treated as 'inan partnership, valid only to the extent of agency.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a new transaction (e.g., investing in a joint-stock company), determine if it falls under 'min sina'at al-tujjar' and thus permissible.
Focus on the presence or absence of suretyship elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : (ويستأجر ويضارب لأنه من صنيع التجار فيجوز له المضاربة أخذاً ودفعاً، وكذا الإجارة بأن يؤجر غلمانه ويستأجر أحراراً وله أن يدفع الأرض مزارعة ويأخذها ومساقاة لأن كل ذلك من عمل التجار قال عليه الصلاة السلام الزارع تاجر ربه» وله أن ويزرعه فيها ويستأجر البيوت والحوانيت ويؤجرها لما فيه من تحصيل المال ويشارك شركة عنان ولا يشارك مفاوضة لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن فلو فعلا ذلك كانت عناناً لأن المفاوضة عنان وزيادة فصحت بقدر ما يتملكه المأذون وهو الوكالة.
May Allah have mercy on him: (He hires and engages in mudaraba because it is the practice of merchants. So he may do mudaraba both as investor and as agent, likewise hiring: he may hire out his slaves and hire free men. He may give land for sharecropping and take it, and for musaqah, because all that is the practice of merchants. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The farmer is a merchant of his Lord.' He may sow in it and rent houses and shops and rent them out, because it involves acquiring wealth. He may enter into a partnership of 'inan but not a partnership of mufawada, because it is based on agency and suretyship, and suretyship is not included in the authorization. If they do that, it becomes 'inan, because mufawada is 'inan plus extra, so it is valid to the extent that the authorized slave can own, which is agency.)
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage expands on the authorized slave's commercial capacity. The matn states that he may hire and enter into mudaraba. The commentator explains that mudaraba (silent partnership) is permissible both as the capital provider (akhdhan) and as the working partner (daf'an). Similarly, hiring (ijara) is allowed: he can hire out his own slaves and hire free men. He may also engage in sharecropping (muzara'a) and orchard irrigation (musaqah), giving or taking land, because all these are customary trade practices. A hadith is cited: 'The farmer is a merchant of his Lord.' He may plant crops in the land, rent houses and shops, and then sublet them. He can enter a partnership of 'inan (where each partner contributes capital and labor proportionally) but not a partnership of mufawada (full unlimited partnership) because mufawada includes kafala (suretyship), which is not within the scope of the authorization. If they do enter a mufawada contract, it is treated as 'inan and is valid to the extent of the authorized slave's capacity—i.e., agency.

Key Terms
المضاربة (al-mudaraba): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, sharing profits as agreed.
المفاوضة (al-mufawada): A full unlimited partnership where each partner is both agent and surety for the other.
الكفالة (al-kafala): Suretyship: a contract where one person guarantees the debt or obligation of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the authorized slave not allowed to enter a mufawada partnership?
A) Because it requires both parties to be free.
B) Because it includes suretyship, which is not covered by the authorization.
C) Because it involves capital the slave does not own.
D) Because it is a form of contract not practiced by merchants.

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave may engage in any customary trade transaction, but cannot enter contracts that require personal liability for others (kafala).

Authorized Slave Hiring Himself: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling with rebuttal of Shafi'i view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits an authorized slave to hire himself out and distinguish this from selling or mortgaging oneself, using the concept of earning profit (iktisab al-manafi).

Core Idea
The authorized slave may hire himself because authorization aims at profit, unlike selling which ends it.

A key issue in early Islamic commercial law is the scope of an authorized slave's capacity to transact. The Hanafi school holds that such a slave can hire out his own labor, while Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the Hanafi position and the critical distinction between hiring and alienating one's person.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its basis
Identify the ruling from the matn: 'He hires himself' (يؤجر نفسه). Understand that the Hanafi school justifies this by linking it to the purpose of authorization: earning profits (iktisab al-manafi).
2
Present the Shafi'i objection
Recognize the opposing view: Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the authorization (idhn) does not extend to transactions concerning the slave's own body, analogizing to the prohibition of selling or mortgaging oneself.
The key distinction is the type of transaction.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: Hiring oneself is a form of earning profit (manfa'a), which falls within the scope of authorization, whereas selling or mortgaging would nullify the authorization itself.
Thus, hiring is valid; selling is not.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Recognize the practical difference: In the Hanafi school, the slave can generate income through wages, while in the Shafi'i school, such contracts are invalid.
This affects the slave's ability to contribute to the master's business.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويؤجر نفسه) المأذون يؤجر نفسه وقد قدمناه . وقال الإمام الشافعي : لا يملك ذلك لأن الإذن لم يتناول التصرف في نفسه ولهذا لا يملك يبيع نفسه ولا أن يرهنها . قلنا الإذن يتضمن اكتساب المنافع والأجارة منه بخلاف البيع أو الرهن لأنه يبطل الإذن .
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (He hires himself). The authorized slave hires himself, and we have already mentioned this. Imam al-Shafi'i said: He does not have the capacity for that, because the authorization does not cover disposing of his own person; hence he cannot sell himself nor mortgage her (i.e., his person). We say: The authorization entails earning profits, and hiring himself is a form of earning profits, unlike selling or mortgaging, because that would nullify the authorization.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of the authorized slave hiring his own person for labor. The matn states clearly that he may do so. The commentator notes this has been mentioned before. Then, the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i is presented: he argues that the slave cannot hire himself because the authorization (idhn) does not cover transactions involving his own person. He draws an analogy: just as the slave cannot sell himself or mortgage himself, he cannot hire himself out. The Hanafi response is that authorization is given for earning profits (manafi), and hiring oneself is a means of earning, whereas selling or mortgaging the slave would effectively end the authorization (because the slave would be transferred or the authorization voided). Therefore, hiring is permissible as part of the slave's capacity to generate gain through trade.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijara): Hiring; a contract for temporary use of a service or asset in exchange for compensation.
الإذن (al-idhn): Authorization; the master's permission for the slave to engage in trade.
المنافع (al-manafi): Profits, benefits, or utilities derived from a thing or service.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the passage, why is hiring oneself valid while selling oneself is not?
A) Because hiring is a less serious transaction.
B) Because hiring earns profit whereas selling nullifies the authorization.
C) Because the slave owns his labor but not his person.
D) Because selling oneself is explicitly forbidden by the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The authorized slave's capacity to hire himself hinges on the purpose of authorization: earning profit (iktisab al-manafi) is central, and hiring yields profit without destroying the authorization.
