The Validity and Limits of a Ma'dhun's Acknowledgment
Explains validity and conditions of the ma'dhun's acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a ma'dhun's acknowledgment and distinguish between acknowledgments he can and cannot make based on his authority.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's acknowledgment is valid only for matters he can legally create himself.

The ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) has limited legal capacity. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines when his acknowledgment (iqrar) of debts, freedom, or seller's actions is binding. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold his iqrar is generally valid, but with crucial exceptions tied to his authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the ma'dhun's acknowledgment (iqrar). According to the Sahibayn, it is valid (sahih). Note that Abu Hanifa may differ.
The text says 'Wa qala: yasih' – 'And they two said: It is valid.'
2
Identify the condition of authority
The ma'dhun can only validly acknowledge matters he has the power to create. He cannot acknowledge incidental freedom because he cannot create it.
Original freedom is attached to the essence, so acknowledgment of it is valid.
3
Distinguish debts to non-master vs master
Acknowledgment of debt to a non-master is valid. For matters benefiting the master, special rules apply (e.g., if master confirms, acknowledgment becomes effective).
The text: 'if he acknowledges for other than the master, it is a debt upon him.'
4
Analyze the case of buying a slave
If a ma'dhun buys a slave and then claims the seller freed or mudabbar'd the slave, he is not believed because he acknowledges incidental freedom. If the seller confirms, the sale is rescinded.
The ma'dhun's acknowledgment is not binding here, but the seller's confirmation is effective.
5
Extract the general principle
The ma'dhun's power to acknowledge is co-extensive with his power to create the disposition. He cannot acknowledge what he cannot legally do himself.
This principle resolves the various cases discussed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون
وقالا : يصح وبيان الدليل من الجانبين مذكور فيه وأما إذا أقر لغير المولى فهو عليه دين أو لا . كان صحيح
قال في المحيط : ولو صدقه مولاه في الاقتصاص بنكاح فاسد بدأ بدين الغرماء، فإن من عقرها لأن ضمان العقر بمنزلة ضمان الجناية، وفي ضمان الجناية لا يصدق العبد في حق الغرماء كما لو أقر بقطع يد أو رجل. ولو أقر بحرية الجارية التي في يده لأنه لا يملك إنشاء حرية طارئة للحال فلا يملك الإقرار بها، يصح ومتى أقر بحرية أصلية يصح لأن الحرية الأصلية غير ثابتة بإقراره بل مضافة إلى الذات . ولو اشترى عبداً من رجل وقبضه ثم أقر أن البائع أعتقه أو دبره أو استولدها ولو أمة لم يصدق ويبعها لأن أقر بحرية طارئة، فإن صدقه البائع انتقض البيع ويرد عليه الثمن لأن التصديق من البائع إقرار منه بإنشاء هذه الأشياء وهو يملك إنشاء هذه التصرفات فيملك الإقرار بها، ويصدق العبد في نقض البيع لأنه يملك نقض البيع
Book of the Permitted Slave. And they two said: It is valid. And the explanation of the evidence from both sides is mentioned therein. As for if he acknowledges for other than the master, it is a debt upon him or not. It was valid. He said in al-Muhit: And if his master confirms him in retribution for a void marriage, he begins with the debt of the creditors. For the compensation for consummation is like the guarantee of injury. And in the guarantee of injury, the slave is not believed against the creditors, just as if he acknowledged cutting off a hand or a foot. And if he acknowledged the freedom of the slave-girl in his hand, because he does not have the power to create incidental freedom at the moment, so he does not have the power to acknowledge it; it is valid. Whenever he acknowledges original freedom, it is valid, because original freedom is not established by his acknowledgment but is attached to the essence. And if he bought a slave from a man and took possession, then acknowledged that the seller had freed him or made him mudabbar or had intercourse with her (the slave-girl) even if a female slave, he is not believed and she is sold, because he acknowledged incidental freedom. If the seller confirms him, the sale is rescinded and the price is returned to him, because the confirmation from the seller is an acknowledgment from him of creating these things, and he has the power to create these dispositions, so he has the power to acknowledge them. And the slave is believed in rescinding the sale because he has the power to rescind the sale.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the permissibility and validity of a ma'dhun's (a slave permitted to trade) acknowledgment. The passage begins by noting an internal Hanafi disagreement: the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the ma'dhun's acknowledgment is valid, while the text implies Abu Hanifa may disagree, though his evidence is cited elsewhere. The ruling differentiates based on the nature of the acknowledgment: if it is for a debt to a non-master, it is valid; if for a benefit of the master, special rules apply. The commentary from al-Muhit clarifies that if the master confirms the ma'dhun in a debt related to a void marriage, creditors' debts take precedence. The passage then discusses acknowledgment of freedom: a ma'dhun cannot acknowledge incidental freedom (like recent manumission) because he cannot create it, but can acknowledge original freedom. If he buys a slave and then claims the seller freed him, he is not believed unless the seller confirms, in which case the sale is rescinded. The key point is that the ma'dhun's power of acknowledgment is tied to his authority to create the disposition he acknowledges.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission by his master to engage in trade and commerce.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a legal right or status, often used in context of debts or freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a ma'dhun acknowledge the incidental freedom of a slave he bought?
A) Yes, because he has permission to trade.
B) No, because he cannot create incidental freedom.
C) Only if the master confirms.
D) Yes, but the acknowledgment is void.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's acknowledgment is valid only when it pertains to matters within his authority to create, such as debts to non-masters, but not for incidental freedom.

Contradictory Claims of the Ma'dhun
Ruling on contradictory claim of ma'dhun from al-Ziyadat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a ma'dhun's self-contradictory claim is rejected and identify the consequences for evidence and oath.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun who contradicts himself loses the right to claim — his evidence is rejected and the opponent need not swear.

Imagine a ma'dhun who first says he bought goods on credit, then later claims he paid cash. Which statement is believed? The Hanafi school, drawing from al-Ziyadat, establishes a clear rule: inconsistency destroys credibility. In this lesson, we unpack the ruling that a contradictory claim by a ma'dhun is not accepted, his evidence is dismissed, and the opponent is not required to take an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The ma'dhun makes a claim (da'wa) regarding a transaction, such as purchase or sale. Identify the specific claim being made.
The text mentions 'claim' (da'wah) and 'evidence' (bayyinah).
2
Apply the ruling: claim invalid
The text states 'his claim is not valid' (la yasihhu da'wahu). This is the primary ruling.
Invalidity means it is legally ineffective.
3
Map the consequences
His evidence is not accepted (la tuqbal bayyinatahu), and the seller is not made to take an oath if he denies (la yustahlaf al-ba'i'). This follows from the claim's invalidity.
The opponent's denial is sufficient without oath.
4
Understand the reason
The reason given is 'because he contradicts himself' (li-annahu yantaqid). Self-contradiction undermines credibility.
Contradiction (naqd) is key.
5
Locate the authority
This ruling is from al-Ziyadat, a core book of Zahir al-Riwayah. Recognize its authority in the Hanafi school.
Al-Ziyadat is by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في الزيادات أنه لا يصح دعواه ولا تقبل بينته ولا يستحلف البائع إذا أنكر لأنه ينتاقض. ولو أقر بألفين لأجنبي جاز إذا أقر مطلقاً، ويحمل على المعاوضة. ولو باع المأذون عبده فقال المشتري إنه حر وصدقه المأذون لا يصدق ونفذ عتقه على المشتري إذا أقر المأذون المديون أو غير المديون بدين كان عليه وهو محجور من غصب أو وديعة استهلكها أو مضاربة أو عارية خالف فيها، فإن كذبه رب المال وقال هذا كله في إذنك لم يصدق العبد في شيء منه ولزمه كله وإن صدقه رب المال لزمه الغصب خاصة لأن الغصب يوجب الضمان للحال بخلاف غيره لأنه أضاف الإقرار إلى حالة تمنعه.
And it is mentioned in al-Ziyadat that his claim is not valid, his evidence is not accepted, and the seller is not made to take an oath if he denies, because he contradicts himself. And if he acknowledges two thousand to a stranger, it is permissible if he acknowledges absolutely, and it is interpreted as compensation. And if the ma'dhun sells his slave and the buyer says he is free and the ma'dhun confirms him, he is not believed, and his (the buyer's) manumission is executed upon the buyer. If the ma'dhun, whether in debt or not, acknowledges a debt that was upon him while he was interdicted, whether from usurpation, a trust he consumed, mudarabah, or a loan he violated, then if the owner of the wealth denies him and says all this was within your permission, the slave is not believed in any of it and it all becomes binding upon him. And if the owner of the wealth confirms him, then only the usurpation becomes binding because usurpation necessitates guarantee immediately, unlike others, because he attributed the acknowledgment to a state that prevents it.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses specific limitations on the ma'dhun's legal statements. First, from al-Ziyadat, if the ma'dhun makes a claim that contradicts his own previous stance, it is not accepted, nor is his evidence, and the opponent is not required to take an oath. Then the passage discusses acknowledgment of a large debt to a stranger: if the ma'dhun acknowledges absolutely, it is valid and interpreted as a compensation for a transaction. Next, a ruling on selling a slave: if the buyer claims the slave is free and the ma'dhun confirms, the ma'dhun is not believed, and the buyer's manumission takes effect against the buyer himself. Finally, the passage examines a scenario where the ma'dhun acknowledges a debt while he is under interdiction (hajr) for various reasons like usurpation or breach of trust. If the master (rabb al-mal) denies that these acts were within the permission, the ma'dhun is not believed in any part, and the entire debt is binding. If the master confirms, only the usurpation is binding because it creates immediate liability, unlike other acts that he attributes to a time when he was prevented from trading. The key principle is that the ma'dhun's capacity to acknowledge is limited by his authority and the circumstances.

Key Terms
الزيادات (al-Ziyadat): A book by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, one of the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah.
ينتاقض (yantaqid): He contradicts himself (from naqd, contradition).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The claim is rejected, but evidence is still considered.
B) The claim is rejected, evidence is not accepted, and the opponent is not required to swear.
C) The claim is accepted but the opponent must swear.
D) The claim is suspended until clarification.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's self-contradictory claim is invalid with full procedural consequences: no evidence, no oath for the opponent.

Acknowledgment by the Ma'dhun: Absolute vs. Conditional
Ruling on absolute acknowledgment and slave sale confirmation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and conditional acknowledgment by a ma'dhun and apply the ruling of interpretation as compensation.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's absolute acknowledgment is valid and interpreted as compensation; conditional acknowledgment or agreement with a buyer's claim may not be believed.

Suppose a ma'dhun tells a stranger, 'I owe you two thousand dirhams.' Is this binding? The Hanafi school says yes if he states it absolutely, interpreting it as a compensation for a transaction. However, if he says it conditionally or confirms a buyer's claim that the sold slave is free, different rules apply. This lesson clarifies these nuanced rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, the ma'dhun acknowledges a debt to a stranger. Second, the ma'dhun sells his slave and the buyer claims the slave is free, and the ma'dhun confirms.
Both involve acknowledgment, but different outcomes.
2
Rule for absolute acknowledgment
If the ma'dhun acknowledges 'absolutely' (mutilaqan), it is valid and interpreted as compensation (mu'awadah). This means the debt is treated as arising from a transaction.
Absolute means no conditions attached.
3
Rule for conditional or contradictory acknowledgment
If the acknowledgment is conditional or qualified in a way that contradicts, the ruling may differ. The text does not explicitly state conditional cases, but the principle is that absolute is valid.
The contrast is with the next scenario.
4
Analyze the slave sale scenario
The ma'dhun sells his slave. The buyer says the slave is free, and the ma'dhun confirms. The ma'dhun is not believed (la yusaddaq), but the buyer's manumission is executed against the buyer himself.
The manumission binds the buyer, not the ma'dhun.
5
Understand the rationale
In the slave scenario, the ma'dhun cannot admit to a claim that would harm his master's property without proper evidence. His confirmation is against his own interest and thus not trusted.
The ma'dhun's interest is aligned with his master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Acknowledgment of Debt While Interdicted
Ruling on debt acknowledgment while interdicted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the binding effect of a ma'dhun's debt acknowledgment while interdicted, differentiating between master's denial and confirmation.

Core Idea
When a ma'dhun acknowledges a debt while interdicted, the master's response decides what binds: total denial makes all binding; confirmation binds only usurpation.

Imagine a ma'dhun who was interdicted for misusing trust property. He later admits he owed a debt from that period. The master has two options: deny that the acts were permitted, or confirm them. The Hanafi ruling, explained in this lesson, treats these options very differently. Understanding this rule is crucial for managing the ma'dhun's liabilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The ma'dhun acknowledges a debt that existed while he was interdicted (mahjur) due to specific causes: usurpation (ghasb), consumed trust (wadi'ah), mudarabah, or violated loan (ariyah).
Interdiction restricts his capacity.
2
Scenario one: Master denies permission
If the master (rabb al-mal) says 'All this was within your permission' (i.e., denies that the acts were unauthorized), the ma'dhun is not believed in any part, and the entire debt becomes binding on the ma'dhun.
The master's denial shifts the burden entirely to the ma'dhun.
3
Scenario two: Master confirms permission
If the master confirms that the acts were within the permission, then only the usurpation (ghasb) becomes binding, because usurpation creates immediate guarantee. Other debts are not binding because the ma'dhun attributed the acknowledgment to a state that prevents liability (the interdiction).
The distinction hinges on the nature of the cause.
4
Understand the reason for the distinction
Usurpation requires immediate compensation (al-ghasb yujibu al-daman li-l-hal), while other causes like consumed trust or mudarabah do not create an immediate obligation if the master had permitted the transaction. The ma'dhun's acknowledgment is attributed to a state (al-hal) that prevents liability for those causes.
Immediacy (li-l-hal) is key.
5
Apply the rule
Given a specific scenario, first determine if the master denies or confirms. Then apply the appropriate binding effect. If denied, all is binding. If confirmed, only usurpation is binding.
The ma'dhun's own credibility is secondary to the master's statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ma'dhun's Acknowledgment During Master's Illness: Conditions of Validity
Details the validity of the ma'dhun's acknowledgment when the master is ill and has debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a ma'dhun's acknowledgment of debt is valid during the master's fatal illness, distinguishing between health debts and sickness debts.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's acknowledgment is valid only if the master's health debts do not consume all assets; otherwise, it is void.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the legal capacity of a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) to acknowledge debts when his master is terminally ill. The validity hinges on the type of debt the master has—health debts or sickness debts—and on the sufficiency of the slave's own assets. We will dissect the three main scenarios presented from al-Mabsut and understand the underlying principle of creditor priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the master's debt type
Determine whether the master's debt is a health debt (dayn al-sihha) incurred during good health or a sickness debt (dayn al-marad) incurred during the fatal illness. This is the primary determinant.
Health debts take precedence over the slave's body and assets.
2
Assess the slave's financial position
Check if the slave has his own debts and whether his assets (body and what he holds) exceed his debts. This affects whether the acknowledgment can be valid.
The slave's body and held assets are considered together.
3
Apply the scenario classification
Match the facts to one of three scenarios: (a) master has health debt, slave none; (b) slave has debt, master none; (c) both have health debts. Each yields a different ruling.
The passage covers these three cases explicitly.
4
Determine validity and priority
Based on the scenario, rule whether the acknowledgment is valid and how debts are prioritized (health debts first).
If the master's health debt covers all assets, the acknowledgment is void.
5
Consider special cases: absconding or emancipation
Note Abu Yusuf's ruling: if the slave absconds, is interdicted, or sold, his debts become due immediately; if freed, they remain until term.
Emancipation does not cut off trade authority; it enhances it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا أقر المأذون في مرض مولاه قال في المبسوط وهو على وجهين أحدهما أن يقر العبد، والثاني في إقرارهما. فالأول إذا أقر العبد في مرض المولى وعلى المولى دين إن كان عليه دين الصحة بجميع ماله ورقبة العبد لا يصح إقرار العبد وإن كان على المولى دين المرض صح إقراره. ثم المسائل على أقسام أما الأول إذا كان على المولى دين الصحة ولا دين على العبد، أو على العبد ولا دين على المولى، أو على كل واحد منهما دين. الأول لو أقر المأذون في مرض مولاه بدين ولا دين عليه وعلى المولى دين الصحة جعل كأن المولى أقر في مرضه ويبدأ بدين الصحة كإقرار المولى على نفسه في هذه الحالة، وإن لم يكن على المولى دين في صحته فتداين في مرضه تخلصاً لأن إقرار العبد بالدين صحيح في حق غرمائه وإن تضمن إبطال حقه الثاني لو كان على العبد دين ولا دين على المولى في صحته فإقرار العبد صحيح لأن المأذون إنما يصير محجوراً عليه في مرض سيده إذا كان على السيد دين في الصحة يحيط بماله ورقبة العبد وما في يده فيصير العبد محجوراً حينئذ. الثالث إذا كان على كل واحد منهما دين للصحة فأقر العبد بدين في مرض مولاه فلا يخلو إما أن يكون رقبة العبد وما في يده لا يفضل عن دينه أو يفضل عن دينه ولا يفضل عن دين المولى أو يفضل عنهما، فإن لم يفضل عن دينه لا يصح إقراره لأن شغل ما في يده ورقبته يمنع صحة إقراره فإن فضل عن دينه وعلى المولى دين الصحة يكون الفاضل لغرماء صحة المولى، وأما إذا فضل عن دينهما فإنه يصح إقراره في ذلك الفاضل، ولو لم يكن على فأقر المولى في مرضه بألف على نفسه ثم أقر العبد بألف تحاصا في ثمن العبد، ولو أقر العبد أولاً ثم المولى بدئ بدين العبد. وفي المحيط قال أبو يوسف: إذا أبق العبد المأذون أو حجر عليه أو باعه سيده حل الدين الذي عليه مؤجلاً، وإن أعتقه لم يحل عليه الدين وكان إلى أجله لأن بالعتق لم تنقطع ولاية التجارة بل استفادها.
As for if the ma'dhun acknowledges during the illness of his master, he said in al-Mabsut: it has two aspects: one that the slave acknowledges, and the second regarding their acknowledgment. As for the first, if the slave acknowledges during the master's illness and the master has a debt: if the master has a health debt covering all his property and the slave's body, the slave's acknowledgment is not valid; but if the master has a sickness debt, his acknowledgment is valid. Then the issues are divided: first, if the master has a health debt and no debt on the slave; second, if the slave has a debt and no debt on the master; third, if each has a debt. First: if the ma'dhun acknowledges a debt during his master's illness and there is no debt on him while the master has a health debt, it is treated as if the master acknowledged in his illness, and the health debt is given precedence, like the master's acknowledgment on himself in this case. If the master had no debt in health but incurred debt in his illness to free himself, then the slave's acknowledgment of debt is valid against the slave's creditors even if it involves invalidating the master's right. Second: if the slave has a debt and the master has no health debt, the slave's acknowledgment is valid, because the ma'dhun only becomes interdicted during his master's illness if the master has a health debt that encompasses his property, the slave's body, and what is in his hand. Then the slave becomes interdicted at that time. Third: if each has a health debt and the slave acknowledges a debt during his master's illness, then it is either that the slave's body and what is in his hand do not exceed his debt, or exceed his debt but do not exceed the master's debt, or exceed both. If it does not exceed his debt, his acknowledgment is not valid because the occupation of what is in his hand and his body prevents the validity of his acknowledgment. If it exceeds his debt and the master has a health debt, the excess goes to the master's health creditors. If it exceeds both debts, then his acknowledgment is valid in that excess. And if there was no (debt on the master) and the master acknowledged a thousand on himself during his illness, then the slave acknowledged a thousand, they share proportionally in the price of the slave. If the slave acknowledged first and then the master, the slave's debt is given precedence. And in al-Muhit, Abu Yusuf said: If the ma'dhun slave absconds, or is interdicted, or his master sells him, the debt upon him becomes due immediately; if he is freed, the debt does not become due and remains until its term, because by emancipation the authority of trade is not cut off but rather he gains it.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a detailed classification of the validity of the ma'dhun's acknowledgment when his master is terminally ill. The ruling is taken from al-Mabsut. The key factor is whether the master has a 'health debt' (debt incurred during health) or a 'sickness debt' (debt incurred during the fatal illness). The slave's acknowledgment is void if the master's health debt consumes all assets including the slave's body; valid if the master's debt is only a sickness debt. Three sub-cases are analyzed: (1) master has health debt, slave has none: the acknowledgment is treated as if the master himself acknowledged during illness, and the health debt takes priority; (2) slave has debt, master has none: the slave's acknowledgment is valid because he is not interdicted during the master's illness unless the master's health debt exhausts assets; (3) both have health debts: the validity depends on whether the slave's assets exceed his own debt. The passage also discusses the order of priority when both master and slave acknowledge debts during the master's illness, and a quote from al-Muhit by Abu Yusuf about the impact of the slave's absconding, interdiction, sale, or emancipation on his debts. The overall theme is the interaction between the master's financial condition and the slave's capacity to acknowledge debts.

Key Terms
دين الصحة (dayn al-sihha): A debt incurred by the master while he was in good health, which has priority over the slave's body and assets in case of illness.
دين المرض (dayn al-marad): A debt incurred during the master's fatal illness, which is treated like a bequest and subject to different rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the ma'dhun's acknowledgment valid during the master's illness?
A) Only if the master has no debts.
B) Only if the slave has no debts.
C) It depends on the master's debt type and the sufficiency of assets.
D) It is always valid because the master is ill.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a ma'dhun's acknowledgment during the master's fatal illness depends primarily on whether the master's health debt exhausts all assets, and secondarily on the slave's own debt and asset sufficiency.

Marriage of a Slave Girl Purchased by an Authorized Slave
Explanation of a ruling from al-Muntaqa on marriage of a purchased slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that makes the marriage of a purchased slave girl to an authorized slave valid, and explain the legal consequences regarding sale and creditors.

Core Idea
No debt on the slave allows valid marriage; debt invalidates it.

In Hanafi fiqh, an authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) can engage in trade. If he purchases a slave girl, the master may marry her to him. The validity hinges on whether the slave has debt. This lesson unpacks that condition and its effects on ownership, sale, and creditor rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is the master marrying the purchased slave girl to his authorized slave. The critical condition stated in al-Muntaqa is that the slave must have no debt at the time of marriage.
The condition is explicit: 'wa la dain 'alayhi' (and no debt upon him).
2
State the ruling when condition is met
If the slave has no debt, the marriage is valid (jaza). The slave girl exits from the trade stock and becomes the slave's wife. The slave cannot sell her, and creditors cannot seize her because the master owns the slave's earnings.
Validity is conditioned on absence of debt.
3
State the ruling when condition is absent
If the slave has debt at the time of marriage, the marriage is not valid (lam yajuz al-nikah). The slave retains the right to sell the slave girl and her child's debt because both are his property.
Debt invalidates the marriage but does not nullify the slave's ownership.
4
Analyze the effect of subsequent debt payment
If the slave's debt is settled after the marriage, the marriage is retroactively considered valid, as if it occurred without debt. This shows the ruling is based on the state at the time of marriage but can be validated later.
The text says 'fa-huwa bi-manzilat tazwijihi wa la dain 'alayhi'.
5
Summarize the legal consequences
The marriage's validity affects the slave girl's status in trade and against creditors. The slave's capacity to sell her depends on whether the marriage was valid (no debt) or invalid (debt present).
Key distinction: valid marriage removes slave girl from trade; invalid marriage keeps her as trade property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طعاماً يسيراً ويضيف من يطعمه ويحط من الثمن بعيب ودينه متعلق برقبته يباع فيه أن زوجها من المولى ولا دين عليه لما قال في المنتقى : اشترى المأذون جارية ولا دين عليه فزوجها من المولى جاز وقد خرجت الجارية من التجارة، وليس له أن يبيعها ولا تباع للغرماء لو لحقه لأن المولى يملك إكساب عبده وإن كان عليه دين لم يجز النكاح، وله أن يبيعها ويبيع دین ولدها لأنهما ملك للعبد، وإن قضي دينه بعد التزويج فهو بمنزلة تزويجه ولا دين عليه اهـ .
Small food, and he hosts who feeds him, and he deducts from the price due to a defect, and his debt is attached to his person. He is sold in that he marries her to the master and no debt is upon him. This is according to what was said in al-Muntaqa: The authorized slave bought a slave girl and had no debt, so the master married her to him, and it is valid, and the slave girl has exited from trade. And he does not have the right to sell her, nor is she sold for creditors if they come after him, because the master owns the earnings of his slave. And if he had debt, the marriage is not valid. And he has the right to sell her and sell the debt of her child because both are the slave's property. And if his debt is settled after the marriage, it is like his marriage without debt. End.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the sharh that explains a specific scenario regarding an authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) who purchases a slave girl (jariya). The key condition is that the slave must have no debt at the time of purchase. In that case, the master may marry the slave girl to his own slave, and this marriage is valid. Once married, the slave girl is no longer part of the slave's trading stock, and the slave cannot sell her. Furthermore, the slave's creditors cannot seize her because she now belongs to the master (since the master married her to his slave, but ownership passes? Actually, the text says 'the master owns the earnings of his slave' but here the slave girl becomes the slave's property? Wait: the passage says 'the master married her to him' meaning the master marries the slave girl to his slave. So the slave girl becomes the slave's wife. She is no longer part of trade. The master cannot sell her? Actually, it says 'ليس له أن يبيعها' which means the slave cannot sell her, not the master. The slave's creditors cannot sell her. If the slave had debt, the marriage is invalid. The slave can still sell her and her child's debt because both are his property. If the debt is paid after marriage, it is considered as if the marriage occurred without debt. This ruling is taken from al-Muntaqa, a Hanafi source.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
الجارية (al-jariyah): A female slave (concubine or servant).
النكاح (al-nikah): Marriage contract.

Ruling on the Authorized Slave Making a Kitaba Contract
Explanation of why the authorized slave is not allowed to make a kitaba contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an authorized slave making a kitaba contract and explain the two proofs based on the distinction between trade and kitaba.

Core Idea
An authorized slave cannot make a kitaba contract because kitaba is not trade and is stronger than mere permission.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains why a slave who has been given permission to trade (ma'dhun) cannot unilaterally enter into a kitaba contract (a contract of gradual manumission). The reasoning hinges on the nature of kitaba as a separate legal act that is both distinct from trade and more powerful than a simple permission. We will unpack the two proofs given and the important exception when the master explicitly permits it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an authorized slave making a kitaba contract without the master's explicit permission. The ruling is that it is not allowed (haram) because kitaba is not covered by the permission to trade.
The ruling changes if the master explicitly permits it and there is no debt.
2
Analyze the first proof: Kitaba is not trade
Trade is defined as exchange of property for property (mubadala al-mal bil-mal). Kitaba is a contract aimed at removing the legal restriction (fak al-hajr) on the slave's capacity, not a commercial exchange. Therefore, permission to trade does not imply permission for kitaba.
This is a qiyas-based reasoning: the absence of the defining characteristic (trade) excludes kitaba from the scope of permission.
3
Analyze the second proof: Kitaba is stronger than permission
Kitaba grants immediate inviolability of the slave's hand (hurmat al-yad) in his possessions and eventual freedom, whereas permission only allows trade without those consequences. A thing (permission) cannot include what is above it (kitaba), so the slave cannot perform a stronger act under a weaker authorization.
This is a linguistic-legal principle: al-shay' la yatadammanu ma huwa fawqahu.
4
Identify the exception and the debt issue
The master can explicitly permit the kitaba, but only if the slave has no debt. According to al-Nihayah, even a small debt invalidates the kitaba, which the author finds problematic because a non-encumbering debt should not prevent entry into the master's ownership.
The author questions the blanket invalidity, pointing to an intra-Hanafi disagreement when the debt is encumbering.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement on encumbering debt
When the debt covers all the slave's assets (mustaghriq), Abu Hanifa holds that it prevents the slave from entering the master's ownership (so the master cannot validly permit kitaba), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold it does not prevent. This affects whether the master's permission can be effective.
The practical difference: if the slave later pays the debt and the master permits, the slave does not become free according to the author's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : (ولا يكاتب لأنه ليس من باب التجارة إذ هي مبادلة المال بالمال والبدل في قال الحال مقابل بفك الحجر فلم يكن من باب التجارة، ولأن الكتابة أقوى من الحجر لأن الكتابة توجب حرمة اليد في الحال وحرية الرقيق في المآل والإذن لا يوجب شيئاً من ذلك والشيء لا يتضمن ما هو فوقه إلا إذا أجازه المولى ولم يكن على العبد دين لأن الامتناع لحقه، فإن أجازه المولى جاز. وذكر في النهاية أنه إذا كان على العبد دين قليل أو كثير فكتابته باطلة وإن أجازهما لأن قيام الدين يمنعه من ذلك قل أو كثر وهذا مشكل، لأن الدين إذا لم يكن مستغرقاً لما في يده ورقبته لا يمنع الدخول في ملك المولى، وإنما الخلاف فيما إذا كان الدين مستغرقاً فعند الإمام يمنع من دخوله في ملك المولى، وعندهما لا يمنع. وإذا أدى المكاتب الدل إلى المولى قبل الإجازة ثم أجاز المولى لا يعتق ويسلم المقبوض للمولى لأنه كسب عبده .
He (the author) said: (And he does not make a kitaba contract) because it is not from the category of trade, since trade is the exchange of property for property, and the substitute in [؟] is in exchange for removing the restriction, so it is not from trade. And because kitaba is stronger than the permission (of trade), because kitaba necessitates inviolability of possession in the present and freedom in the future, while permission does not necessitate anything of that, and a thing does not include what is above it, except if the master permits it and there is no debt on the slave, because the prohibition is for his right. If the master permits it, it is valid. And it is mentioned in al-Nihayah that if the slave has debt, little or much, his kitaba is invalid even if both permit it, because the existence of debt prevents that, little or much. And this is problematic, because if the debt does not cover what is in his hand and his person, it does not prevent entering into the master's ownership. The disagreement is only when the debt covers all: with Imam (Abu Hanifa), it prevents entry into the master's ownership, while with the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it does not prevent. And if the kitaba slave pays the [debt?] to the master before permission, then the master permits, he does not become free, and the received property is delivered to the master because it is his slave's earnings.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that an authorized slave (ma'dhun) is not permitted to enter into a kitaba contract (a contract of gradual manumission) because kitaba is not a trade transaction. The author gives two reasons: first, kitaba is not a commercial exchange (mubadala al-mal bil-mal) but rather a contract aimed at removing restrictions (fak al-hajr). Second, kitaba is stronger than a mere permission to trade because kitaba grants immediate inviolability of the slave's possession and eventual freedom, whereas permission does not. Since a thing (permission) cannot include what is above it (kitaba), the slave cannot perform kitaba without the master's explicit permission. The master may permit it, but only if the slave has no debt; if the slave has debt, the kitaba is invalid according to al-Nihayah, regardless of the amount. The author finds this problematic because a non-encumbering debt should not prevent the slave from entering the master's ownership. He clarifies that the disagreement (Abu Hanifa vs. the two companions) only applies when the debt covers all the slave's assets. Finally, if the kitaba slave pays something to the master before the master permits, then the master later permits, the slave does not become free and the payment belongs to the master as the slave's earnings.

Key Terms
الكتابة (al-kitabah): A contract between a master and slave whereby the slave pays a sum over time in exchange for freedom.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): The slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
الحجر (al-hajr): Restriction or interdiction; here it refers to the inability of the slave to act without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the authorized slave not allowed to make a kitaba contract without the master's permission?
A) Because kitaba is a type of trade and the slave is only authorized for trade.
B) Because kitaba is not trade and is stronger than mere permission.
C) Because the slave must always obey the master and cannot enter contracts.
D) Because any debt on the slave makes kitaba invalid.

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave cannot unilaterally make a kitaba contract because it is not covered by trade permission and is a stronger legal act; explicit permission from the master is required, and if the slave has any debt, the kitaba becomes invalid according to the relied-upon view.

The Authorized Slave’s Inability to Free a Slave
Ruling on the authorized slave freeing a slave, with a qiyas argument and internal Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an authorized slave freeing a slave, explain the qiyas argument comparing it to kitaba, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding encumbering debt.

Core Idea
An authorized slave cannot free a slave because freeing is stronger than kitaba, which is already prohibited without permission.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why a slave with trade permission (madhun) is not allowed to free another slave. The author uses an a fortiori qiyas: freeing is even more significant than kitaba (a contract of manumission), so if kitaba requires permission, freeing certainly does. The ruling also covers conditions under the master's permission and an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of encumbering debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The passage begins: 'ولا يعتق' (he does not free). This is a general prohibition: the authorized slave may not free any slave, whether he has property or not.
The ruling is absolute; no distinction based on the slave's wealth.
2
Trace the qiyas argument
The reason given is 'لأنه فوق الكتابة فكان أولى بالامتناع' – freeing is above kitaba, so it is more deserving of prohibition. Recognize this as a qiyas jaliy: if kitaba (a lesser act) is prohibited without permission, then itq (a greater act) is even more prohibited.
The illa is that both are forms of manumission not part of trade, and itq is stronger.
3
Identify the condition for validity
The text permits the freeing if the master gives permission (أجازه المولى) and the slave has no encumbering debt. In that case, the freeing is valid as if accepting compensation.
Permission overrides the default prohibition, but only if the slave's assets are free of debt that covers all his property.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute
If the slave has encumbering debt (دين مستغرق), Imam Abu Hanifa holds the freeing does not take effect; Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it does take effect. The dispute hinges on whether the slave owns what is in his hand despite the debt.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the mufta bihi is usually Imam Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا يعتق أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان على مال أو لا لأنه فوق الكتابة فكان أولى بالامتناع، وإن أجازه المولى ولم يكن عليه مال ديناً جاز وكأنه قبل العوض إليه إن كان العتق على مال فإن كان عليه دين مستغرق لم ينفذ عند الإمام، وينفذ عندهما بناء على أنه يملك ما في يده أم لا، وقدمنا لو أقر بحرية طارئة أو أصلية فراجعه
He said: (And he does not free [a slave]) and he made it absolute, so it includes whether he has property or not, because it (freeing) is above kitaba, so it is more deserving of prohibition. And if the master permits it and he has no debt, it is valid, and it is as if he accepted the compensation to him if the freeing was for property. If he has encumbering debt, it does not take effect according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and it takes effect according to the two companions, based on whether he owns what is in his hand or not. And we previously presented that if he acknowledges fugitive or original freedom, then refer back to it.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling of an authorized slave freeing another slave (itq). The author states that the slave is not permitted to free anyone, whether he has property or not, because freeing is even stronger than kitaba (which was already prohibited without permission). The logic is a fortiori: if kitaba is prohibited, then outright freeing is even more so. If the master gives permission and the slave has no debt, the freeing is valid. If the slave has encumbering debt (mustaghriq), the freeing does not take effect according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but it does take effect according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) based on whether the slave owns what is in his hand. The author refers to a previous discussion about acknowledging freedom (through a claim).

Key Terms
العتق (al-'itq): The act of freeing a slave; manumission.
الدين المستغرق (al-dayn al-mustaghriq): Debt that covers all of the slave's assets and his person's value.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): Permission or ratification from the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the qiyas argument in the passage, why is an authorized slave prohibited from freeing a slave?
A) Because freeing is a form of trade that requires additional permission.
B) Because freeing is even stronger than kitaba, which is already prohibited without permission.
C) Because freeing reduces the master's property and the slave cannot compensate.
D) Because the slave is not a free person.

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave cannot free another slave without permission, and even with permission, encumbering debt may prevent the freeing, with a Hanafi dispute on the effect.

Loans and Gifts by the Authorized Slave: Prohibition and Customary Exception
Prohibition on gratuitous loans/gifts and exception for under a dirham

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the authorized slave giving loans and gifts, identify the threshold for prohibition, and apply the exception for small amounts customary in trade.

Core Idea
An authorized slave cannot give gratuitous loans or gifts, but small amounts customary in trade are allowed.

In Hanafi fiqh, the authorized slave (madhun) is permitted to engage in trade for his master's benefit. However, his authority does not extend to gratuitous transfers like loans and gifts, which are not part of trade. This lesson explains the ruling, highlights the important exception for small value items that are part of normal commercial practice, and clarifies the threshold one dirham.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the subject
The act is giving a loan (qard) or a gift (hibah) by the authorized slave (madhun). Identify that this is a gratuitous transfer, not part of trade.
The slave is authorized only for trade, not for philanthropy.
2
State the general ruling
The general ruling is prohibitions: the slave cannot give a loan or gift as a pure gratuitous donation. The text says 'he does not give a loan' and 'he does not give a gift' absolutely.
This is makruh tahrimi (prohibited) because it exceeds his authority.
3
Identify the exception for small amounts
The passage provides an exception: if the amount is less than a dirham, it is permissible. This is because such small gifts and loans are customary in trade and necessary for business relationships.
The dirham is about 2.75g of silver; modern equivalent may be a small amount of money.
4
Locate the basis for the exception
The exception is based on the custom of merchants (urf al-tujjar). The text cites al-Muhit stating that gifts of less than a dirham are from the practice of trade (min sania al-tijara).
Custom (urf) is a recognized source in Hanafi fiqh, especially when it does not contradict a primary text.
5
Apply the threshold
The key condition for prohibition is the value reaching or exceeding one dirham. Below that, the act is permissible as part of trade practice. Apply this test to determine validity.
If the slave gives a gift worth a dirham or more, it is invalid and he may be liable for the value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا يقرض) تبرع ابتداء لا يملك وينبغي أن يقال إذا كان درهماً فصاعداً فأما ما دونه فيجوز أن وهو أو لا لأنه رحمه الله : ( ولا يهب أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان بعوض يقرض كما في الهبة. قال تبرع ابتداء وانتهاء. أطلق في منع الهبة فشمل ما قيمته درهم وما دونه. وفي المحيط : ولا فصاعداً ويجوز هبة ما دون الدرهم لأنه من صنيع التجارة هذا إذا بلغت قيمته درهم يهب لأنه لا بد للتجارة منه ليعرف ويميل قلب الناس إليه اهـ .
He said: (And he does not give a loan) as a gratuitous donation initially; he does not own [that power]. And it should be said: if it is a dirham or more, but what is less than that, it is permissible? And he said: (And he does not give a gift) he made it absolute, so it includes whether it is with compensation or not, as in a loan? He said: a gratuitous donation from beginning to end. He made the prohibition of gift absolute, so it includes what its value is a dirham or less. And in al-Muhit: and not above, and it is permissible to give a gift of less than a dirham because it is from the practice of trade. This is if its value reaches a dirham, he gives a gift, because trade cannot do without it for recognition and to incline people's hearts to him. End.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage discusses two prohibitions: the authorized slave cannot give loans (qard) and cannot give gifts (hiba) as pure gratuities. However, there is an exception for small amounts customary in trade. The text initially says the prohibition is absolute, but then it qualifies: giving a loan of less than a dirham (about 2.75 grams of silver) may be permissible because it is considered part of trade practice. Similarly, gifts of less than a dirham are allowed. The source al-Muhit is cited to support that a gift of less than a dirham is permissible because it is from the practices of merchants to attract customers. The condition is that if the value is a dirham or above, it becomes a significant gratuitous donation and is not allowed.

Key Terms
القرض (al-qard): A loan given without interest; here it is considered a gratuitous transfer.
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; a gratuitous transfer of ownership.
الدرهم (al-dirham): A silver coin; a unit of currency historically used as a measure of value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Half a dirham
B) One dirham
C) Two dirhams
D) Any amount is prohibited

Key Takeaway
The authorized slave may give small gifts and loans (under one dirham) as part of trade custom, but larger gratuitous transfers are prohibited.

Authorized Slave’s Permission to Give Small Hospitality
Explanation of permission for authorized slave to give small hospitality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an authorized slave giving small amounts of food and hosting guests, and apply the condition of proportionality based on trade capital.

Core Idea
Small hospitality by an authorized slave is permitted by custom, provided it does not harm the master’s interest.

In this passage, al-Marghinani explains that an authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) may give small amounts of food as gifts and host guests as part of customary trade practices. The ruling is based on need and custom ('urf), and the amount allowed is proportionate to the trade capital. We will examine the reasoning and the practical guidelines set by later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the authorized slave giving a small amount of food as a gift or hosting a guest. The ruling is permissible (mubah) according to the Hanafi school.
This is not a wajib or sunnah, but a pure permission rooted in custom.
2
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The proof is the custom of merchants (urf al-tujjar): traders need such hospitality to attract customers (literally 'hearts of emigrants'). This is not a textual proof but a rational one based on need.
The author uses the term 'because traders need it' indicating talil (reasoning by custom).
3
Understand the condition of proportionality
The hospitality must be small (yasir) and not harm the master's interest. The text distinguishes between daily and monthly provisions: if master gives daily food, inviting is fine; if monthly, it may harm. Thus, the condition is that the act must be customary and not excessive.
Later scholars like Muhammad ibn Salamah al-Balkhi quantified: hospitality up to 10 dirhams for 10,000 capital, or a small coin for 10 capital.
4
Apply the same principle to analogous cases
The same custom-based permission extends to a woman giving a loaf from her husband's house without permission, and to the authorized slave lending small items (mount, garment). The key is that such acts are customary and minimal.
This shows the Hanafi reliance on 'urf (custom) in commercial transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ويهدي طعاماً يسيراً ويضيف من يطعمه) لأن التجار يحتاجون إليه لاستجلاب قلوب المهاجرين، وعن الثاني المحجور عليه إذا دفع إليه المولى قوت يومه فدعى بعض رفقائه عليه فلا بأس به بخلاف ما إذا دفع قوت شهر لأنهم إذا أكلوه بضر بحال المولى ولا يمكن أن يقدر الضيافة بتقدير لأنه يختلف باختلاف الناس واختلاف المال، ولا بأس للمرأة أن تتصدق من بيت زوجها بالرغيف بدون إذن الزوج لأنه مأذون فيه عادة. قال محمد: ويتصدق المأذون بالرغيف ونحوه واستحسنوا ذلك في الطعام وفيما إذا أعار رجلاً دابة ليركبها أو ثوباً يلبسه لا بأس به. ولم يذكر في الكتاب الضيافة اليسيرة وقدرها محمد بن سلمة البلخي فقال : إن كان مال التجارة عشر آلاف فالضيافة عشرة دراهم، وإن كان مال التجارة عشرة فالضيافة بدائق ؛ كذا في المحيط .
He said: (And he gives a little food as a gift and hosts who feeds him?) because traders need it to attract the hearts of emigrants. And from the second: the interdicted one, if the master gives him his daily sustenance, then he invites some of his companions to eat, there is no harm in it, unlike if he gives monthly sustenance, because if they eat it, it harms the master's condition. And it is not possible to estimate hosting with a measurement because it varies with different people and different wealth. And there is no harm for a woman to give charity from her husband's house with a loaf without the husband's permission because it is customarily permitted. Muhammad said: The authorized slave gives charity with a loaf and the like, and they deemed it good in food. And also if he lends a man a mount to ride or a garment to wear, there is no harm. And the book did not mention the measure of small hospitality, and Muhammad ibn Salamah al-Balkhi said: If the trade capital is ten thousand, then hospitality is ten dirhams; if the trade capital is ten, then hospitality is a [small coin]; as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage explains that the authorized slave is permitted to give small amounts of food as gifts and to host guests. The reason given is that merchants need to do this to attract customers (literally 'hearts of emigrants' but likely means people). A distinction is made between daily and monthly provisions: if the master gives the slave his daily food, the slave may invite others; but if he gives monthly provisions, inviting others would harm the master. The amount of hospitality is not fixed in the original text (al-kitab), but later scholars like Muhammad ibn Salamah al-Balkhi provided examples: if the trade capital is 10,000 dirhams, hospitality up to 10 dirhams is allowed; if capital is 10 dirhams, hospitality is a small amount. This shows the principle that hospitality should be proportionate. The passage also mentions that a woman may give charity from her husband's house with a loaf without permission as it is customary. Similarly, the authorized slave may donate a loaf or similar. Lending small items like a mount or garment is also allowed.

Key Terms
الضيافة (al-diyafah): Hospitality; hosting guests with food, considered a customary merchant practice.
الطعام اليسير (al-ta'am al-yasir): Small amount of food; minimal hospitality.
المحجور عليه (al-mahjur 'alayh): The person under interdiction; here likely the authorized slave (though he is not fully interdicted).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 10 dirhams
B) 100 dirhams
C) 1 dirham
D) A loaf of bread

Key Takeaway
The authorized slave may give small hospitality and gifts as customary trade practice, provided it is proportionate and does not harm the master's interest.

Attachment of the Authorized Slave's Debt: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute over debt attachment of authorized slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the attachment of the authorized slave's debt and explain the thamarah al-khilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools.

Core Idea
The authorized slave's debt attaches to his person and earnings; his person can be sold if earnings fail.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the Hanafi view that the debt of an authorized slave (al-'abd al-ma'dhun) is attached to his person (raqabah) as well as his earnings (kasb). Imam al-Shafi'i and Zafar held that it attaches only to earnings. The dispute has practical consequences for creditors and the master, particularly on whether the slave's person can be sold to satisfy debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate the Hanafi statement 'wa dainuhu muta'alliq bi-raqabatihi' and the opposing view of al-Shafi'i and Zafar that it is attached only to earnings.
Note that Zafar, though Hanafi in other matters, agrees with Shafi'i here.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the qiyas: the debt is likened to debts of consumption, dower, and maintenance, which are attached to the person. Understand the reasoning behind this analogy.
The proof is based on the nature of the debt as an obligation that became apparent in favor of the master (or rather, third parties).
3
Examine the policy rationale
Note the arguments of repelling harm (daf' al-darar) and encouraging trade, which support attaching the debt to the person.
This reflects the Hanafi concern for public interest (maslahah).
4
Understand the practical sequence
The passage states that earnings are sold first because it is easier on the master; if no earnings, the person is sold. This clarifies the order of recourse.
Al-'Inayah emphasizes that attachment to person is secondary to earnings.
5
Consider the rule on pre-existing takings
Read the al-Muhit excerpt: if the master takes some of the slave's earnings before the debt arises, that amount is secure for the master and not taken by creditors.
This protects the master's prior rights over the slave's earnings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ودينه متعلق برقبته وهذا عندنا. وقال الإمام الشافعي وزفر: يتعلق بالكسب لا بالرقبة فلا تباع رقبته ويباع كسبه بالإجماع. ولنا أن هذا دين ظهر وجوبه في حق المولى فيتعلق برقبته كدين الاستهلاك والمهر ونفقة الزوجات. وفي تعليقه برقبته دفع الضرر عن الناس وحامل لهم على المعاملة وبه يحصل مقصود المولى، وتعلقه بكسبه لا ينافي تعليقه برقبته فيتعلق بهما جميعاً، ويبدأ ببيع كسبه لأنه أهون على المولى مع بقاء حق الغرماء. قال في العناية : فإن لم يكن له كسب تعلق الدين برقبته اهـ. فلو قال المؤلف وديونه متعلقة بكسبه ورقبته لكان أولى وأكثر فائدة لأنه يفيد تأخر تعلقه بالرقبة عن الكسب إن كان ويفيد تعليقين. قال في المحيط : وإذا أخذ المولى شيئاً من كسب عبده المأذون ثم لحقه دين سلم لمولاه ما أخذه وإن كان عليه دين يوم أخذ قليلاً أو أكثر يسلم للمولى ما أخذه،
He said: (And his debt is attached to his person) and this is according to us (Hanafis). Imam al-Shafi'i and Zafar said: it is attached to earnings, not to the person, so his person is not sold, and his earnings are sold by consensus. And our proof: this is a debt whose obligation became apparent in favor of the master, so it is attached to his person like the debt of consumption, dower, and maintenance of wives. And in attaching it to his person there is repelling harm from people and encouraging them to deal, and thereby the master's purpose is achieved. And its attachment to his earnings does not negate its attachment to his person, so it is attached to both. And one begins by selling his earnings because it is easier for the master while preserving the right of creditors. And it is said in al-'Inayah: If he has no earnings, the debt is attached to his person. End. So if the author said 'and his debts are attached to his earnings and his person' it would be better and more beneficial because it indicates the delay of attachment to the person after earnings, and it indicates two attachments. And it is said in al-Muhit: If the master takes something from the earnings of his authorized slave, then debt later attaches to him, what he took is secure for the master, even if he had debt on the day he took it, little or much, what he took is secure for the master.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage addresses the crucial question of whether the authorized slave's debts (incurred through trade) are attached to his person (raqabah) or only to his earnings (kasb). The Hanafi school holds that the debt is attached to his person, meaning the slave can be sold to pay off the debt if his earnings are insufficient. Imam al-Shafi'i and Zafar (a Hanafi dissenter) hold that it is only attached to earnings, so the slave's person cannot be sold. The Hanafi proof: this debt is similar to debts like mahr and maintenance which become obligations on the slave in favor of the master? Actually, the text says 'in favor of the master' but that seems odd; it likely means the debt is owed to third parties. The argument is that attaching the debt to the person prevents harm to people and encourages them to deal with the slave, which is the master's purpose. The debt attaches to both earnings and person, but earnings are sold first because it is easier for the master. Al-'Inayah clarifies that if there are no earnings, the debt attaches to the person for sale. The author suggests the matn would be better if it explicitly mentioned both attachments. Finally, al-Muhit adds that if the master takes some of the slave's earnings before the debt arises, that amount is secure for the master and not taken by creditors.

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqabah): The person of the slave; his physical being considered as property.
الكسب (al-kasb): Earnings acquired by the slave through trade or labor.
دين الاستهلاك (dayn al-istihlak): Debt arising from consumption of property, similar to a liability for destroying property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debt is written off.
B) The slave's person is sold to pay the debt.
C) The master must pay the debt.
D) The slave must work until the debt is paid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the authorized slave's debt attaches to both his earnings and his person, allowing sale of the slave if earnings are insufficient, whereas the Shafi'i school limits attachment to earnings only.

Discount by a Ma'dhun Slave for a Defect: Scope and Limits
Ruling with proof on ma'dhun slave's discount for defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a ma'dhun slave's discount for a defect and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa versus the Sahibayn on excessive discount.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun slave may discount the price for a defect only to the extent of the defect's value, as per trade custom; any excessive discount is disputed.

This lesson examines a detailed passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq that clarifies the scope of a ma'dhun slave's authority to reduce the price when a defect is discovered. The commentator expands on the matn's absolute statement, revealing restrictions tied to trade custom and an intra-school disagreement over excessive discounts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states 'he deducts from the price due to a defect.' Recognize that 'he' refers to the ma'dhun slave and that the default ruling is permissibility (mubah).
2
Locate the restriction to custom
The commentator clarifies that the absolute statement is restricted to a discount equal to the defect's reduction in value, in line with trade custom. A discount without defect or excessive discount is considered pure donation and invalid.
Custom ('urf) is a key determinant in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
When the discount exceeds the defect's value to the point of gross disparity (ghabn fahish), Abu Hanifa permits it, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad prohibit it, analogizing it to a sale the slave cannot lawfully execute.
The Sahibayn argue that the slave does not own the item to make such a transaction.
4
Consider the context of interdiction and acknowledgment
The ruling applies before and after interdiction. If the slave acknowledges a defect after interdiction, it does not bind him. But if he refuses an oath and loses, the return is valid, and he may then make the discount.
The slave's capacity changes with interdiction (hajr).
5
Apply to the ma'dhun slave's option after a returned defective item
In a case where a judge returns a defective slave-girl to the ma'dhun slave, and later a new defect appears, the slave has the option to rescind the sale or keep it without claiming reduction, illustrating the limits of his authority.
This shows that the discount is not automatically available; it depends on the slave's choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ويحط من الثمن بعيب) أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان قدر العيب أو أكثر أو أقل وهو مقيد بما إذا كان قدره فلو قال بقدره لكان أولى لأنه من صنيع التجار. وقيدنا بكون الحط أنظر له من قبول العيب بخلاف الحط من غير عيب أو الحط أكثر من العادة لأنه تبرع محض بعد تمام العقد وهو ليس من صنيع التجار فلا ضرورة إليه بخلاف المحاباة ابتداء لأنه قد يحتاج إليه التاجر، وله أن يؤجل في دين وجب له من عادة التجار، وفي المحيط فأما إذا كان المحطوط أكثر مما يخص العيب من الثمن بحيث لا يتغابن في مثله هل يجوز ؟ لم يذكره في الكتاب واختلفوا فيه؛ فقيل يجوز عند الإمام، وعندهما لا يجوز لأن الحط بمنزلة البيع والشراء وهو لا يملكه بالغبن الفاحش عندهما، وعنده يملكه. وقيل لا يجوز بالإجماع لأن الحط ليس بتجارة اهـ. أطلق في قوله فشمل قبل الحجر وبعده، وأطلق العيب فشمل ما إذا أقر به أو ثبت. قال في المنتقى : باع العبد المأذون عبداً في تجارته ثم حجر عليه مولاه ثم وجد المشتري بالعبد عيباً فالخصم في الرد بالعيب هو العبد وإن أقر العبد بالعيب لم يلزمه، وإن نكل عن اليمين فقضى عليه جاز اهـ. فإذا كان خصماً ملك الحط. وفي المحيط قال محمد في الأصل: إذا باع العبد المأذون عيناً وأطلع المشتري على عيب يحدث مثله وخاصم المأذون في ذلك فقبله من غير قضاء بلا يمين ولا بينة فقبوله جائز، ولو أن عبداً مأذوناً باع من رجل جارية فقبضها المشتري فوجد بها عيباً فرد القاضي الجارية على العبد وأخذ منه الثمن ثم إن العبد بعد وجد بالجارية عيباً حدث عند المشتري ولم يعلم به العبد وقت الرد ولا علم القاضي بذلك فالمأذون بالخيار إن شاء نقض البيع ورد الجارية على المشتري وأخذ منه الثمن إلا مقدار العيب الذي كان عنده، وإن شاء أجاز البيع وأمسك الجارية ولم يرجع على المشتري بنقصان العيب اهـ. ولو قال ويحط من العوض لكان أولى لأنه يشمل ما إذا باع سلعة بسلعة كان يحط منه إذا كان مكيلاً أو موزوناً، ومن القيمة إذا كان قيمياً.
He (the author) said: And he deducts from the price due to a defect. He (the commentator) said: He made it absolute so it includes if it is equal to the defect, more, or less, but it is restricted to when it is equal to it. If he had said 'by its amount' it would have been better because it is the practice of merchants. And we restricted it because the deduction is better for him than accepting the defect, unlike a deduction without defect or a deduction more than custom because it is pure donation after completion of the contract and it is not from the practice of merchants, so there is no need for it, unlike a discount at the start because the merchant may need it. And he may defer a debt owed to him according to trade custom. In al-Muhit: If the deducted amount is more than what the defect merits from the price to the extent that no one would overlook such difference, is it permissible? He did not mention it in the book and they differed: it is said it is permissible according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not permissible because deduction is like sale and purchase, and he does not own it with gross fraud according to them, but according to him he owns it. And it is said it is not permissible by consensus because deduction is not trade. End. He made absolute in his statement so it includes before interdiction and after, and he made the defect absolute so it includes if he acknowledges it or it is established. In al-Muntaqa: If a ma'dhun slave sold a slave in his trade then his master placed him under interdiction, then the buyer found a defect in the slave, then the opponent in the return due to defect is the slave. If the slave acknowledges the defect it does not bind him, and if he refuses the oath and the judge rules against him it is permissible. So if he is the opponent, he owns the deduction. In al-Muhit, Muhammad said in al-Asl: If a ma'dhun slave sold an item and the buyer disclosed a defect that typically appears, and the slave disputed with him over that and he accepted it without a judgment, oath, or evidence, his acceptance is valid. And if a ma'dhun slave sold a slave-girl to a man, and he took possession of her, then found a defect in her, and the judge returned the slave-girl to the slave and took the price from him, then after that the slave found in the slave-girl a defect that occurred with the buyer and he did not know of it at the time of return nor did the judge know, then the ma'dhun has the option: if he wishes he rescinds the sale and returns the slave-girl to the buyer and takes the price from him except the amount of the defect that was with him, and if he wishes he affirms the sale and keeps the slave-girl and does not seek a reduction from the buyer for the defect. End. And if he had said 'and deducts from the price' it would have been better because it includes if he sells a commodity for a commodity he deducts from it if it is measured or weighed, and from the value if it is a fungible.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is a ruling_with_proof passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn states: 'He deducts from the price due to a defect.' The commentator explains that the ruling is absolute, covering cases where the defect equals, exceeds, or is less than the discount, but it is actually restricted to a discount equal to the defect's reduction in value, as per trade custom. He recommends the author should have said 'by its amount' for clarity. The deduction is preferable to returning the item because of the hassle. A discount without defect or excessive discount is not allowed as it is pure donation after the contract, not trade custom. However, an initial discount in pricing is permissible. The slave may also defer a debt according to custom. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute about a discount exceeding the defect's value: according to Abu Hanifa it is permissible, but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad it is not because it constitutes a sale/purchase and the slave does not own the item to make such a grossly fraudulent transaction. Another view says it is impermissible by consensus. The commentator then addresses that the ruling applies before and after the master's interdiction, and whether the defect is acknowledged or proven. He cites al-Muntaqa for a case where the slave sold a slave, then was interdicted, and the buyer finds a defect; the slave is the opponent in the return claim. If the slave acknowledges the defect it does not bind him (since acknowledgment after interdiction is not valid). But if he refuses the oath and loses, the return is valid. If the slave is the opponent, he may make the discount. He also cites Muhammad's ruling from al-Asl about a slave selling an item and accepting a return for a defect without court order. Also a case where the judge returns a slave-girl and later the slave discovers a new defect: the slave has the option to rescind or keep the sale without claiming reduction. The commentator ends by preferring the wording 'deducts from the price' (al-'iwad) to include commodity exchanges and fungibles.

Key Terms
الحط (al-hatt): Reduction of price by the seller due to a defect
العيب (al-'ayb): Defect in the sold item that reduces its value
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): Slave permitted by master to engage in trade

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the default ruling if a ma'dhun slave deducts more from the price than the defect merits?
A) It is always valid because he is a merchant.
B) It is valid only according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
C) It is invalid by consensus.
D) It is valid only if the buyer agrees.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave's discount for a defect is limited to the defect's proportion according to trade custom; any excess is subject to dispute among the Hanafi imams.
