Debt of the Ma'dhun Slave: Attached to Person and Earnings
Proves Hanafi view that debt attaches to slave's person, not just earnings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the debt of a ma'dhun slave attaches to both his earnings and his person, and explain the qiyas used to support it.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun slave's debt attaches to both earnings and person; earnings sold first, then the slave himself.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on Kanz al-Daqa'iq and addresses a key dispute: does the debt of a ma'dhun (authorized) slave attach only to his earnings or also to his person? The Hanafi school holds that it attaches to both, relying on qiyas to other debts that bind the slave's person. Imam al-Shafi'i and the Hanafi Zufar restrict it to earnings. The text then explores practical implications through detailed scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
The matn states 'His debt is attached to his person (raqabah).' Recognize that this is a contested point: the Hanafi school (including Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds attachment to both person and earnings; al-Shafi'i and Zufar limit it to earnings only.
Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl was a disciple of Abu Hanifa who sometimes differed.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof (qiyas)
The text presents a qiyas: this debt is like debts of consumption, mahr, and maintenance because they all become obligatory in favor of the master/master’s role. Since those debts attach to the slave's person (the slave can be sold to pay them), the same applies here. Also, attaching the debt to the person promotes public benefit (deflects harm, encourages dealings).
The illa (effective cause) is that the debt is a claim that obligates the master's property.
3
Note the opponent's view and its rebuttal
Al-Shafi'i and Zufar argue that only earnings (kasb) are subject to debt, not the person. The Hanafi response: attachment to earnings does not negate attachment to the person; both are possible. The text asserts that attachment to both is more protective of creditors and achieves the master's purpose.
The rebuttal is logical: 'la yanafi' (does not negate).
4
Work through the practical scenarios from al-Muhit
The text details: if the master takes earnings before any debt, it is valid; if debts exist, the master's taking is still valid but proceeds from sale of the slave are shared proportionally. Scenarios of absolution by a creditor before/after debt attachment illustrate when the master's taking remains valid.
Key principle: prior to debt attachment, the master owns earnings; after, earnings are shared.
5
Understand the ruling's application
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position: debt attaches to both, starting with earnings. This means if the slave has no earnings, the slave can be sold (person sold). This is consistent with the Hanafi view that the slave's person is property.
Al-'Inayah explicitly says: 'If no earnings, debt attaches to the person.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ودينه متعلق برقبته وهذا عندنا. وقال الإمام الشافعي وزفر: يتعلق بالكسب لا بالرقبة فلا تباع رقبته ويباع كسبه بالإجماع. ولنا أن هذا دين ظهر وجوبه في حق المولى فيتعلق برقبته كدين الاستهلاك والمهر ونفقة الزوجات. وفي تعليقه برقبته دفع الضرر عن الناس وحامل لهم على المعاملة وبه يحصل مقصود المولى، وتعلقه بكسبه لا ينافي تعليقه برقبته فيتعلق بهما جميعاً، ويبدأ ببيع كسبه لأنه أهون على المولى مع بقاء حق الغرماء. قال في العناية : فإن لم يكن له كسب تعلق الدين برقبته اهـ. فلو قال المؤلف وديونه متعلقة بكسبه ورقبته لكان أولى وأكثر فائدة لأنه يفيد تأخر تعلقه بالرقبة عن الكسب إن كان ويفيد تعليقين. قال في المحيط : وإذا أخذ المولى شيئاً من كسب عبده المأذون ثم لحقه دين سلم لمولاه ما أخذه وإن كان عليه دين يوم أخذ قليلاً أو أكثر يسلم للمولى ما أخذه، لم يفده السيد وقسم ثمنه بالحصص وما بقي طولب به بعد عتقه ويحجر بحجر وإن ما أخذ من كسبه . ويظهر ذلك فيما إذا لحقه دين آخر يرد المولى جميع ما كان أخذه لأنا لو جعلنا بعضه مشغولاً بقدر الديون وجب على المولى رد قدر المشغول على الغريم الأول، فإذا أخذه كان للغريم الثاني أن يشاركه فيه إن كان دينهما سواء، وكان للغريم الأول أن يرجع بما أخذه منه على السيد، وإذا أخذ منه ثانياً كان للغريم الآخر أن يشاركه ثم وثم إلى أن يأخذ منه جميع كسبه. ولو أخذ المولى من المأذون ضريبة مثله وعليه دين سلمت للمولى استحساناً لأن للمولى أن يستخدم المأذون لأن المنافع باقية على ملكه، فإذا شرط عليه غلة المثل فقد ترك الخدمة عوضاً عنها فكان ما أخذه عوضاً بخلاف ما إذا زاد على غله لأنه أخذه بغير عوض، عليه خمسمائة وفي يده عبد قيمته ألف فأخذ مولاه ثم لحقه دين ألف ثم أراد إبراء الأول العبد دين عن دينه لم يسلم لمولى ما أخذه أو لو أبرأه قبل الحوق الدين سلم للمولى ما أخذه كله وكان كسبه فارغاً عن الدين فملك المولى كسبه وخرج المأخوذ عن كونه كسب العبد بخلاف ما إذا أبرأه قبل لحوق الدين فقد أبرأه بعدما تعلق به حق الغرماء فصار مشغولاً، ولو لم يبرئه الأول ولكن قال لم يكن لي على العبد دين قليل ولا كثير بعدما لحقه الدين الثاني يسلم للمولى ما أخذه كله لأنه لما قال لم يكن لي عليه دين وأقر بذلك كاذباً فقد كذب العبد في إقراره فبطل إقراره من الأصل فظهر أنه لم يكن عليه دين فصح أخذ المولى وبالإبراء لم يظهر أنه لم يكن لأن الإبراء إسقاط بعد الوجوب فلم يصح أخذه، ولو كان المولى صدق عبده حيث أقر للأول بالدين ثم لحقه دين الثاني ثم قال الأول لم يكن لي له عليه شيء لم يسلم للمولى ما قبض لأن المولى لما صدق عبده في الدين فقد أقر أن ما أخذه كان مشتركاً بين الأول والثاني فقد أقر لرجلين بخلاف ما إذا صدق المولى الغريم في قوله لا دين وكذبه العبد لا يصح إقرار العبد في حق ما في يد المولى لأنه إقرار بما ليس في يده. وقوله وديونه متعلقة» صادقة بدين للمولى أو لابنه أو لأبيه أو لابن العبد الحر أو لأبيه أو لأجنبي وقد قدمنا بيانه .
He (the author) said: (His debt is attached to his person, and this is according to us. Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar said: It is attached to earnings, not to the person, so his person cannot be sold and his earnings are sold by consensus. Our proof: This is a debt the obligation of which has become apparent in favor of the master, so it is attached to his person like the debt of consumption, marriage payment, and maintenance of wives. And attaching it to his person repels harm from people and encourages dealings, and thus the master's purpose is achieved. And its attachment to his earnings does not negate its attachment to his person, so it attaches to both, and the sale of his earnings is begun because it is easier for the master while preserving the rights of creditors. Al-'Inayah said: If he has no earnings, the debt attaches to his person. End. If the author had said 'and his debts are attached to his earnings and his person', it would have been better and more beneficial because it indicates the delay of attachment to the person after earnings if any, and it indicates two attachments. In al-Muhit: If the master takes something from the earnings of his ma'dhun slave, then debt befalls him, it is valid for the master what he took, even if there was a debt on the day he took little or much, it is valid for the master what he took, if the master did not pay, his price is divided proportionally and what remains is demanded after his emancipation, and he is placed under interdiction with interdiction, and what he took from earnings... This appears when another debt befalls him: the master returns all he had taken because if we consider part of it as occupied by the debts, it would be obligatory for the master to return the occupied amount to the first creditor. So if he took it, the second creditor can share it if their debts are equal, and the first creditor can claim back what he took from the master. And if he took from him a second time, the other creditor can share, and so on until he takes all his earnings. And if the master took a tax similar to his earnings and there is debt, it is valid for the master by istihsan because the master may use the ma'dhun, as benefits remain in his ownership. So if he conditioned a similar yield on him, he left service in exchange for it, so what he took is compensation, unlike if it exceeds his yield because he took it without compensation. He owes five hundred, and he has a slave worth a thousand, so his master took it, then a debt of one thousand befalls him, then the first wishes to absolve the slave of debt for his debt, it is not valid for the master what he took. Or if he absolved him before the debt occurred, it is valid for the master what he took entirely, and his earnings were free of debt, so the master owned his earnings and the taken amount left being the slave's earnings, unlike if he absolved him before the occurrence of debt, he absolved him after the creditors' right attached, so it became occupied. And if the first did not absolve him but said: 'I had no debt upon the slave, little or much' after the second debt befell him, it is valid for the master what he took entirely, because when he said 'I had no debt upon him' and acknowledged falsely, he made the slave's acknowledgment false, so his acknowledgment is nullified from the root, and it appears there was no debt, so the master's taking is valid. But by absolution it does not appear that there was none, because absolution is a dropping after obligation, so his taking is not valid. And if the master confirmed his slave where he acknowledged the debt to the first, then the second debt befell him, then the first said 'I had nothing upon him', it is not valid for the master what he took, because when the master confirmed the slave in the debt, he acknowledged that what he took was shared between the first and second, so he acknowledged to two men. Unlike if the master confirmed the creditor in saying 'no debt' and the slave lied, the slave's acknowledgment is not valid regarding what is in the master's hand because it is an acknowledgment of what is not in his hand. And his statement 'and his debts are attached' applies to a debt owed to the master, or his son, or his father, or to the free slave's son, or to his father, or to a stranger, and we have explained it previously.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is an inter_madhhab_dispute from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn states: 'His (the ma'dhun slave's) debt is attached to his person (raqabah).' This is the Hanafi position. Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar (a Hanafi disciple who differed) hold that debt attaches only to earnings (kasb), not the person. The Hanafi proof is: This debt has become obligatory in favor of the master, so it attaches to the person like the debts of consumption (e.g., if the slave consumed someone's property), the marriage payment (if the slave married someone), and maintenance of wives. Attaching it to the person repels harm from people dealing with the slave and encourages transactions, achieving the master's purpose. Attachment to earnings does not preclude attachment to the person, so it attaches to both, but earnings are sold first as it is easier on the master. Al-'Inayah adds: If no earnings, debt attaches directly to the person. The commentator wishes the author had said 'attached to earnings and person' for clarity. He then cites al-Muhit for detailed cases about the master taking earnings before debts arise: if the master takes earnings before any debt, it is valid for him. If debts existed, but the master took, it remains valid for him. The master's taking is valid even if there are debts, but if the master does not pay the debts, the slave is sold and proceeds divided. The text then discusses scenarios where the master takes earnings, then debts occur, and the effect of the first creditor absolving the slave. The key principle is that after debts attach to the slave's earnings and person, the master's prior takings are valid if there were no debts at the time, and any later absolution by a creditor must be prior to the debt's attachment to be effective. The commentator ends by noting that 'his debts' includes debts to the master, his son, father, etc.

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqabah): The physical person of the slave, subject to sale
الكسب (al-kasb): Earnings of the slave from trade or work
ضريبة (daribat): A fixed sum taken by master from slave's earnings as compensation for use

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this text, what happens if the ma'dhun slave has no earnings?
A) The debt is written off.
B) The debt becomes the master's responsibility.
C) The debt attaches to the slave's person (raqabah), and he may be sold.
D) The debt is transferred to the master.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a ma'dhun slave's debt attaches to both his earnings and his person, with earnings sold first, ensuring creditors are fully protected.

Sale of Ma'dhun Slave if Master Does Not Pay
Explains the procedure for selling the slave when master fails to pay debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the procedure for selling a ma'dhun slave when the master fails to pay debts, and explain the judge's waiting period and the requirement of the master's presence.

Core Idea
The judge sells the ma'dhun slave only after a waiting period and only in the master's presence.

This passage explains the ruling when a ma'dhun slave has debts but no earnings. The judge cannot sell him immediately; he must wait in case payment appears. The sale also requires the master's presence. This protects the master's right while satisfying creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition triggering the sale
The debt is attached to the slave's person (ragaabah) because he has no earnings (kasb). The master fails to redeem (yufid) by paying the debt.
The slave is ma'dhun (permitted to trade) and debts incurred in trade attach to his person if no earnings.
2
Determine the judge's duty: waiting period (talum)
The judge does not sell immediately but waits (yatalawwamu) in case the master or slave produces money. The duration is not fixed by the text; some say it is to the judge's discretion, others say three days.
The waiting period is a condition for the sale; immediate sale would be invalid.
3
Condition of the master's presence
The judge sells only in the presence of the master (mawlaa) or his representative (naa'ib). This ensures the master's right over his property is not violated.
If the slave is in another city and claims interdiction, the judge may first sell his earnings (kasb) by istihsan, but not his person until the master is present.
4
Rational proof (dalil aqli) against the objection
An objection is raised: Abu Hanifa does not allow interdiction (hajr) on a free person, so how can the judge sell the slave without the master's consent? The answer: the slave is already under the master's interdiction (as a slave), so the judge's sale is not a new interdiction but a repetition of the existing one for debt collection.
This rational proof justifies the sale as consistent with Hanafi principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (يباع فيه أن لم يفده السيد) يعني إذا تعلق الدين برقبته حيث لا كسب له يباع فيه رقبته دفعاً للضرر عن الغرماء، ولا يعجل القاضي بيعه بل يتلوم لاحتمال أن يكون له مال يقدم أو دين بقبضه فإذا مضت مدة التلوم ولم يظهر له وجه باعه لأن القاضي نصب ناظراً للمسلمين. ولم يقدر مدة التلوم؛ قيل هو مفوض إلى رأي القاضي، وقيل يقدر بثلاثة أيام ولا يبيعه القاضي إلا بحضرة مولاه أو نائبه فإذا خرج العبد إلى مصر وتصرف ولحقه ديون وفي يده أموال وقال أنا محجور علي وكذب الغرماء باع القاضي كسبه استحساناً ولا يبيع رقبته حتى يحضر المولى ؛ كذا في المحيط . قال في العناية : فإن قيل : فما وجه البيع على قول الإمام وهو لا يرى الحجر على الحر العاقل بسبب الدين وبيع القاضي العبد بغير رضا مولاه حجر عليه؟ أجيب بأن ذلك ليس بحجر عليه لأنه كان قبل ذلك محجوراً وأعيد
He (the author) said: (He is sold if the master does not redeem him). Meaning: when the debt attaches to his person because he has no earnings, his person is sold to repel harm from the creditors. And the judge does not hasten to sell him but delays expecting that he may have wealth to present or a debt to collect. Then when the period of delay passes and no means appears, he sells him because the judge is appointed as a supervisor for the Muslims. And the period of delay is not fixed; it is said it is delegated to the judge's opinion, and it is said it is fixed as three days. And the judge does not sell him except in the presence of his master or his representative. So if the slave goes out to a city and transacts and debts befall him and he has assets in his hand, and he says 'I am under interdiction' but the creditors deny, the judge sells his earnings by istihsan, and does not sell his person until the master is present. This is in al-Muhit. In al-'Inayah: If it is said: What is the basis of sale according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and he does not consider interdiction on a free sane person due to debt, and the judge's sale of the slave without the master's consent is an interdiction on him? The answer: That is not an interdiction on him because he was already interdicted and it is repeated.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This is a ruling_with_proof from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn: 'He is sold if the master does not redeem him.' That is, if the ma'dhun slave has no earnings to pay debts attached to his person, the judge sells the slave. The judge should not sell immediately but wait (talum) in case the master or slave produces funds. The waiting period is not fixed by the text; some say it is to the judge's discretion, others say three days. The sale must be in the master's presence or his representative. If the slave is in another city, claims interdiction (to avoid paying), but creditors deny, the judge may sell his earnings first, but not his person until the master is present (from al-Muhit). An objection is raised: Abu Hanifa does not allow interdiction on a free person, so how can the judge sell the slave without master's consent? The answer: The slave is already under interdiction (the master's authority), so the judge is merely re-imposing it for debt collection.

Key Terms
التلوم (al-talum): Waiting period before judge sells the slave
يفده (yufiduhu): The master redeems the slave by paying the debt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To allow the master to repent.
B) In case the master or slave produces money to pay the debt.
C) To give the slave time to escape.
D) To notify the creditors.

Key Takeaway
The judge must wait and sell only in the master's presence when selling a ma'dhun slave for debts.

Selling the Ma'dhun Slave to Satisfy Debts: Conditions and Procedure
Explaining when and how the qadi sells the ma'dhun slave to pay debts, and the division of the sale price among creditors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the judge's sale of a ma'dhun slave to satisfy debts, explain the waiting period and the requirement of the master's presence, and apply the principle of proportional distribution (tahassus) of the sale price among creditors.

Core Idea
The judge sells the ma'dhun only after a waiting period and in the master's presence—this is not interdiction but enforcement of creditors' rights.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the legal procedure when a ma'dhun slave has debts but no earnings. The judge must ensure fairness to both the master and the creditors. You will learn the conditions for selling the slave's person, the waiting period, the requirement of the master's presence, and how the sale price is divided proportionally among creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger for sale
When does the judge have the right to sell the ma'dhun slave? The passage says: when the slave has no earnings and debt is attached to his person, the judge may sell to remove harm from creditors.
The debt must be attached to the slave's raqaba (person).
2
Examine the waiting period (talawwum)
The judge delays to see if payment can come from other sources—either the slave's own wealth or a debt to collect. The duration is not fixed; it is left to the judge's discretion or estimated at three days.
The waiting period is to allow the slave or master to satisfy the debt without selling the person.
3
Determine who must be present
For the sale of the slave's person, the master or his deputy must be present. For the sale of the slave's earnings, the slave's presence suffices because the opponent (khāṣim) regarding earnings is the slave.
The master is the opponent regarding the person; the slave is the opponent regarding earnings.
4
Analyze the objection about interdiction (hajr)
An objection is raised: Imam Abu Hanifa does not allow interdiction on a free person for debt, yet the judge sells the slave without the master's full consent, which seems like interdiction. The Hanafi response: the sale is not a new interdiction; rather, the slave was already under interdiction from the master's permission to trade. The judge's action is a continuation of that existing interdiction.
The key distinction: the master cannot sell the ma'dhun without creditors' consent, so the judge's sale does not add a new restriction.
5
Apply the principle of tahassus
The sale price is divided proportionally among creditors, similar to the division of an insolvent estate (tarika). If the price is insufficient, creditors share proportionally; the remaining debt is pursued after the slave's freedom, but the slave is not sold a second time to avoid deterring buyers.
The slave's debt is stronger than the master's debt; if both exist, the slave's debt is paid first from the slave's value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علیه دین : قال رحمه الله : (يباع فيه أن لم يفده السيد) يعني إذا تعلق الدين برقبته حيث لا كسب له يباع فيه رقبته دفعاً للضرر عن الغرماء، ولا يعجل القاضي بيعه بل يتلوم لاحتمال أن يكون له مال يقدم أو دين بقبضه فإذا مضت مدة التلوم ولم يظهر له وجه باعه لأن القاضي نصب ناظراً للمسلمين. ولم يقدر مدة التلوم؛ قيل هو مفوض إلى رأي القاضي، وقيل يقدر بثلاثة أيام ولا يبيعه القاضي إلا بحضرة مولاه أو نائبه فإذا خرج العبد إلى مصر وتصرف ولحقه ديون وفي يده أموال وقال أنا محجور علي وكذب الغرماء باع القاضي كسبه استحساناً ولا يبيع رقبته حتى يحضر المولى ؛ كذا في المحيط . قال في العناية : فإن قيل : فما وجه البيع على قول الإمام وهو لا يرى الحجر على الحر العاقل بسبب الدين وبيع القاضي العبد بغير رضا مولاه حجر عليه؟ أجيب بأن ذلك ليس بحجر عليه لأنه كان قبل ذلك محجوراً وأعيد بيعه إذ لا يجوز للمولى أن يبيع العبد المأذون بغير رضا الغرماء وحجر المحجور عليه متصور، وقوله «إن لمن يفده سيده إشارة إلى أن البيع إنما يجوز إذا كان المولى حاضراً فأما إذا كان غائباً فإنه لا يبيع العبد حتى يحضر المولى فإن الخصم في رقبة العبد هو المولى فلا يجوز بيعه إلا بحضرة المولى أو نائبه بخلاف الكسب فإنه يباع بالدين، وإن كان المولى غائباً فإن الخصم فيه هو العبد فالشرط حضور العبد اهـ . قال الشارح والمراد بالدين ما يظهر في حق المولى، وأما ما لا يظهر في حقه فلا يباع فيه ويطالبه المولى به بعد الحرية وفي المحيط : ولا يجوز بيع العبد المأذون بأمر بعض الغرماء إلا برضا الباقين أو يكون القاضي هو الذي باعه ويعزل نصيب الغائب عنهم لأن للمولى في العبد حق ملك وللغرماء حق الاستسعاء فيباغ ليصل إليهم كمال حقهم وهذا الحق مفوت عليهم ببيع المولى فشرط إذنهم فيه اهـ. وفيه أيضاً : وإذا ولدت المأذونة المديونة بعد لحوقها دين لزم الدين الولد والأم ويباعان فيه لأن دين الأم حق ثبت في رقبتها فيسري إلى الولد وإن لحقها بعد الولادة لا يباع الولد وهو للمولى لأن الدين إنما تعلق برقبتها حال انفصاله فلا يتعلق بالولد وأما الهبة والكسب فيباعان في الدين وإن استفادهما قبل الدين والفرق أن الكسب يتبع المكاتب حقيقة وحكماً بكل حال لأنه حدث بكسبه وفعله والولد يتبع الاتصال ويصير أصلاً حال الانفصال، ولو كان عليها ألف قبل الولادة وألف بعد الولادة فالولد للأول خاصة ولا يدفع الولد بجناية الأم وإن ولدت بعد الجناية لأن موجب الجناية لا تجب في رقبة الجاني بل يخير المولى بين الدفع والفداء والولد ليس بجان فلم يجب دفعه. قال رحمه الله: (وقسم ثمنه بالحصص) أي بين الغرماء لأن ديونهم متعلقة برقبته فيتحاصصون من الاستيفاء وفاء من البدل كما في التركة، وإن لم يكن بالثمن وفاء يضرب كل غريم في الثمن بقدر حقه كالتركة إذا ضاقت فإن فضل شيء من ديونه طولب به بعد الحرية ولا يباع ثانياً كيلا يمتنع الناس عن شراء المأذون ودفعها للضرورة عن المشتري لأنه لما لم يؤذن في التجارة فلم يكن راضياً ببيعه ولا يلزم ما لو اشتراه الإذن فإنه لا يباع ثانياً وإن كان راضياً للبيع لأن الملك قد تبدل وتبدل الملك لا يبدل العين؛ كذا في العناية. قال في المبسوط : الأصل أن دين العبد أقوى من دين المولى ولهذا يقدم دين العبد على دين المولى في الإيفاء من رقبة العبد وهنا مسائل : أحدها في دين الوارث على عبد الميت مع دين الميت. والثاني في دين الميت ودين العبد الموصى له والثالث في هبة المريض عبده لرجل وللموهوب له على العبد دين الأول إذا هلك الرجل وعليه دين ألف وترك ابنين وعبداً قيمته ألف لا مال له غيره ولأحد الابنين على العبد خمسمائة يباغ العبد ويستوفي الابن دينه ثم يستوفي الأجنبي خمسمائة ثانية لأن دين الوارث دين العبد ودين الأجنبي دين على المولى ودين العبد متقدم على دين المولى، وإن كان دين الميت خمسمائة والمسألة بحالها يسقط نصف دين الابن ويستوفي نصفه أولاً ثم يستوفي الأجنبي دينه . درهم
On whom there is debt: He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'He is sold if the master does not free him.' Meaning: When the debt attaches to his neck (i.e., to his person) because he has no earnings, his person is sold to remove harm from the creditors. The judge does not hasten to sell him, but waits (yatalawwamu) for the possibility that he may have wealth to present or a debt to collect. When the period of waiting passes and no means appears, he sells him, because the judge is appointed as a supervisor for the Muslims. The period of waiting is not fixed; it is said to be delegated to the judge's opinion, and it is said it is estimated at three days. The judge does not sell him except in the presence of his master or his deputy. If the slave goes out to a city (Misr) and transacts, and debts befall him, and in his hand there are properties, and he says, 'I am under interdiction,' and the creditors call him a liar, the judge sells his earnings by istihsan, but does not sell his person until the master is present. This is in al-Muhit. He said in al-Inayah: If it is said: What is the basis for sale according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), while he does not recognize interdiction (hajr) on a free sane person due to debt, and the judge's sale of the slave without the master's consent is a form of interdiction on him? The answer: That is not interdiction on him, because he was already under interdiction before, and his sale is not permitted for the master to sell the ma'dhun slave without the creditors' consent, and interdiction of one already under interdiction is conceivable. His statement 'if the master does not free him' indicates that sale is only permissible when the master is present. As for when he is absent, he does not sell the slave until the master is present, because the opponent regarding the slave's person is the master, so it is not permitted to sell him except in the presence of the master or his deputy. Unlike earnings, which are sold for debt even if the master is absent, because the opponent regarding them is the slave, so the condition is the slave's presence. The commentator said: What is meant by 'debt' is that which appears regarding the master's right. As for what does not appear regarding his right, he is not sold for it, and the master demands it after freedom. In al-Muhit: It is not permitted to sell the ma'dhun slave by order of some creditors except with the consent of the rest, or the judge himself sells him and sets aside the share of the absent one from them, because the master has a right of ownership in the slave and the creditors have a right of pursuit (isti'sa'), so it is sold to give them their full right. This right is lost to them by the master's sale, so their permission is a condition. Also in it: If the indebted female ma'dhun gives birth after a debt befalls her, the debt applies to both the child and the mother, and they are both sold for it, because the mother's debt is a right established in her person, so it extends to the child. If it befalls her after birth, the child is not sold and belongs to the master, because the debt attaches to her person at the time of separation, so it does not attach to the child. As for gifts and earnings, they are sold for the debt even if acquired before the debt. The difference is that earnings follow the mukatab actually and legally in all cases because they occur through his earnings and action, while the child follows the connection and becomes an original at the time of separation. If she owed one thousand before birth and one thousand after birth, the child is particular to the first (debts) and the child is not given for the mother's tort, even if she gives birth after the tort, because the consequence of the tort does not become due in the person of the tortfeasor; rather, the master is given the choice between surrender and ransom, and the child is not a tortfeasor, so it is not required to be surrendered. He (the author) said: 'And divide his price by shares' i.e., among the creditors, because their debts are attached to his person, so they share proportionally in the proceeds as in the estate (tarika). If the price is insufficient, each creditor receives a proportion of the price according to his right, like when an estate is insufficient. If something remains of his debts, he is demanded for it after freedom, and he is not sold a second time, so that people are not deterred from buying the ma'dhun and due to necessity for the buyer, because since he was not permitted to trade, he was not content with his sale. It is not analogous to if the permission purchases it, for then he is not sold a second time even if he is content with the sale, because ownership has changed and change of ownership does not change the substance. This is in al-'Inayah. He said in al-Mabsut: The principle is that the debt of the slave is stronger than the debt of the master, and for this reason the slave's debt is given precedence over the master's debt in payment from the slave's person. Here there are issues: One: regarding the debt of the heir on the slave of the deceased alongside the debt of the deceased. Second: regarding the debt of the deceased and the debt of the slave bequeathed to him. Third: regarding the gift of a sick person's slave to a man, and the donee has a debt on the slave. The first: If a man dies owing one thousand dirhams and leaves two sons and a slave worth one thousand, with no other property, and one of the sons has a debt of five hundred on the slave, the slave is sold and the son collects his debt, then the stranger collects five hundred second, because the heir's debt is a debt of the slave, and the stranger's debt is a debt on the master, and the slave's debt takes precedence over the master's debt. If the deceased's debt is five hundred and the case is as is, half of the son's debt falls away, and he collects half first, then the stranger collects his debt. Dirham.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq explaining the situation where the ma'dhun slave has debts but no earnings. The judge may sell the slave's person (raqaba) to satisfy the creditors, but only after a waiting period (talawwum) to see if the slave can pay from other means. The judge does not sell the slave without the master's presence because the master is the opponent regarding the slave's person. The passage then addresses an objection: Imam Abu Hanifa does not allow interdiction (hajr) on a free sane person for debt, so how can the judge sell the slave against the master's will? The response is that the sale is not an interdiction but a continuation of the existing interdiction from the master's permission to trade. The sale price is divided proportionally among creditors (tahassus), similar to the division of an insolvent estate. If the price is insufficient, the remaining debt is pursued after the slave's freedom, but the slave is not sold again to avoid deterring buyers. The passage also includes a discussion from al-Mabsut about the priority of the slave's debt over the master's debt, illustrated with examples of debts on a slave in the context of inheritance and gifts.

Key Terms
التلوم (al-talawwum): The waiting period the judge observes before selling the slave, to allow the slave or his master to satisfy the debts from other means.
التحاصص (al-tahāṣuṣ): Proportional distribution of the sale price among creditors when the price is insufficient to cover all debts.
دين العبد (dayn al-'abd): Debts incurred by the slave in his capacity as ma'dhun; these debts are attached to his person and take precedence over the master's debts.
دين المولى (dayn al-mawlā): Debts owed by the master that are not related to the slave's activities; these are secondary to the slave's debts when both are due from the slave's assets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the judge waits (yatalawwamu) before selling the ma'dhun slave to satisfy debts?
A) To allow the master to object
B) To give the slave or his master time to pay from other means
C) To find a buyer offering a higher price
D) To consult with the creditors

Key Takeaway
The judge's sale of a ma'dhun slave to satisfy debts is a necessary legal remedy that ensures creditors' rights while respecting the master's ownership through procedural safeguards.

Effective Imprisonment of the Authorized Slave: Public Knowledge and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling on the validity of hajr and its publicity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for hajr of an authorized slave and explain why publicity is required on the basis of istihsan, and identify the practical difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
A master's secret revocation of his slave's trading permission is ineffective in Hanafi law – creditors must be protected, so public knowledge is required.

This lesson examines the legal mechanism of hajr (restriction) on an authorized slave. When a master wishes to revoke his slave's permission to trade, the Hanafi school imposes conditions to protect third-party creditors. The central issue is whether the revocation can be secret or must be publicly known, leading to a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. Understanding this reveals how istihsan (juristic preference) is used to prioritize preventing harm over strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the hajr is effective only if known to the majority of the marketplace. The master's private declaration is insufficient.
The text explicitly says 'and he is imprisoned by an imprisonment even if the majority of the market people know of it.'
2
Identify the opposing school's position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the hajr is valid even if no one from the market knows. For him, it is a unilateral act of the master.
The text cites: 'Imam al-Shafi'i said: The imprisonment is valid even if no one from the market knows of it.'
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi argument is based on preventing harm to creditors. If the hajr were secret, the master could seize the slave's earnings, leaving creditors with an uncertain claim after emancipation.
The text says: 'if his imprisonment were valid without their knowledge, they would be harmed because if the slave earns something, the master takes it, and their right is delayed until after emancipation.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing view
The requirement of publicity is justified by istihsan because informing everyone is difficult or impossible, but requiring the knowledge of the majority strikes a balance.
'The qualification with 'majority' is based on istihsan because informing everyone is impossible or difficult.'
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, a transaction with the slave by someone unaware of the hajr is valid. Under Shafi'i, it would be invalid because the hajr is effective from the moment the master declares it.
The text implies that if the hajr is not publicly known, the slave remains authorized. This is the key practical consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وما بقي طولب به بعد عتقه) يعني ما بقي من الدين بعد قسمة الثمن يطالب به بعد الحرية لأن الغرماء بالخيار إن شاءوا استسعوا العبد، وإن شاءوا باعوه لم يبق لهم تعلق به لأن من هو مخير بين شيئين أو أشياء فاختار أحدهما بطل خياره في غيره، ولما كان الإذن تارة يكون شائعاً فلا يحجر إلا بالحجر الشائع وتارة يكون غير شائع فينجبر بالحجر غير الشائع. قال رحمه الله : (ويحجر بحجر وإن علم به أكثر أهل سوقه وقال الشافعي: الحجر صحيح وإن لم يعلم به أحد من أهل سوقه. ولنا أن حجره لو صح بدون علمهم لتضرروا به لأنه إذا اكتسب شيئاً فالمولى يأخذه فيتأخر حقهم إلى ما بعد العتق وهو موهوم لأنه قد يعتق وقد لا يعيق. وقيد بالأكثر الاستحسان لأن وهو إعلام الكل متعذر أو متعسر. ولو حجر عليه بحضره الأقل لم يصر محجوراً عليه حتى لو باعه من علم منه ومن لم يعلم جاز البيع لأنه لما صار مأذوناً له في حق من لم يعلم صار مأذوناً له في حق من علم أيضاً لأن الإذن لا يتجزئ ويشترط علم العبد أيضاً وبقي مأذونا له حتى يعلم بالحجر. وفي المحيط أصله أن الحجر الخاص لا يرد على الإذن العام ويرد على الإذن الخاص بأن أذن له بمحضر رجل أو رجلين وثلاثة فحجر بمحضر هؤلاء يصح . رآه المولى يبيع ويشتري بعدما حجر عليه قبل أن يعلم العبد فلم ينهه ثم علم العبد بالحجر يبقى مأذوناً استحساناً. ووجهه أن سكوت المولى إجازة حال رؤية عبده يبيع ويشتري يرفع الحجر الثابت فلأن يرفع الموقوف أولى. ولو أرسل إليه صبياً يخبره بحجره أو كتب إليه صار .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And whatever remains he is pursued for after his emancipation' – meaning that what remains of the debt after division of the price is pursued after emancipation because the creditors have the option: if they wish, they can seek compensation from the slave's labor (istis'a'), and if they wish, they can sell him. They have no further attachment to him because the one who is given a choice between two or more things, if he chooses one, his choice is void with respect to the other. Since authorization is sometimes public and is not revocable except by public imprisonment, and sometimes it is private and can be revoked by private imprisonment. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And he is imprisoned by an imprisonment even if the majority of the market people know of it.' Imam al-Shafi'i said: 'The imprisonment is valid even if no one from the market knows of it.' Our argument is that if his imprisonment were valid without their knowledge, they would be harmed because if the slave earns something, the master takes it, and their right is delayed until after emancipation, which is uncertain because he may be emancipated or may not. The qualification with 'majority' is based on istihsan because informing everyone is impossible or difficult. If he imprisons him in the presence of a few, he does not become imprisoned. Even if he sells to someone who knew and someone who did not know, the sale is valid because since he became authorized with respect to those who did not know, he also became authorized with respect to those who knew, because authorization is indivisible. The slave's knowledge is also a condition, and he remains authorized until he knows of the imprisonment. In al-Muhit, the origin is that a specific imprisonment does not override a general authorization, but it overrides a specific authorization. So if he authorized him in the presence of one, two, or three men, and then imprisoned him in their presence, it is valid. If the master sees the slave buying and selling after he has imprisoned him but before the slave knows, and he does not forbid him, then when the slave learns of the imprisonment, he remains authorized by istihsan. The reasoning is that the master's silence at seeing his slave buying and selling is a ratification that lifts the established imprisonment, so it is more appropriate that it lifts the suspended one. If he sends a boy to inform him of his imprisonment or writes to him, it becomes effective.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for the master's hajr (restriction) of his authorized slave. After the slave's assets are distributed among creditors, any remaining debt is pursued after the slave's emancipation. The text then addresses the conditions for the hajr to be effective, including that the majority of the marketplace must know. A disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i is noted: al-Shafi'i holds the hajr valid even without anyone knowing, while the Hanafi position requires public knowledge to protect creditors from harm. The Hanafi argument is that without such knowledge, the master could secretly seize the slave's earnings, leaving creditors with an uncertain future claim. Publicity is required by istihsan due to the difficulty of informing everyone. The text also states that the slave must be personally informed, and if the master sees the slave trading after the hajr without objecting, the hajr is effectively lifted. The slave remains authorized until he actually knows of the hajr.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction placed by the master on the authorized slave, revoking his permission to trade.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; here, preferring to require publicity due to difficulty of informing everyone.
استسعاء (istis'a'): Seeking compensation from the slave's labor after emancipation to pay off debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the imprisonment (hajr) of an authorized slave become effective against third parties?
A) As soon as the master verbally declares it in private
B) When the slave is told of it personally
C) When the majority of the market people know about it
D) When a notice is posted in the marketplace

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the master's revocation of an authorized slave's trading permission is only effective if it is public knowledge, protecting creditors from harm through istihsan.

Remaining Debt Pursued After Slave's Freedom
Explains that remaining debt is pursued after the slave is freed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on remaining debt after a slave's freedom and explain the legal effect of the creditor's choice on the debt obligation.

Core Idea
A creditor's choice to sell a slave does not extinguish the remaining debt; it can be collected after freedom.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a key ruling concerning a trading slave sold to satisfy debts. The matn states: 'And what remains is demanded after his emancipation.' The commentary expands on the creditor's options (sale or istis'a) and the legal effect of choosing one: the other option becomes void, but the debt itself survives the sale. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the concept that the creditor's right to the debt is not extinguished by the sale; it is merely postponed until the slave's freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case scenario
The scenario involves a trading slave (ma'dhun) who owes a debt. Creditors either sell the slave or seek istis'a. The matn addresses what happens if they sell the slave and the sale price does not cover the full debt.
2
State the ruling from the matn
The matn says: 'وما بقي طولب به بعد عتقه' – whatever remains of the debt after dividing the price is demanded after the slave is freed. This is the core ruling.
3
Understand the creditor's options
The commentary explains that creditors have a choice (khiyar) between istis'a (seeking payment by having the slave work) or selling the slave. Once they choose one, the other option becomes void.
This is based on the principle that one who has a choice between two things, upon choosing one, loses the other.
4
Apply the legal consequence
Despite the sale, any debt not covered by the sale price remains a valid obligation. It does not disappear; it is simply deferred until the slave is freed. Then the creditor can pursue it.
The sale does not extinguish the debt; it only liquidates part of it.
5
Note the additional principle about permission and interdiction
The text adds a related principle: if permission (idhn) is general (shai'), it can only be revoked by a general interdiction (hajr shai'); if specific, by a specific interdiction. This is a broader usul point linked to the case.
This principle might apply to other contexts, such as a guardian's permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وما بقي طولب به بعد عتقه) يعني ما بقي من الدين بعد قسمة الثمن يطالب به بعد الحرية لأن الغرماء بالخيار إن شاءوا استسعوا العبد، وإن شاءوا باعوه لم يبق لهم تعلق به لأن من هو مخير بين شيئين أو أشياء فاختار أحدهما بطل خياره في غيره، ولما كان الإذن تارة يكون شائعاً فلا يحجر إلا بالحجر الشائع وتارة يكون غير شائع فينجبر بالحجر غير الشائع.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And what remains is demanded after his emancipation) meaning what remains of the debt after the division of the price is demanded after freedom because the creditors have a choice: if they wish, they can seek payment from the slave's earnings, and if they wish, they can sell him; nothing remains of their claim because one who has a choice between two or more things, if he chooses one, his choice in the other becomes void. And since permission is sometimes common, then he is not interdicted except by a common interdiction, and sometimes it is not common, so it is remedied by a non-common interdiction.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn with its commentary. The author clarifies that after the slave is sold and the price distributed among creditors, if any debt remains, the creditors can demand payment after the slave is freed. The creditor has the option to either have the slave work to pay off the debt (istis'a) or sell him, but once he chooses one, the option lapses. The passage also introduces a principle about permission (idhn) and interdiction (hajr): if permission is general (shai'), it can only be revoked by a general interdiction; if specific, by a specific interdiction.

Key Terms
استسعاء (istis'a): Seeking payment by having the slave earn through work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After a trading slave is sold and the price is distributed, what happens to the remaining debt?
A) It is forgiven
B) It is claimed after the slave is freed
C) It is transferred to the buyer
D) The slave must be sold again

Key Takeaway
The creditor's right to the remaining debt survives the sale of the slave and can be exercised after the slave's freedom.

Notification of Hijr via Messenger or Letter
Ruling on validity of hijr via messenger or letter, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on notifying a slave of interdiction and explain how the later view of al-Balkhi resolved the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
Notification of hijr via messenger or letter is valid; the later fatwa removes the distinction between permission and interdiction.

This lesson examines the methods of informing a slave of legal interdiction (hajr) according to the Hanafi school. The source passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the number and character of informants needed when no direct message is sent. A later view from al-Balkhi, transmitted by Khaherzadeh, reconciles the positions and becomes the basis for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is notifying a slave of his interdiction (hajr). Determine which mode of notification is used: direct messenger/letter or an independent informant.
2
Distinguish the two modes
If a messenger or letter is sent, it is equivalent to direct speech and valid regardless of the messenger's character. If an informant reports without being sent, conditions apply.
Messenger includes a boy, slave, or wicked person.
3
State Imam Abu Hanifa's condition
For an independent informant, Abu Hanifa requires two witnesses or one just man known to the slave.
This is stricter for interdiction than for permission.
4
State the Sahibayn's condition
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that any informant suffices, regardless of being free, slave, just, wicked, or a boy.
They see notification as a simple communication.
5
Analyse the later tarjih
Al-Balkhi eliminates the distinction between permission and interdiction; the key is the slave's belief in the truthfulness of the news. Fatwa is on this view.
This view simplifies practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أرسل إليه صبياً يخبره بحجره أو كتب إليه صار محجوراً لأن الرسالة والكتابة من الغائب بمنزلة المشافهة من الحاضر، سواء كان الرسول عدلاً أو فاسقاً، حراً أو عبداً. وإن أخسره بالحجر رجل أو صبي من غير رسالة لم يكن حجر حتى يخبره رجلان أو رجل عدل يعرفه العبد عند الإمام، وعندهما يصير محجوراً عليه كان المخبر حراً أو عبداً عدلاً أو فاسقاً أو صبياً. وفي الخانية فرق الإمام بين الإذن والحجر، فعنده لا يثبت الحجر بخبر الواحد إلا إذا كان عدلاً أو اثنان ويثبت الإذن بقول الفضولي الواحد على كل حال. وذكر الشيخ خواهر زاده عن الفقيه أبي بكر البلخي أنه لا فرق بينهما وإنما يصير مأذوناً إذا كان الخبر صادقاً عند العبد وكذا الحجر والفتوى على هذا القول اهـ.
And if he sent a boy to him to inform him of his interdiction or wrote to him, he becomes interdicted, because the message and writing from an absent person are like direct speech from a present person, whether the messenger is just or wicked, free or slave. But if a man or a boy informs him of the interdiction without a message, it is not interdiction until two men or one just man whom the slave knows inform him according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he becomes interdicted whether the informant is free or slave, just or wicked, or a boy. In al-Khaniyyah, the Imam made a distinction between permission and interdiction: according to him, interdiction is not established by the report of a single person unless he is just or two; permission is established by the statement of a single outsider in all cases. And Shaykh Khaherzadeh narrated from al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi that there is no difference between them, and one becomes permitted only if the news is truthful to the slave, and similarly for interdiction, and the fatwa is on this view.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage discusses the methods of notifying a slave of his interdiction (hajr) and contains an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The text explains that if the master sends a messenger (even a boy) or writes a letter, the slave becomes interdicted, as these are equivalent to direct speech. However, if a person simply informs the slave without being a messenger, Imam Abu Hanifa requires two witnesses or one just man known to the slave; the two say it is sufficient regardless of the informant's status. There is also a distinction between permission (idhn) and interdiction: Abu Hanifa is stricter for interdiction. However, a later view from al-Balkhi says there is no difference, and the fatwa is on that view.

Key Terms
رسالة (risalah): Messenger sent to inform of interdiction; considered like direct speech.
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction restricting a slave's capacity to transact.
إذن (idhn): Permission given to a slave to trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (fatwa) view after al-Balkhi, what is the key condition for a valid notification of hijr via an independent informant?
A) The informant must be two just men
B) The informant must be a free adult
C) The slave believes the news is truthful
D) The informant must be a messenger sent by the master

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi fatwa eliminates the distinction between permission and interdiction and makes notification valid if the slave considers the news truthful.

Interdiction of Slaves: Master's Denial and Consequence of Ma'dhun's Permission
Explains effect of master's denial on interdiction and rule for ma'dhun's permission to his slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a slave becomes interdicted when the master denies, and apply the rule that a ma'dhun's permission to his own slave is subject to the master's authority via hukman.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's permission to his own slave is only effective through the master's deemed authority.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two important rulings regarding interdiction of slaves engaged in trade. First, if the master is present and denies the interdiction, the slave is not interdicted. Second, when a ma'dhun (permitted slave) gives trade permission to his own slave, the master's later interdiction of the ma'dhun also interdicts the second slave if the ma'dhun has debts—because the master is deemed to have permitted the second slave by operation of law (hukman). We'll unpack the reasoning and the analogy used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two distinct rulings: (a) master present and denies, (b) ma'dhun permits his own slave then master interdicts the ma'dhun. Separate these in your mind.
2
Analyze the condition for interdiction upon denial
Focus on the first clause: if the master is present and confirms the informant, the slave is interdicted; if the master denies, the slave is not. Why? Because interdiction requires the master's active will. Denial negates the intention.
3
Trace the logic of the second ruling
The ma'dhun (slave A) gives permission to his own slave (B). When master interdicts A, the effect on B depends on whether A has debts. If A has debts, B is also interdicted; if not, B remains permitted. Reason: with debts, the master's permission to B is established by operation of law (hukman), because A's permission is derived from the master's authority.
Key term: hukman – legally deemed.
4
Understand the analogy (talil)
The author draws an analogy: if the master directly permitted B and then interdicted A, B would not be affected. Similarly, when B's permission is deemed from the master (due to A's debts), interdiction of A does not affect B. The same logic holds when A dies with debts.
This is a qiyas (analogy) where the illa is the master's effective permission.
5
Apply to a hypothetical
Create your own scenario: A ma'dhun without debts permits his slave. Master interdicts the ma'dhun. What happens to the second slave? Not interdicted. Why? No debt means master's deemed permission does not arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إذا حضر المولى وصدقه، فلو حضر المولى وكذبه لا يصير محجوراً عليه، وإذا أذن العبد المأذون لعبده في التجارة ثم حجر المولى على الأول إن كان عليه دين يحجر على الثاني، ومثله لو مات الأول وعليه دين، وإن لم يكن على الأول دين لم يحجر على الثاني في الوجهين لأنه لا يملك المولى إذن العبد الثاني، وإن لم يكن عليه دين فالمولى يملك أن يأذن للثاني الأول دين فصار المولى آذناً للثاني حكماً، والمولى لو أذن للثاني حقيقة ثم حجر على الأول لم يحجر على الثاني فكذا حكماً.
This is if the master is present and confirms him (the informant). But if the master is present and denies him, he does not become interdicted. And if the permitted slave (ma'dhun) gives permission to his own slave for trade, then the master interdicts the first, if the first had a debt, the second is also interdicted; similarly if the first dies and has a debt. If the first has no debt, the second is not interdicted in either case because the master does not own the permission of the second slave. If the first has no debt, the master owns the right to permit the second; so the master becomes a permitter for the second by operation of law. And if the master actually permitted the second and then interdicted the first, the second is not interdicted; so likewise by operation of law.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings. First: if the master is present when someone informs the slave of interdiction and he denies that he interdicted, the slave does not become interdicted. Second: if a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) gives permission to his own slave to trade, and then the master interdicts the ma'dhun, what happens to the second slave? If the ma'dhun has debts, the second slave is also interdicted; if not, the second remains permitted. This is because when the ma'dhun has debts, the effective permission for the second slave comes from the master as a legal consequence (hukman). The author analogizes to the case where the master directly permits the second slave: if he then interdicts the first, the second is unaffected.

Key Terms
حكماً (hukman): By operation of law, i.e., the legal effect is attributed to the master even if he did not act directly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the interdiction of a ma'dhun slave also interdict his own slave (to whom he gave trade permission)?
A) Always, regardless of circumstances.
B) Only if the ma'dhun has debts.
C) Only if the master explicitly names the second slave in the interdiction.
D) Never, because permission from a ma'dhun is independent.

Key Takeaway
The master's deemed permission (hukman) binds the second slave to the first's interdiction only when the first slave has debts.

Rulings on Mukatab and Heir's Permission to a Permitted Slave
Rulings on interdiction of mukatab and validity of heir's permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a master's interdiction of a mukatab and explain when an heir's permission to a slave of the mukatab becomes valid.

Core Idea
A master cannot interdict a mukatab who has permission to trade; an heir's permission is conditional on paying the kitaba.

This lesson examines two specific rulings from the Hanafi school about the relationship between a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) and a permitted slave (ma'dhun). First, the master's power of interdiction over a permitted slave does not extend to a mukatab. Second, when a mukatab dies leaving a child, the child's permission to the slave is initially void but becomes valid if the kitaba is paid from the slave's earnings. We will unpack the reasoning behind these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling
Determine the ruling on a master's ability to interdict a mukatab who is a permitted slave.
The passage states it is not permitted.
2
Understand the basis for the first ruling
Note that the mukatab's own status (death or incapacity) triggers interdiction, not the master's action.
3
Identify the second ruling
When a mukatab dies leaving a child, the child's permission to the slave to trade is initially void.
The reason is the estate is burdened by debt.
4
Understand the condition for validity
The permission becomes valid if the kitaba is paid from the slave's earnings.
This is a conditional retroactive validity.
5
Explain the legal consequence
The permission is effective retrospectively once the condition is met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يجز حجر المولى على المأذون مكاتبه وينحجر بموت المكاتب وعجزه. ولو مات المكاتب عن ولد فأذن الولد للعبد في التجارة فإذنه باطل لأن التركة ما دامت مشغولة لا يملكها الوارث، فلو أدى بدل الكتابة من كسب المأذون صح الإذن وإن كان الإذن قبل مضي الدين لأنه تبين أنه ملك من وقت الموت متى قضى الدين وأطلق في قوله ينحجر بحجره» فشمل المنجز والمعلق وهو صحيح في المنجز غير صحيح في المعلق. قال في المحيط : لو قال القاضي لرجل قسد حجرت عليك إذا سفهت لم يكن حجراً. وإذا قال لسفيه قد أطلقتك إذا صلحت جائز لأن الإذن والإطلاق إسقاط للحجر وتعليق الإسقاط بالشرط جائز كالطلاق والعتاق وأما الحجر عزل وتعليق العزل بالشرط لا يصح وروى ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف ومحمد جاز الحجر كما جاز الإذن لأن الحجر منع وتعليق المنع بالخطر جائز اهـ.
And it is not permitted for the master to interdict a permitted slave who is a mukatab; he is interdicted by the death or incapacity of the mukatab. And if the mukatab dies leaving a child, and the child gives permission to the slave to trade, his permission is void because the estate, as long as it is occupied, is not owned by the heir. If he pays the kitaba from the earnings of the permitted slave, the permission becomes valid, even if the permission was before the debt was paid, because it becomes clear that he owned from the time of death when the debt is paid. And he said generally in his statement: 'he is interdicted by his interdiction', so it includes immediate and conditional. It is valid for immediate, not valid for conditional. He said in Al-Muhit: If the judge says to a man, 'I have interdicted you if you become foolish', it is not an interdiction. And if he says to a foolish person, 'I have released you if you become upright', it is valid because permission and release are removals of interdiction, and suspending removal on a condition is valid like divorce and emancipation. As for interdiction, it is a removal of authority, and suspending removal on a condition is not valid. And Ibn Sama'a narrated from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that interdiction is valid just as permission is valid because interdiction is prevention, and suspending prevention on a condition is valid.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage covers three sub-topics. First: a master cannot interdict a mukatab (slave on a contract of manumission) who is also a permitted trader; interdiction occurs only by the mukatab's death or incapacity. Second: if a mukatab dies leaving a son, the son's permission to a slave of the mukatab is void initially but becomes valid if the kitaba is paid from the slave's earnings. Third: conditional interdiction and conditional permission. The author states that the matn's 'hijr' includes both immediate and conditional, but immediate is valid while conditional is not. He cites Al-Muhit: a judge's conditional interdiction ('if you become foolish') is void, but conditional release of a foolish person is valid. However, a narration from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad says conditional interdiction is valid by analogy with conditional permission, as both are forms of prevention.

Key Terms
معلق (mu'allaq): Conditional (suspended on a future event).
منجز (munajjaz): Immediate/effective now.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with his master, agreeing to pay a sum over time in exchange for freedom.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does an heir's permission to a slave of a deceased mukatab become valid?
A) Immediately upon the mukatab's death
B) When the kitaba is paid from the slave's earnings
C) When the heir becomes an adult
D) Only if the master consents

Key Takeaway
The heir's permission to a slave of a deceased mukatab is conditional on the kitaba debt being cleared from the slave's earnings.

Conditional Hijr vs Conditional Idhn: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ruling on conditional hijr/idhn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditional hijr (interdiction) and conditional idhn (permission) according to the Hanafi school, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on conditional hijr.

Core Idea
Conditional permission is valid because it is a removal of a restriction; conditional interdiction is invalid in the dominant view because it is a new restriction.

This lesson focuses on the Hanafi ruling regarding conditional (mu'allaq) legal actions: can a judge say 'I have interdicted you if you become foolish'? The dominant view says no, because interdiction (hijr) is a prohibition, and suspending a prohibition on a condition is invalid. However, conditional permission (idhn) is valid, as it is a removal of interdiction, similar to suspending divorce or emancipation on a condition. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: a narration from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that conditional interdiction is valid by analogy with permission. We will examine the proof structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
Conditional hijr (interdiction) and conditional idhn (permission).
2
Understand the classification
Idhn is isqat (removal of a restriction); hijr is man' (prohibition).
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
Suspending isqat on a condition is valid (like talak and itq); suspending man' is invalid.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dissent
Ibn Sama'a narrated that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow conditional hijr by analogy with conditional idhn.
This is a nawadir transmission.
5
Evaluate the weight
The dominant view is from Zahir al-Riwayah; the minority view is a rare narration.
Mufta bihi is the dominant view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
معلق (mu'allaq): Conditional (suspended on a future event).
منجز (munajjaz): Immediate/effective now.
إذن (idhn): Permission; in this context, releasing someone from a restriction.
حجر (hajr/hijr): Interdiction; a legal restriction placed on a person's capacity to act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Debts and Litigation After Hijr of Ma'dhun
Rules on litigation and acknowledgment after hijr of ma'dhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine who is the proper litigant for debts of a ma'dhun after hijr, and evaluate the validity of his acknowledgment of defects in goods sold before interdiction.

Core Idea
Hijr does not affect pre-existing debts; the slave remains litigant, but the master takes over after death or sale.

When a permitted slave (ma'dhun) is interdicted, questions arise about who can claim debts owed to him and whether his own acknowledgments are valid. This passage from Al-Muhit lays out the Hanafi rulings based on the nature of the debt—whether it existed before hijr—and the status of the slave. Understanding these rules is crucial for correctly managing the financial affairs of a ma'dhun after interdiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage first addresses cases where the ma'dhun is owed debts from before hijr, and then cases where he acknowledges a defect in goods sold while permitted.
Each scenario has different litigants and validity conditions.
2
Determine the litigant for pre-hijr debts
For debts that were established before the slave was interdicted, the slave himself is the litigant. Hijr does not strip him of rights already fixed.
The reasoning: hijr does not affect pre‑existing rights, and collecting the price is part of the contract rights.
3
Determine the litigant after slave's death or sale
If the slave dies or is sold, the master becomes the litigant for those same debts, because the master is the nearest person to him, like an heir.
Exception: the master cannot collect if he himself owes the slave.
4
Analyze the slave's acknowledgment of a defect after hijr
If the slave admits a defect that could not have occurred after sale (e.g., a manufacturing flaw), his acknowledgment is not accepted; the judge relies on the contract or buyer's proof.
If the defect could occur later, the slave's word is not trusted against creditors, and the master litigates based on his own knowledge.
5
Understand the rationale behind each ruling
The rulings balance the slave's pre‑existing rights, the master's authority as guardian, and the need to protect creditors from collusive acknowledgments.
The talil (rationale) is explicit: hijr does not affect pre‑established rights; the master stands in for the slave; acknowledgment of a non‑occurring defect is presumed to be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط في باب إقرار المأذون بعد الحجر: وإذا حجر على المأذون وله ديون على الناس كان الخصم فيها العبد حتى لو قبضها العبد برئ الغريم لأن الحجر لا يعمل فيما ثبت للعبد قبل الحجر ولأن قبض الثمن من حقوق العقد، ولو مات العبد أو باعه فالخصم فيها هو المولى وإن كان على العبد دين لأن المولى أقرب الناس إليه فإذا عجز العبد عن قبض حقه والخصومة فيه يقوم المولى مقامه كالوارث يقوم مقام المورث إلا أنه لا يقبض الدين إذا كان له دين عليه. وإذا أقر العبد بعد الحجر عند القاضي بعيب لا يحدث مثله في متاع باعه حال إذنه يرد عليه لا بإقرار لكن القاضي بوجود عقد البيع أو أقام المشتري البينة، وإن كان عيباً يحدث مثله لم يصدق العبد على الغرماء والخصم فيه هو المولى بحال فيه على علمه.
And in Al-Muhit, in the chapter on the acknowledgment of the ma'dhun after interdiction: When the ma'dhun is interdicted and he has debts owed to him by people, the litigant is the slave, such that if the slave collects them, the debtor is discharged, because the interdiction does not affect what was established for the slave before the interdiction, and because collecting the price is part of the rights of the contract. If the slave dies or is sold, the litigant is the master, even if the slave has a debt, because the master is the nearest person to him. So when the slave is unable to collect his right and litigate regarding it, the master stands in his place, just as the heir stands in place of the testator, except that he does not collect the debt if he himself has a claim against him. If the slave, after interdiction, acknowledges before the judge a defect that does not usually occur in the merchandise he sold while permitted, it is not accepted from him by acknowledgment, but rather the judge (acts) based on the existence of the sale contract or the buyer establishes evidence. If it is a defect that usually occurs, the slave is not believed against the creditors, and the litigant is the master in all cases based on his knowledge.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage from Al-Muhit provides detailed rules about the ma'dhun after interdiction. For debts owed to the slave that were established before hijr, the slave himself is the litigant and can collect them; hijr does not affect pre-existing rights. If the slave dies or is sold, the master litigates on behalf of the slave, as the master is the heir-like figure. However, the master cannot collect if he himself owes the slave. Regarding acknowledgments by the slave after hijr: if he admits a defect in goods sold while permitted, the acknowledgment is not effective; instead, the judge relies on the existence of the contract or the buyer's proof. If the defect is one that could occur after sale, the slave's acknowledgment is not believed against the creditors, and the master is the litigant.

Key Terms
الخصم (al-khasm): The litigant party in a legal claim.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal interdiction preventing a person from managing his finances.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or admission, especially concerning a right or debt.
عيب (ayb): A defect in goods that gives the buyer the option to rescind the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is the litigant for debts owed to a ma'dhun that were established before his interdiction?
A) The master
B) The slave
C) The judge
D) The debtor

Key Takeaway
For debts established before hijr, the slave himself is the proper litigant; after his death or sale, the master takes over, but cannot collect if he himself owes the slave.

Who Can Exercise Hajr (Interdiction)?
Definition of who can exercise hajr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four authorities capable of exercising hajr and explain the unified principle behind their inclusion.

Core Idea
Interdiction (hajr) is wielded by the master, father, executor, and judge—all are equal in removal of authority.

This brief note from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key point about the term 'hajr' in the matn. Many students assume interdiction applies only to a master's control over a trading slave, but the Hanafi school extends it to other guardians. Understanding who these authorities are is foundational for further rulings on sales and agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase
Locate 'bi-hajrihi' in the matn and note that the pronoun 'hi' refers to the authority figure being discussed.
The matn likely states something like 'by his interdiction'.
2
List the four authorities
Explicitly name: master (sayyid), father (ab), executor (wasi), and judge (qadi). These are the ones included by the pronoun.
The passage states these are 'all equal in removal'.
3
Recognize the common ground
Understand that all four have the authority of removal ('azl) over those under their care. The word 'hajr' here refers to that power of interdiction.
The equality means the same fiqh rules of hajr apply to each.
4
Apply the principle
When reading later rulings about hajr, remember that they are not limited to masters but extend to these other guardians as well.
This avoids the common misunderstanding that hajr is exclusive to the master-slave relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضمير في قوله بحجره» يشمل السيد والأب والوصي والقاضي لأن الكل سواء في العزل
And the pronoun in his statement 'by his interdiction' includes the master, the father, the executor, and the judge because all are equal in removal (of authority).
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This is a brief concluding note on the matn's phrase 'bi-hajrihi' (by his interdiction). It clarifies that the term 'hajr' in this context is not limited to the master alone but also applies to the father (who can interdict his minor child), the executor (wasi) of a will, and the judge (qadi). All these authorities have the power of interdiction over those under their guardianship, and the rules discussed apply to them equally.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction that can be exercised by various guardians.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, who can perform the interdiction referred to in the matn?
A) Only the master
B) The master, father, executor, and judge
C) Only the judge
D) Only the father

Key Takeaway
The term 'hajr' in the matn encompasses master, father, executor, and judge—all share equal removal authority.
