Conditions for Effective Hijr via Messenger
Explains how hijr is communicated and when it takes effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for establishing hijr on an authorized slave via messenger or informant, and explain the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, as well as the final fatwa position.

Core Idea
Hijr via messenger is always effective; direct informants require two or one upright man per Imam, but fatwa equalizes based on truthfulness.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical question: how does a master effectively revoke an authorized slave’s permission to trade when the master is absent? The text outlines the general rule that a message or letter is equivalent to face-to-face speech, but then dives into a nuance: what if someone simply tells the slave of the restriction without being a designated messenger? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute, ultimately resolved by a later fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for messages
The text states that a message or writing from an absent master is like direct speech, regardless of the messenger's character.
This is the baseline principle.
2
Distinguish between messenger and informant
The passage then considers the case of an informant who is not a messenger. Here, Imam Abu Hanifa requires two witnesses or one upright witness known to the slave. The Sahibayn require only one informant of any status.
This shows a key ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the basis of the dispute
Imam differentiates between idhn (permission, a grant) and hajr (restriction, a removal). The Sahibayn analogize the two.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) dispute.
4
Consider the later fatwa
Abu Bakr al-Balkhi eliminates the distinction, making the truthfulness of the news from the slave’s perspective the criterion. The fatwa adopts this view.
This simplifies the rule for mufti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متی كان على محجوراً لأن الرسالة والكتابة من الغائب بمنزلة المشافهة من الحاضر، سواء كان الرسول عدلاً أو فاسقاً، حراً أو عبداً. وإن أخسره بالحجر رجل أو صبي من غير رسالة لم يكن حجر حتى يخبره رجلان أو رجل عدل يعرفه العبد عند الإمام، وعندهما يصير محجوراً عليه كان المخبر حراً أو عبداً عدلاً أو فاسقاً أو صبياً. وفي الخانية فرق الإمام بين الإذن والحجر، فعنده لا يثبت الحجر بخبر الواحد إلا إذا كان عدلاً أو اثنان ويثبت الإذن بقول الفضولي الواحد على كل حال. وذكر الشيخ خواهر زاده عن الفقيه أبي بكر البلخي أنه لا فرق بينهما وإنما يصير مأذوناً إذا كان الخبر صادقاً عند العبد وكذا الحجر والفتوى على هذا القول اهـ.
Whenever [a slave] is placed under restriction because the message and writing from an absent person are equivalent to face-to-face speech from the present one, whether the messenger is upright or sinful, free or slave. And if a man or a boy informs him of restriction without a message, restriction does not take effect until two men or one upright man whom the slave knows inform him according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he becomes restricted even if the informant is free or slave, upright or sinful, or a boy. And in al-Khaniyya, Imam Abu Hanifa differentiated between permission (idhn) and restriction (hajr). In his view, restriction is not established by the report of a single person unless he is upright, or two persons, while permission is established by the report of a single unauthorized person in all cases. And Shaykh Khahirzada mentioned from al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi that there is no difference between them; rather, he becomes authorized (ma'dhun) only if the news is true according to the slave, and similarly for restriction. And the fatwa is on this statement.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses how a slave becomes restricted (mahjur) when the master is absent. The principle is that a message (risala) or writing (kitaba) from an absent master is equivalent to direct speech (mushafaha) from a present one. Therefore, if a messenger conveys the master's intention to restrict the slave, it is effective regardless of the messenger's moral character or status. However, the passage then discusses a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn) regarding the establishment of hijr through a reporter. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person (not a messenger) simply informs the slave of the master's restriction, it takes effect only if two men or one upright man known to the slave report it. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, hijr is established even by a single informant, regardless of his qualities. The passage notes that Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between idhn (granting permission to trade) and hajr (restriction): idhn is established by a single unauthorized person's report, while hajr requires more. Khahirzada then reports from Abu Bakr al-Balkhi that there is no difference; what matters is that the information is truthful from the slave's perspective. The fatwa is according to this latter view.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhūn): A slave whom his master has granted permission to engage in trade.
الحجر (al-ḥajr): Restriction placed on a person's legal capacity; here, the master revokes the slave's permission to trade.
الرسالة (ar-risālah): A message sent by the master via a third party conveying his decision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, how many witnesses are required to establish hajr on an authorized slave when the informant is not a messenger?
A) One witness, regardless of character
B) Two witnesses or one upright witness known to the slave
C) Four witnesses
D) Only the slave's own acknowledgment

Key Takeaway
The effectiveness of hijr depends on the method of notification: messages are always effective; for direct informants, the fatwa considers the truthfulness from the slave's perspective.

Automatic Hajr upon Master's Death, Continuous Insanity, and Apostasy
Commentary expanding on three causes of automatic hijr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three causes that automatically revoke a slave's permission and explain the intra-Hanafi differences regarding apostasy.

Core Idea
The authorized slave is automatically restricted when the master loses capacity: death, continuous insanity, or apostasy with joining dar al-harb.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that the master's permission (idhn) is automatically revoked when the master's legal capacity ends—whether by death, continuous insanity, or apostasy. This is called 'hajr hukmi' (legal restriction) and takes effect without the slave's knowledge. The commentary also highlights a key difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn on when apostasy triggers restriction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes of automatic hijr
From the passage, list the three events that automatically place the authorized slave under restriction: death of the master, continuous insanity of the master, and apostasy of the master combined with joining dar al-harb.
The text states that this occurs whether the slave knows or not.
2
Understand the legal basis for automatic hijr
These events void the master's capacity (ahliyyah) to give permission, so the permission is automatically revoked. This is termed 'hajr hukmi' (legal restriction) as opposed to a verbal pronouncement.
The permission (idhn) is not binding; it is contingent on the master's capacity.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference on apostasy
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, hijr only occurs if the apostate master also joins dar al-harb. According to the two Sahibayn, apostasy alone suffices. The relied-upon position is the latter. Also, if the apostate is killed, Imam voids transactions made during apostasy; Sahibayn validate them.
This difference is noted in al-Muhit and resolved by later authorities favoring Sahibayn.
4
Distinguish continuous from intermittent insanity
Only continuous insanity (mutbiq) that lasts a full year removes guardianship. Intermittent insanity (janna wa afaqa) leaves the permission intact.
The text cites al-Muhit for this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله تعالى: (وبموت سيده وجنونه ولخوقه بدار الحرب مرتداً) يعني يصير محجوراً عليه بهذه الأشياء علم العبد أو لم يعلم لأن الإذن غير لازم، وبما ذكر بطلت أهلية الآذن فينعزل وينحجر عليه لأن حجر حكمي ولهذا يعتق بما ذكر مدبروه وأمهات أولاده ويقسم ماله بين ورثته فصار محجوراً في ضمن بطلان الأهلية فلا يشترط علمه ولا علم أهل السوق أيضاً. قيد بلحوقه بدار الحرب والظاهر أنه قول الإمام وعندهما ينحجر بنفس الارتداد لحق أو لا. قال في المحيط وإن ارتد فتصرف ثم أسلم جاز تصرفه فإن قتل على ردته بطل عند الإمام. وقالا لا يبطل ولو كاتب أمة جاز بالإجماع. وأفاد بتوسط الجنون بين الموت الحقيقي والحكمي أنه الجنون المطبق قال في المحيط : فإن كان يجن ويفيق فهو على إذنه لأن ولايته لا تزول بغير المطبق الذي يستوعب السنة.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And by the death of his master, his insanity, and his joining the abode of war as an apostate) meaning he becomes restricted by these things whether the slave knows or not, because the permission is not binding, and by what was mentioned the capacity of the permitter is voided, so he is removed and becomes restricted because it is a legal restriction. For this reason, his mudabbars and umm walad are freed and his wealth is divided among his heirs, so he becomes restricted within the nullification of capacity; thus his knowledge nor the knowledge of the market people is required. He restricted with 'joining the abode of war' and apparently it is the statement of Imam. According to the two, he becomes restricted by the apostasy itself, whether he joins or not. He said in al-Muhit: If he apostatizes and then transacts, then becomes Muslim, his transaction is valid; if he is killed upon his apostasy, it becomes void according to Imam. The two said it does not become void. If he wrote a kitaba [contract] for a female slave, it is valid by consensus. And he indicated by placing insanity between real and legal death that it is continuous (mutbiq) insanity. He said in al-Muhit: If he becomes insane intermittently, he remains on his permission because his guardianship does not cease except by continuous insanity that encompasses a year.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage addresses scenarios where the authorized slave becomes restricted automatically due to events affecting the master's legal capacity. The three causes mentioned are: death of the master, continuous insanity (mutbiq), and apostasy combined with joining the abode of war (dar al-harb). In all these cases, the permission (idhn) is automatically revoked because the master's capacity to give permission is lost. This is a 'hukmi' (legal) restriction that does not require the slave's knowledge or the knowledge of the market. The commentator notes that 'joining dar al-harb' is a condition according to Imam Abu Hanifa; the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that apostasy alone suffices. A dispute also exists regarding transactions during apostasy: if the apostate later becomes Muslim, the transaction stands; if killed upon apostasy, Imam says it becomes void, while the two say it remains valid. Regarding insanity, the relevant type is continuous insanity that lasts a full year; intermittent insanity does not revoke permission. The rationale is that the master's guardianship (wilaya) only ends with continuous insanity.

Key Terms
المطبق (al-muṭbiq): Continuous insanity that lasts for a full year or more, with no lucid intervals.
الحجر الحكمي (al-ḥajr al-ḥukmī): Legal restriction that takes effect automatically without a direct act of the master, due to loss of capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of insanity causes an authorized slave to become restricted automatically?
A) Any temporary insanity
B) Continuous insanity lasting a full year (mutbiq)
C) Insanity that prevents understanding only
D) Insanity certified by two witnesses

Key Takeaway
The authorized slave is automatically restricted when the master's capacity ends by death, continuous insanity, or apostasy (with the mufta bihi that apostasy alone suffices).

Effect of Sale of Authorized Slave on Permission
Ruling on whether authorization ends when the slave is sold or transferred

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether authorization returns when the slave returns to the master's ownership, and explain the key difference from agency using the purpose (maqsud) of each.

Core Idea
Authorization removes restriction; once removed, it cannot return—unlike agency which is tied to the object.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a critical question: when an authorized slave is sold and then returns to the master, does his authorization revive? The Hanafi answer is no, and the reasoning involves a subtle distinction between idhn and wakalah that hinges on the nature of each institution. Understanding this helps clarify the structure of legal capacity and agency in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for sale without debt
When the master sells a debt-free authorized slave, the slave becomes restricted (mahjur) automatically, even if not known in the market.
2
Note the exception for debt
If the slave has debts, selling without creditor consent does not end the authorization—the restriction is not triggered.
This protects creditors' rights.
3
Compare return of ownership: agency vs authorization
If ownership returns to the master (via defect return or gift revocation), authorization does not return, but agency (wakalah) would revive.
4
Understand the illa (effective cause)
Authorization is essentially removal of restriction (fakk al-hajr). Once removed, it cannot automatically return. Agency is a contract to sell a specific object; its purpose is tied to the object, so it can revive with ownership.
This is a clear distinction based on the maqsud (purpose).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا باع المولى العبد صار محجورا عليه وإن لم يعلم أهل سوقه. هذا إذا لم يكن عليه دين، فإن كان عليه دين فباعه بغير إذن الغرماء لا يصير محجوراً عليه وهذا الحجر ثبت ضمناً للبيع، وكذا لو زال عن ملكه بالهبة أو غيرها فإن عاد إلى قديم ملكه بالرد بالعيب أو بالرجوع في الهبة لا يعود الإذن بخلاف الوكيل إذا باع الموكل فيه ثم عاد إلى ملكه تعود الوكالة. والفرق أو المقصود من الإذن فك الحجر والحجر يسقط والساقط لا يعود. والمقصود الوكالة بيع العين فجاز أن تعود الوكالة كما عاد إليه ملكه.
And if the master sells the slave, the slave becomes restricted even if the people of his market do not know. This is when there is no debt upon him. If there is debt upon him and he sells him without the permission of the creditors, he does not become restricted. This restriction is established implicitly through the sale. Similarly, if he leaves his ownership by gift or other means, then if it returns to his former ownership by return due to defect or by revocation of the gift, the permission does not return, unlike the agent: if he sells the subject matter then it returns to his ownership, the agency returns. The difference is that the purpose of permission is to remove restriction, and restriction is removed, and what is removed does not return. The purpose of agency is to sell the object, so it is permissible for the agency to return just as ownership returned.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of transferring ownership of an authorized slave on his authorization. The general rule: if the master sells the slave, the slave becomes restricted automatically (even without market knowledge) unless the slave had debts. If the slave has outstanding debts incurred during his authorized trade, the creditors have rights against the slave's estate. Therefore, sale of a debt-laden authorized slave without creditor consent does not terminate the authorization (to protect creditors). The restriction here is 'dhimni' (implicit) as a consequence of the sale. If the ownership returns to the master (e.g., the buyer returns the slave due to a defect, or a gift is revoked), the authorization does not return. This differs from agency (wakalah): if an agent sells the item and then it returns to the principal's ownership, the agency is revived. The reason: authorization is essentially a removal of restriction (fakk al-hajr); once removed, it cannot return. Agency, however, is a contract to sell a specific object; when the object returns, the agency can be revived. This distinction highlights the nature of idhn as a suspension of hajr, not a contract like wakalah.

Key Terms
فك الحجر (fakk al-ḥajr): Removal of the restriction that prevents the slave from trading freely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master sells his authorized slave who has no debts, and later the buyer returns the slave due to a defect, does the authorization revive?
A) Yes, because the slave is back in the master's ownership
B) No, because the restriction was removed and cannot return
C) Yes, but only if the master explicitly re-authorizes
D) No, but the slave can request new authorization

Key Takeaway
Authorization (idhn) is the removal of a restriction; once the restriction is lifted by a transfer of ownership, it cannot return automatically—unlike agency, which is tied to the object and can revive.
